The Four Modalities in Categorical Propositions
The gloss defines the four modalities of propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four modal classifications (jihat) in categorical propositions and explain the distinction between necessity, absoluteness, existentiality, and possibility.

Core Idea
Modality tells us how the predicate relates to the subject: necessarily, absolutely, existentially, or possibly.

In Islamic logic, every categorical proposition can be qualified by a modality (jiha) that indicates the mode of truth. The classical tradition recognizes four primary modalities: necessity, absoluteness, existentiality, and possibility. This lesson introduces these four types and their significance in constructing precise arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of modality
Modality (jiha) refers to the manner in which the predicate holds of the subject. For example, does it hold necessarily, or only sometimes?
2
Identify the four modalities
The source lists: al-darura (necessity), al-mutlaqa (absolute), al-wujudiya (existential), al-imkan (possibility). These are the standard four.
Some texts add a fifth, but the classical position is four.
3
Define each modality
Necessity: predicate holds always and necessarily. Absolute: predicate holds but not always (e.g., some individuals). Existential: predicate holds as long as the subject exists. Possibility: predicate may or may not hold; it is not necessary.
The absolute is sometimes called 'general absolute'.
4
Apply the classification to a known proposition
Take 'Every human is mortal'. This is not necessary (since immortality is possible but not actual), but it is existential: mortality holds as long as humans exist. In kalam, 'The world is contingent' is a necessary proposition (contingency cannot be otherwise).
Examples from Islamic theology help solidify the concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجهة اربع الضرورة والمطلقة والوجودية والامكان. المشروطة الخاصة هي التي حكم فيها ب [؟] بالازدواج [؟] . المطلقة هي التي حكم فيها بالطلاق لا دائما. الوجودية كقولنا كل [؟] الوجودية اعلان امة [؟] . الامكان وهي التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النسبة.

The possibility is that in which judgment is made by non-necessity of the relation.

Commentary
This passage from the gloss (hashiya) of al-Dasuqi defines the four modalities (jihat) of categorical propositions. The gloss lists them as: al-darura (necessity), al-mutlaqa (absolute), al-wujudiya (existential), and al-imkan (possibility). It then attempts to define each briefly. The 'special conditioned' proposition is defined with an unclear term; the absolute is that which is not always true; the existential is illustrated by an example that is garbled; the possibility is defined as the negation of necessity. Due to the severe OCR corruption, the exact definitions and examples are not fully recoverable. The passage is likely a note on the standard classification of modal propositions found in logic texts.

The Particular Conditional Proposition (al-Musharata al-Khassa)
Defines the particular conditional proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a particular conditional proposition and distinguish it from other conditional propositions.

Core Idea
The particular conditional ties a judgment to a specific condition, like perpetuity.

In this lesson, we examine the particular conditional proposition, which is a key building block for understanding modal logic. The passage defines it briefly, and we will unpack that definition to see how it differs from other conditionals. This concept is foundational for analysing conditional statements in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Locate the Arabic term المشروطة الخاصة in the passage and read its definition: 'هي التي حكم فيها بقيد بالدوام' meaning it is a judgment with a condition of perpetuity.
The term 'particular conditional' is contrasted with other types of conditionals not mentioned here.
2
Extract the condition (qayd)
The defining feature is the condition (قيد) of perpetuity (دوام). This means the judgment holds as long as a specific circumstance continues, e.g., 'Whenever Zaid writes, he moves his hand.'
Perpetuity here means continuous connection, not eternal duration.
3
Distinguish from general conditionals
Unlike the general conditional (المشروطة العامة), which may be unconditional or have a different type of condition, the particular conditional ties the judgment to a specific subsidiary condition.
In logic, conditionals are divided into general and particular; this distinction is crucial for syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشروطة الخاصة وهي التي حكم فيها بقيد بالدوام؟ ... والجهة أربع: الضرورة والمطلقة والإمكان والوجودية.
The particular conditional is that in which judgment is made with a condition of perpetuity... The modalities are four: necessity, absoluteness, possibility, and existence.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage defines the 'particular conditional' proposition (al-musharata al-khassa) and then lists the four modalities (al-jiha). The particular conditional is contrasted with other types of conditionals. The four modalities are: الضرورة (necessity) – where the judgment is necessary continuously; المطلقة (absolute) – where the judgment is unrestricted; الإمكان (possibility) – where the judgment is possible; الوجودية (existence) – where the judgment is about actual existence. These modalities are crucial in distinguishing different types of judgments in logic, especially in the context of syllogisms. For example, in fiqh, a ruling may be necessary (wajib) or merely permissible (mubah), paralleling the logical modalities.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة
قيد
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is unconditional.
B) It has a condition of perpetuity.
C) It is always true.
D) It is false.

Key Takeaway
The particular conditional is a proposition that includes a specific condition, such as perpetuity, distinguishing it from general conditionals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Modalities in Logic (al-Jiha al-Arba')
Lists the four modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and define the four logical modalities: necessity, absoluteness, possibility, and existence.

Core Idea
Modalities qualify how a judgment holds: necessary, absolute, possible, or existential.

This lesson breaks down the four modalities (al-jiha) that are standard in post-Avicennan logic. The passage lists them: الضرورة (necessity), المطلقة (absoluteness), الإمكان (possibility), and الوجودية (existence). Understanding these modalities is essential for modal syllogisms and for categorizing judgments in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
List the four modalities
Read the passage and write down the four terms: الضرورة, المطلقة, الإمكان, الوجودية. Memorize them in order.
Some later sources add a fifth modality, but the four here are the classical set.
2
Define each modality
Using the commentary, define each: Necessity (the judgment holds at all times), Absoluteness (unrestricted), Possibility (may or may not hold), Existence (the judgment is about actual occurrence).
In fiqh, 'wajib' corresponds to necessity, 'mubah' to possibility/absoluteness, etc.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Match each modality to a legal category: Wajib (necessity), Mandub/Mubah (absoluteness/possibility), and Wujud (existence of a specific act).
For example, 'Prayer is obligatory (wajib)' has the modality of necessity.

Source Text

These modalities are crucial in distinguishing different types of judgments in logic, especially in the context of syllogisms. For example, in fiqh, a ruling may be necessary (wajib) or merely permissible (mubah), paralleling the logical modalities.

Definition and Types of Proposition
Definition of proposition, its four types, three parts, and matter/modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given proposition into one of four types (personal, universal quantified, particular quantified, unquantified) and identify its three components (subject, predicate, relation) and its modality.

Core Idea
The proposition is the basic unit of truth; its type depends on quantification and its structure on three parts.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of the proposition (qadiyya) as defined by the author of the Tadhheeb. In Islamic logic, the proposition is what can be true in itself. We will break down its four types based on quantification and the three essential parts: subject, predicate, and the relation between them. Understanding these basics is crucial for all subsequent logical analysis in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Learn the definition of proposition (qadiyya)
The passage defines proposition as 'what can be true in itself.' This definition highlights that truth is a property of the proposition as a whole, considered by itself regardless of external factors.
This definition sets the scope for all subsequent logical analysis.
2
Memorize the four types
There are four types based on quantification: Personal (subject is particular, e.g., 'Zayd is writing'), Universal quantified (universal quantifier, e.g., 'Every human is an animal'), Particular quantified (particular quantifier, e.g., 'Some human is an animal'), and Unquantified (no quantifier, e.g., 'Human is an animal').
Personal propositions are a subtype of particular, but here they are listed separately.
3
Practice classifying examples
Take each example from the passage and identify its type: 'Zayd is writing' is personal; 'Every human is an animal' is universal quantified; 'Some human is an animal' is particular quantified; 'Human is an animal' is unquantified.
The quantifier words (كل, بعض) are key indicators.
4
Identify the three parts of any proposition
Every proposition has a subject (what is judged about), a predicate (what is judged about the subject), and a relation (affirmation or negation) connecting them. In 'Zayd is writing', subject=Zayd, predicate=writing, relation=affirmative.
The relation is sometimes called nisba and is the core of the judgment.
5
Understand matter (madda) and modality (jiha)
The relation has a quality (kayfiyya) called matter, e.g., possibility, necessity, impossibility. The word expressing that quality is the modality. For example, in 'Zayd is possibly writing', 'possibly' indicates the modality.
Matter relates to the inherent modal status of the relation, not its truth or falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ
قَوْلُهُ : بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيم الكلامُ عليها قَدْ أُفرِدَ بالتأليف، ولكن لا بأس بِالتَّعرُّض لشيءٍ مِمَّا ذكروهُ مِمَّا يتعلق بها مِنَ الفنّ المشروع فيه، وبيانه يحتاج لتقديم مُقدِّمةٍ مِنَ الفنّ، وَهِيَ أنَّ القضيَّة مَا احتملَ الصَّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ، وهي أقسام أربعة :
١ . شَخصيَّةُ : إِنْ كانَ مَوضوعُهَا جُزئيًا نحو : زيد كاتب.
٢. وَمُسوَّرةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ : إِنْ قُرِنَت بِسورٍ كُلِّي نحو : كُلُّ إنسان حيوان. وَمُسوَّرةٌ جُزئيَّةٌ : إِنْ قُرِنَتْ بِسورٍ جُزئيّ نحو : بعضُ الإنسان حيوان. ٤ . وَمُهمَلَةٌ : إن لم تقترن بذلك ؛ أي: بِسورٍ نحو: الإنسان حيوان. وللقضيَّة أجزاء ثلاثة :
۱. محكوم عليهِ كَـ (زيد) في المثالِ الأَوَّلِ، ويُسمَّى : موضوعاً . ٢ ومحكومٌ بِهِ كَـ (كاتب) في المثالِ المذكور، ويُسمَّى محمولاً .
۳. ونسبةٌ كَثبوتِ الكتابةِ لزيدٍ في المثال المذكور.
وَلَا بُدَّ لِلنِّسبةِ في نَفْسِ الأمرِ مِن كيفيَّةٍ، وتُسمَّى : مادَّةٌ كَالإمكان في المثالِ المذكور ، واللَّفْظُ الدَّالُ عليها يُسمَّى : جهة .
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. His saying: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The discussion on it has been singled out in authorship, but there is no harm in addressing some of what they mentioned concerning it from the art that is being embarked upon. Its clarification requires presenting a preface from the art, which is that the proposition is what can be true in itself. And it has four divisions:
1. Personal: if its subject is particular, like 'Zayd is writing.'
2. And quantified universal: if it is associated with a universal quantifier, like 'Every human is an animal.'
3. And quantified particular: if it is associated with a particular quantifier, like 'Some human is an animal.'
4. And unquantified: if it is not associated with that, i.e., with a quantifier, like 'Human is an animal.'
And the proposition has three parts:
1. That which is judged about, like 'Zayd' in the first example, and it is called subject.
2. And that which is judged with, like 'writing' in the mentioned example, and it is called predicate.
3. And a relation, like the affirmation of writing to Zayd in the mentioned example.
And the relation must have a quality in itself, called matter, like possibility in the mentioned example; and the word indicating it is called modality.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is from the sharh (commentary) defining the proposition (qadiyya). It begins with the interpreter's explanation of the author's basmalah, then provides a preliminary definition of proposition as what can be true in itself. The author then divides propositions into four types: personal (shakhsiyya) with a particular subject, universal quantified (musawwara kulliyya) with a universal quantifier, particular quantified (musawwara juz'iyya) with a particular quantifier, and unquantified (muhmala) without a quantifier. Examples are given using the Islamic example 'Zayd is writing' (personal) and 'Every human is an animal' (universal) etc. Then it lists the three parts of a proposition: subject (mahkum 'alayhi), predicate (mahkum bihi), and relation (nisba). Finally, it mentions that the relation requires a quality (kayfiyya) called matter (madda), e.g., possibility (imkan), and the word indicating it is called modality (jiha). This is foundational for understanding categorical propositions.

Key Takeaway

The Four Types of Propositions in Mantiq
Al-Dasuqi defines proposition and classifies it into four types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given Arabic proposition as personal, universal quantified, particular quantified, or indefinite based on its subject and quantifier.

Core Idea
A proposition's type is determined by its subject's scope and the presence of a quantifier.

Al-Dasuqi opens his commentary on the Basmala by providing a foundational preliminary: the definition of a proposition and its four types. This classification is essential for understanding syllogistic reasoning, as the quantity of a proposition dictates its logical behavior in inference. Let's learn to identify each type with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Find the subject of the proposition – the thing being described. Is it a specific individual (like 'Zayd') or a general term (like 'every human' or just 'human')?
2
Check for quantifier
If the subject is a general term, look for a quantifier word (sawr) like 'kull' (all/every) or 'ba'd' (some). If no quantifier is present, the proposition is indefinite (muhmala).
3
Classify the type
Apply the rules: personal (shakhsiyya) if the subject is a particular individual; universal quantified (musawwara kulliyya) if accompanied by 'kull'; particular quantified (musawwara juz'iyya) if accompanied by 'ba'd'; indefinite (muhmala) if no quantifier and the subject is a general term.
The indefinite proposition is a distinct type in this classification, though some logicians treat it as equivalent to particular by default.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الشارح الدسوقي [مُقَدِّمَةُ الشَّارِحِ] بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ (قَوْلُهُ : بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيم الكلامُ عليها قَدْ أُفرِدَ بالتأليف، ولكن لا بأس بِالتَّعرُّض لشيءٍ مِمَّا ذكروهُ مِمَّا يتعلق بها مِنَ الفنّ المشروع فيه، وبيانه يحتاج لتقديم مُقدِّمةٍ مِنَ الفنّ، وَهِيَ أنَّ القضيَّة مَا احتملَ الصَّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ، وهي أقسام أربعة : : ١ . شَخصيَّةُ : إِنْ كانَ مَوضوعُهَا جُزئيًا نحو : زيد كاتب. .٣ ۲. وَمُسوَّرةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ : إِنْ قُرِنَت بِسورٍ كُلِّي نحو : كُلُّ إنسان حيوان. وَمُسوَّرةٌ جُزئيَّةٌ : إِنْ قُرِنَتْ بِسورٍ جُزئيّ نحو : بعضُ الإنسان حيوان. ٤ . وَمُهمَلَةٌ : إن لم تقترن بذلك ؛ أي: بِسورٍ نحو: الإنسان حيوان.
Introduction of the commentator al-Dasuqi. In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. (His saying: In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Discourse on it has been composed separately, but there is no harm in addressing something of what they mentioned regarding it from the art under consideration. Its explanation requires presenting a preliminary from the art, which is that the proposition (qadiyya) is what admits truth in itself, and it has four divisions: (1) Personal: if its subject is particular, e.g., Zayd is a writer. (2) Universal quantified: if accompanied by a universal quantifier, e.g., Every human is an animal. (3) Particular quantified: if accompanied by a particular quantifier, e.g., Some human is an animal. (4) Indefinite: if not accompanied by that, i.e., a quantifier, e.g., Human is an animal.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a preliminary from al-Dasuqi defining the proposition and its four types. He begins by noting that the basmala has its own dedicated literature but he will touch on its relevance to logic. Then he states that to explain it, a preliminary from the art of logic is needed: the definition of a proposition (qadiyya) as what admits truth in itself. Then he lists its four divisions: (1) Personal (shakhsiyya) when the subject is a particular, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer'; (2) Universal quantified (musawwara kulliyya) when accompanied by a universal quantifier, e.g., 'Every human is an animal'; (3) Particular quantified (musawwara juz'iyya) with a particular quantifier, e.g., 'Some human is an animal'; (4) Indefinite (muhmala) without any quantifier, e.g., 'Human is an animal'. He emphasizes that the quantifier (sawr) is what distinguishes these types. This classification is foundational for understanding syllogistic reasoning, as the quantity of propositions determines their behavior in inference.

شخصية
مسورة كلية
مسورة جزئية
مهملة

Key Takeaway
The four types of propositions—personal, universal quantified, particular quantified, and indefinite—are distinguished by the subject's particularity and the presence of a quantifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Parts of a Proposition and the Matter-Modality Distinction
Al-Dasuqi explains the three parts of a proposition, matter, and modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, relation, matter, and modality in any categorical proposition.

Core Idea
Every proposition has three parts; the relation's intrinsic quality is its matter, expressed as modality.

Al-Dasuqi begins by dissecting the proposition into its fundamental components. Understanding these elements is essential for analyzing any logical statement in Islamic sciences, from fiqh rulings to kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three parts
In a proposition like 'Zayd is a writer', identify the subject (Zayd), predicate (writer), and the relation (the copula connecting them).
2
Distinguish the relation from its quality
The relation exists as a connection; it has an intrinsic quality (e.g., possibility, necessity) called the matter. For example, 'Zayd is a writer' has the matter of possibility (since writing is not essential to Zayd).
3
Define matter and modality
Matter (madda) is the real quality of the relation; modality (jiha) is the word that indicates it, such as 'necessarily' or 'possibly'.
4
Recognize a modal proposition
A proposition that explicitly mentions the modality is called modal (muwajjaha). For instance, 'Zayd is necessarily a writer' includes the modality of necessity.

وللقضيَّة أجزاء ثلاثة : ۱. محكوم عليهِ كَـ (زيد) في المثالِ الأَوَّلِ، ويُسمَّى : موضوعاً . .٢ ومحكومٌ بِهِ كَـ (كاتب) في المثالِ المذكور، ويُسمَّى محمولاً . .٣ ونسبةٌ كَثبوتِ الكتابةِ لزيدٍ في المثال المذكور. وَلَا بُدَّ لِلنِّسبةِ في نَفْسِ الأمرِ مِن كيفيَّةٍ، وتُسمَّى : مادَّةٌ كَالإمكان في المثالِ المذكور ، واللَّفْظُ الدَّالُ عليها يُسمَّى : جهة . وتُسمّى القضيَّةُ : مُوجّهةً عند ذكر الجهة، كما لَوْ قُلْتَ في المثال المذكور : زيدٌ كاتب بالإمكان العام أو الخاص والجهات أربع : الضرورة، والإمكان والدوام، والإطلاق. - والقضايا الموجّهة التي جرت العادة بالبحث عنها خمسة عشر . ويرجعُ حاصلها إلى أقسام أربعة: الضَّروريَّاتُ السَّبْعُ، وَهِيَ : الضَّروريّةُ المطلقةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بضرورة نسبة المحمول للموضوع ما دامَتْ ذات الموضوع نحو : كلَّ إنسانٍ حيوان بالضَّرورة. - والمشروطة العامَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورة النسبة ما دام وصف الموضوع، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرّكُ الأصابع بالضَّرورةِ ما دام كاتباً . - والمشروطة الخاصَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقُيِّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : بِالضَّرورةِ كلُّ كاتب مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع كاتباً لا دائماً . والوقتيَّةُ المطلقة : وَهِي التي مُحكِمَ فيها بضرورة النِّسبة في وقتٍ مُعيَّنٍ كَقولنا : كُلُّ قمر مُنخسفٌ بِالضَّرورة وقتَ حيلولةِ الأرضِ بينَهُ وبينَ الشَّمْسِ . والوقتيَّةُ: وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ في وقتٍ مُعيَّنٍ، وقُيَّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي كَقولنا : كُلُّ قمرٍ مُنخسفٌ بِالضَّرورة وقتَ حَيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشَّمْس لا دائماً .
And the proposition has three parts: (1) That which is judged upon, like 'Zayd' in the first example, and it is called the subject. (2) And that which is judged with, like 'writer' in the mentioned example, and it is called the predicate. (3) And a relation, like the affirmation of writing to Zayd in the mentioned example. And the relation in itself must have a quality, and it is called the matter, like possibility in the mentioned example. And the word indicating it is called the modality. And the proposition is called modal when the modality is mentioned, as if you say in the mentioned example: Zayd is a writer with general or specific possibility. And the modalities are four: necessity, possibility, perpetuity, and absoluteness. And the modal propositions customarily studied are fifteen. Their outcome reduces to four divisions: the seven necessary [propositions], which are: the absolute necessary: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate for the subject as long as the subject's essence remains, e.g., Every human is an animal by necessity. And the general conditional: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the relation as long as the description of the subject remains, like our saying: Every writer is necessarily moving fingers as long as he is a writer. And the special conditional: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the relation as long as the description of the subject remains, and it is qualified by non-self-perpetuity, like our saying: Every writer is necessarily moving fingers as a writer, not always. And the absolute temporal: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the relation at a specific time, like our saying: Every moon is necessarily eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun. And the temporal: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the relation at a specific time, and it is qualified by non-self-perpetuity, like our saying: Every moon is necessarily eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
In this passage, al-Dasuqi continues the preliminary by detailing the internal structure of a proposition. He states that every proposition has three parts: (1) that which is judged upon (al-mahkum 'alayh) called the subject (mawdu'), e.g., 'Zayd' in the first example; (2) that which is judged (al-mahkum bihi) called the predicate (mahmul), e.g., 'writer'; and (3) the relation (nisba) which is the connection between them, e.g., the affirmation of writing to Zayd. Furthermore, the relation in itself requires a quality (kayfiyya) called the matter (madda), such as possibility in the example, and the word indicating it is called the modality (jiha). A proposition mentioning the modality is called a modal proposition (muwajjaha). The modalities (jihat) are four: necessity (darura), possibility (imkan), perpetuity (dawam), and absoluteness (itlaq). He then notes that the modal propositions customarily studied are fifteen, but they reduce to four divisions, one of which is the seven necessary propositions (dharuriyyat al-sab'). He lists five of them: (1) Absolute necessary (dharuriyya mutlaqa): judgment of necessity as long as the subject's essence remains, e.g., 'Every human is necessarily an animal.' (2) General conditional (mushtarata 'amma): necessity as long as the description of the subject remains, e.g., 'Every writer is necessarily moving fingers while a writer.' (3) Special conditional (mushtarata khassa): same as above but qualified by non-perpetuity (la dawam), i.e., not eternally, e.g., 'Every writer is necessarily moving fingers while a writer, not always.' (4) Absolute temporal (waqtiyya mutlaqa): necessity at a specific time, e.g., 'Every moon is necessarily eclipsed when the earth interposes between it and the sun.' (5) Temporal (waqtiyya): necessity at a specific time qualified by non-perpetuity, e.g., same example with 'not always'. The remaining two are not listed here but are part of the seven.

Key Terms
موضوع
محمول
نسبة
مادة
جهة
موجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proposition 'Every human is necessarily an animal', what is the modality?
A) Necessity
B) Possibility
C) Perpetuity
D) Absoluteness

Key Takeaway
A proposition has three essential parts; modality expresses the quality of the relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Prayer is obligatory necessarily.' Identify the subject, predicate, relation, matter, and modality.

Propositions
intermediate
5 min
The Seven Necessary Modal Propositions
Al-Dasuqi lists the seven necessary modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five types of necessary modal propositions mentioned by al-Dasuqi.

Core Idea
The seven necessary propositions classify necessity according to duration and condition.

Al-Dasuqi then presents the modal propositions customarily studied, focusing on the seven necessary ones. He lists five of them, each specifying the condition under which the necessity holds, from perpetual to time-bound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of necessity
Each necessary proposition has the modality of necessity (ضرورة) but with different conditions regarding duration and subjects.
2
Distinguish absolute necessary
The absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة) holds as long as the subject's essence exists. Example: 'Every human is an animal by necessity' (as long as the human exists).
3
Distinguish general conditional
The general conditional (مشروطة عامة) holds as long as a specific description of the subject exists. Example: 'Every writer moves fingers necessarily while a writer.'
4
Distinguish special conditional
The special conditional (مشروطة خاصة) is similar but explicitly states that the necessity is not perpetual. Example: 'Every writer moves fingers necessarily as a writer, not always.'
5
Distinguish temporal propositions
The absolute temporal (وقتية مطلقة) is necessary at a specific time. Example: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily when earth interposes.' The temporal (وقتية) adds the qualification of non-perpetuity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Propositions
beginner
5 min
The Four Modalities: Necessity, Possibility, Perpetuity, Absoluteness
Author introduces the concept of modal propositions and lists the four modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four modalities in any Arabic proposition and classify a given proposition as modal or non-modal.

Core Idea
A proposition's modality tells you the mode of truth, not just its content.

In classical Islamic logic, every proposition can be examined for its modality—the way in which the predicate relates to the subject. This lesson introduces the four modalities that form the basis for the fifteen modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality word
Look for keywords like 'بالضرورة' (necessarily), 'دائماً' (perpetually), 'بالإمكان' (possibly), 'بالإطلاق' (absolutely). These words signal that the proposition is modal.
2
Determine which of the four modalities it signals
Match the word to the corresponding modality: necessity, possibility, perpetuity, or absoluteness. The passage states these are the four modalities.
3
Check if the proposition explicitly mentions the modality
If the proposition includes a modality word explicitly, it is modal (muwajjaha). If not, it is non-modal (mutlaqa or muhmala).
4
Practice on sample sentences
Use the examples from the passage: e.g., 'زيد كاتب بالإمكان العام' contains the modality 'بالإمكان', so it is modal. 'كل إنسان حيوان' without a modality word is non-modal unless context adds one.

وتُسمّى القضيَّةُ : مُوجّهةً عند ذكر الجهة، كما لَوْ قُلْتَ في المثال المذكور : زيدٌ كاتب بالإمكان العام أو الخاص والجهات أربع : الضرورة، والإمكان والدوام، والإطلاق. - والقضايا الموجّهة التي جرت العادة بالبحث عنها خمسة عشر . ويرجعُ حاصلها إلى أقسام أربعة: الضَّروريَّاتُ السَّبْعُ، وَهِيَ : الضَّروريّةُ المطلقةُ : وَهِى الَّتي حُكم فيها بضرورة نسبة المحمول للموضوع ما دامَتْ ذات الموضوع نحو : كلَّ إنسانٍ حيوان بالضَّرورة. - والمشروطة العامَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورة النسبة ما دام وصف الموضوع، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرّكُ الأصابع بالضَّرورةِ ما دام كاتباً . - والمشروطة الخاصَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقُيِّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : بِالضَّرورةِ كلُّ كاتب مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع كاتباً لا دائماً . والوقتيَّةُ المطلقة : وَهِي الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورة النِّسبة في وقتٍ مُعيَّنٍ كَقولنا : كُلُّ قمر مُنخسفٌ بِالضَّرورة وقتَ حيلولةِ الأرضِ بينَهُ وبينَ الشَّمْسِ . والوقتيَّةُ: وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ في وقتٍ مُعيَّنٍ، وقُيَّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي كَقولنا : كُلُّ قمرٍ مُنخسفٌ بِالضَّرورة وقتَ حَيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشَّمْس لا دائماً . والمنتشرة المطلقةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكمَ فيها بضرورة النسبة في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالضرورة وقتاً ما . والمنتشرة : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ، وَقُيدَتْ باللادوامِ الذَّاتي كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالضَّرورة وقتاً ما لا دائماً . والدوائمُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ الدَّائمةُ المطلقةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فيها بِدوامِ النِّسبةِ مَا دامَ ذات الموضوع ، كَقولنا : كُلَّ إنسان حيوان دائماً . - والعُرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ : وَهِي الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوامِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع دائماً ما دامَ كاتباً . - والعُرفيَّةُ الخاصة : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوام النّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقيدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتب مُتحرّك الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً. والمطلقاتُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ المطلقةُ العامَّةُ: وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالإطلاقِ العام. - والوجوديَّةُ اللأضروريَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ باللاضرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس بالإطلاقِ العامّ لَا بِالضَّرورة. - والوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذاتي كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالإطلاقِ العام لا دائماً .
The proposition is called modal when the modality is mentioned, as if you said in the mentioned example: Zayd is a writer with general or specific possibility. The modalities are four: necessity, possibility, perpetuity, and absoluteness. The modal propositions that are customarily examined are fifteen. Their total returns to four divisions: The seven necessary: they are: the absolute necessary: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate for the subject as long as the subject's essence exists, like: every human is an animal necessarily. - The general conditional: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate as long as the subject's description exists, like our saying: every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer. - The specific conditional: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate as long as the subject's description exists, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: necessarily every writer moves his fingers while he is a writer, not perpetually. The absolute temporal: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate at a specific time, like our saying: every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun. The temporal: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate at a specific time, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not perpetually. The absolute spread: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate at an unspecified time, like our saying: every human breathes necessarily at some time. The spread: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate at an unspecified time, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: every human breathes necessarily at some time, not perpetually. The three perpetual: they are: the absolute perpetual: it is that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate as long as the subject's essence exists, like our saying: every human is an animal perpetually. - The general conventional: it is that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate as long as the subject's description exists, like our saying: every writer moves his fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer. - The specific conventional: it is that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate as long as the subject's description exists, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not perpetually. The three absolute: they are: the general absolute: it is that in which judgment is made of the actuality of the predicate, like our saying: every human breathes with general absoluteness. - The non-necessary existential: it is that in which judgment is made of the actuality of the predicate, and it is restricted by essential non-necessity, like our saying: every human breathes with general absoluteness, not necessarily. - The non-perpetual existential: it is that in which judgment is made of the actuality of the predicate, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: every human breathes with general absoluteness, not perpetually.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha). It begins by stating that a proposition is called modal when its modality (al-jiha) is explicitly mentioned. Four modalities are listed: necessity (al-darura), possibility (al-imkan), perpetuity (al-dawam), and absoluteness (al-itlaq). The author then states that the modal propositions customarily studied are fifteen in number, which reduce to four divisions. He then enumerates three groups: seven necessary propositions, three perpetual propositions, and three absolute propositions, totaling thirteen. The missing two may be the possibility propositions or a miscalculation, but the text proceeds with definitions and examples for each listed type. The seven necessary are: (1) absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa): e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily' – the predicate holds as long as the subject's essence exists. (2) general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma): e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' – the predicate holds as long as a description (being a writer) exists. (3) specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa): same as general conditional but restricted by essential non-perpetuity (la dawam dhati), meaning the predicate does not hold perpetually even when the description applies. (4) absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa): e.g., 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition' – the predicate holds with necessity at a specific time. (5) temporal (al-waqtiyya): same but restricted by essential non-perpetuity. (6) absolute spread (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa): e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time' – necessity at an unspecified time. (7) spread (al-muntashira): same but restricted by non-perpetuity. Next are the three perpetual: (8) absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa): e.g., 'Every human is an animal perpetually' – the predicate holds perpetually as long as the subject's essence exists. (9) general conventional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma): e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer' – perpetuity tied to a description. (10) specific conventional (al-'urfiyya al-khassa): same but restricted by essential non-perpetuity. Finally, the three absolute: (11) general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma): e.g., 'Every human breathes with general absoluteness' – mere actuality without modality. (12) non-necessary existential (al-wujjudiyya al-la-daruriyya): actuality restricted by non-necessity. (13) non-perpetual existential (al-wujjudiyya al-la-da'ima): actuality restricted by non-perpetuity. The examples drawn from everyday observations help clarify each modality. Note that the text says there are fifteen types but only thirteen are listed; the remaining two are likely the unqualified possibility propositions, which are often included in modal logic but omitted here for brevity.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
The Seven Necessary Propositions
Author lists the seven necessary propositions with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the seven types of necessary modal propositions and distinguish them based on condition and time.

Core Idea
Necessity comes in seven flavours: absolute, conditional, temporal, spread—each with a restriction of non-perpetuity.

The most detailed group in modal logic is the seven necessary propositions. They all assert necessity, but differ in whether the necessity holds as long as the subject's essence exists, or as long as a description exists, and whether it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity. This lesson walks through each type with examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base type: absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة)
This is the simplest: the predicate is necessary as long as the subject's essence exists. Example: 'كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة' (Every human is an animal necessarily). No condition on description.
2
Check for a condition tied to a description
If the necessity depends on a description (e.g., being a writer), it is conditional. General conditional (المشروطة العامة) ties necessity to the description. Specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة) adds the restriction that the predicate does not hold perpetually even when the description applies.
The key difference: general conditional lacks the non-perpetuity restriction; specific conditional includes it.
3
Check for a time specification
If the necessity is tied to a specific time (e.g., moon eclipse), it is temporal (الوقتية). If the time is unspecified (e.g., breathing at some time), it is spread (المنتشرة). Both have absolute and restricted versions with non-perpetuity.
Absolute temporal (الوقتية المطلقة) has no non-perpetuity restriction; temporal (الوقتية) does.
4
Determine if the non-perpetuity restriction is present
The restriction 'لا دائماً' (not perpetually) distinguishes the absolute from the restricted versions in conditional, temporal, and spread types. If present, the proposition is the 'specific' or 'restricted' variant.
This restriction is called 'اللادوام الذاتي' (essential non-perpetuity).
5
Apply to new examples
Given a new example, classify it among the seven: e.g., 'كل نار حارة بالضرورة' is absolute necessary because heat is essential to fire. 'كل طائر يطير بالضرورة ما دام طائراً' is general conditional.

والمشروطة العامَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورة النسبة ما دام وصف الموضوع، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرّكُ الأصابع بالضَّرورةِ ما دام كاتباً . - والمشروطة الخاصَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقُيِّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : بِالضَّرورةِ كلُّ كاتب مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع كاتباً لا دائماً . والوقتيَّةُ المطلقة : وَهِي الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورة النِّسبة في وقتٍ مُعيَّنٍ كَقولنا : كُلُّ قمر مُنخسفٌ بِالضَّرورة وقتَ حيلولةِ الأرضِ بينَهُ وبينَ الشَّمْسِ . والوقتيَّةُ: وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ في وقتٍ مُعيَّنٍ، وقُيَّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي كَقولنا : كُلُّ قمرٍ مُنخسفٌ بِالضَّرورة وقتَ حَيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشَّمْس لا دائماً . والمنتشرة المطلقةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكمَ فيها بضرورة النسبة في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالضرورة وقتاً ما . والمنتشرة : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ، وَقُيدَتْ باللادوامِ الذَّاتي كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالضَّرورة وقتاً ما لا دائماً . والدوائمُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ الدَّائمةُ المطلقةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فيها بِدوامِ النِّسبةِ مَا دامَ ذات الموضوع ، كَقولنا : كُلَّ إنسان حيوان دائماً . - والعُرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ : وَهِي الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوامِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع دائماً ما دامَ كاتباً . - والعُرفيَّةُ الخاصة : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوام النّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقيدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتب مُتحرّك الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً. والمطلقاتُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ المطلقةُ العامَّةُ: وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالإطلاقِ العام. - Propositions

The Perpetual and Absolute Propositions
Author lists the three perpetual propositions and three absolute propositions with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish perpetual propositions from absolute propositions and identify their subtypes.

Core Idea
Perpetuity asserts 'always' as long as a condition holds; absoluteness asserts mere actuality without modality.

The final two groups in the classification are the three perpetual propositions and the three absolute propositions. Perpetual propositions assert that the predicate holds continuously, while absolute propositions assert only that the predicate actually holds at some time without necessity or perpetuity. This lesson clarifies the distinction and subtypes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the difference: perpetuity vs. absoluteness
Perpetuity (الدوام) means the predicate holds at all times when the condition applies. Absoluteness (الإطلاق) means the predicate merely obtains in actuality (الفعلية), without any modal force.
2
Examine the perpetual types
There are three: (1) absolute perpetual (الدائمة المطلقة) – holds as long as the subject's essence exists, like 'كل إنسان حيوان دائماً'. (2) general conventional (العرفية العامة) – holds as long as a description applies, like 'كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً ما دام كاتباً'. (3) specific conventional (العرفية الخاصة) – same but restricted by essential non-perpetuity (لا دائماً).
The specific conventional says the predicate holds during the description but not perpetually (i.e., not when the description is absent, but also not at all times even when present? Actually it's restricted to the duration of the description only, not beyond.
3
Examine the absolute types
There are three: (1) general absolute (المطلقة العامة) – mere actuality without any modality, e.g., 'كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام'. (2) non-necessary existential (الوجودية اللاضرورية) – actuality but not necessary, e.g., 'كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام لا بالضرورة'. (3) non-perpetual existential (الوجودية اللادائمة) – actuality but not perpetual, e.g., 'كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام لا دائماً'.
These absolute propositions are the weakest modal type; they only affirm that the predicate holds in fact.
4
Distinguish using the restriction phrase
Look for 'لا دائماً' (not perpetually) in perpetual types, and 'لا بالضرورة' (not necessarily) or 'لا دائماً' in absolute types. These restrictions differentiate the subtypes.
5
Apply to new examples
Given a proposition like 'كل فقير محتاج دائماً ما دام فقيراً' (Every poor person is needy perpetually as long as he is poor), classify it as general conventional perpetual.

وتُسمّى القضيَّةُ : مُوجّهةً عند ذكر الجهة، كما لَوْ قُلْتَ في المثال المذكور : زيدٌ كاتب بالإمكان العام أو الخاص والجهات أربع : الضرورة، والإمكان والدوام، والإطلاق. - والقضايا الموجّهة التي جرت العادة بالبحث عنها خمسة عشر . ويرجعُ حاصلها إلى أقسام أربعة: الضَّروريَّاتُ السَّبْعُ، وَهِيَ : الضَّروريّةُ المطلقةُ : وَهِى الَّتي حُكم فيها بضرورة نسبة المحمول للموضوع ما دامَتْ ذات الموضوع نحو : كلَّ إنسانٍ حيوان بالضَّرورة. - والعُرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ : وَهِي الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوامِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع دائماً ما دامَ كاتباً . - والعُرفيَّةُ الخاصة : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوام النّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقيدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتب مُتحرّك الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً. والمطلقاتُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ المطلقةُ العامَّةُ: وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالإطلاقِ العام. - والوجوديَّةُ اللأضروريَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ باللاضرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس بالإطلاقِ العامّ لَا بِالضَّرورة. - والوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذاتي كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالإطلاقِ العام لا دائماً .
The proposition is called modal when the modality is mentioned, as if you said in the mentioned example: Zayd is a writer with general or specific possibility. The modalities are four: necessity, possibility, perpetuity, and absoluteness. The modal propositions that are customarily examined are fifteen. Their total returns to four divisions: The seven necessary: they are: the absolute necessary: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate for the subject as long as the subject's essence exists, like: every human is an animal necessarily. - The general conditional: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate as long as the subject's description exists, like our saying: every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer. - The specific conditional: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate as long as the subject's description exists, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: necessarily every writer moves his fingers while he is a writer, not perpetually. The absolute temporal: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate at a specific time, like our saying: every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun. The temporal: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate at a specific time, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not perpetually. The absolute spread: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate at an unspecified time, like our saying: every human breathes necessarily at some time. The spread: it is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate at an unspecified time, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: every human breathes necessarily at some time, not perpetually. The three perpetual: they are: the absolute perpetual: it is that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate as long as the subject's essence exists, like our saying: every human is an animal perpetually. - The general conventional: it is that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate as long as the subject's description exists, like our saying: every writer moves his fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer. - The specific conventional: it is that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate as long as the subject's description exists, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not perpetually. The three absolute: they are: the general absolute: it is that in which judgment is made of the actuality of the predicate, like our saying: every human breathes with general absoluteness. - The non-necessary existential: it is that in which judgment is made of the actuality of the predicate, and it is restricted by essential non-necessity, like our saying: every human breathes with general absoluteness, not necessarily. - The non-perpetual existential: it is that in which judgment is made of the actuality of the predicate, and it is restricted by essential non-perpetuity, like our saying: every human breathes with general absoluteness, not perpetually.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha). It begins by stating that a proposition is called modal when its modality (al-jiha) is explicitly mentioned. Four modalities are listed: necessity (al-darura), possibility (al-imkan), perpetuity (al-dawam), and absoluteness (al-itlaq). The author then states that the modal propositions customarily studied are fifteen in number, which reduce to four divisions. He then enumerates three groups: seven necessary propositions, three perpetual propositions, and three absolute propositions, totaling thirteen. The missing two may be the possibility propositions or a miscalculation, but the text proceeds with definitions and examples for each listed type. The seven necessary are: (1) absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa): e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily' – the predicate holds as long as the subject's essence exists. (2) general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma): e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' – the predicate holds as long as a description (being a writer) exists. (3) specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa): same as general conditional but restricted by essential non-perpetuity (la dawam dhati), meaning the predicate does not hold perpetually even when the description applies. (4) absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa): e.g., 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition' – the predicate holds with necessity at a specific time. (5) temporal (al-waqtiyya): same but restricted by essential non-perpetuity. (6) absolute spread (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa): e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time' – necessity at an unspecified time. (7) spread (al-muntashira): same but restricted by non-perpetuity. Next are the three perpetual: (8) absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa): e.g., 'Every human is an animal perpetually' – the predicate holds perpetually as long as the subject's essence exists. (9) general conventional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma): e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers perpetually as long as he is a writer' – perpetuity tied to a description. (10) specific conventional (al-'urfiyya al-khassa): same but restricted by essential non-perpetuity. Finally, the three absolute: (11) general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma): e.g., 'Every human breathes with general absoluteness' – mere actuality without modality. (12) non-necessary existential (al-wujjudiyya al-la-daruriyya): actuality restricted by non-necessity. (13) non-perpetual existential (al-wujjudiyya al-la-da'ima): actuality restricted by non-perpetuity. The examples drawn from everyday observations help clarify each modality. Note that the text says there are fifteen types but only thirteen are listed; the remaining two are likely the unqualified possibility propositions, which are often included in modal logic but omitted here for brevity.

Perpetual propositions assert continuous holding under a condition; absolute propositions assert mere actuality with optional restrictions of non-necessity or non-perpetuity.

Propositions
intermediate
8 min
The Absolute Distributed and Distributed
Defines the first two modal types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and differentiate between the absolute distributed and distributed modal propositions using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Distributed propositions capture necessary relations at an unspecified time, with or without permanence.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions specify how a predicate relates to its subject. The first type, the distributed propositions, deal with necessity at an unspecified time. The author defines two variants: the absolute distributed and the distributed, the latter adding a condition of non-permanence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core feature
Notice that both propositions involve necessity, but the time is unspecified ('at some time'). This is the defining characteristic of distributive necessity.
2
Compare the two definitions
The absolute distributed simply asserts necessity at some time. The distributed adds the restriction 'not always' (la da'im), making it more specific.
The Arabic labels are 'al-muntashira al-mutlaqa' and 'al-muntashira' (without 'mutlaqa').
3
Analyze the examples
For the absolute distributed: 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time.' For the distributed: 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time, not always.' The second denies permanence.
These examples use human breathing, which is necessary at some times but not at all times (e.g., when sleeping?).

المنتشرة المطلقةُ وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكمَ فيها بضرورة النسبة في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ - : كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالضرورة وقتاً ما . والمنتشرة : وَهِيَ وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ، وَقُيدَتْ باللادوامِ الذَّاتي كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالضَّرورة وقتاً ما لا دائماً . والدوائمُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ الدَّائمةُ المطلقةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فيها بِدوامِ النِّسبةِ مَا دامَ ذات الموضوع ، كَقولنا : كُلَّ إنسان حيوان دائماً . - والعُرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ : وَهِي الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوامِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع دائماً ما دامَ كاتباً . - والعُرفيَّةُ الخاصة : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوام النّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقيدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتب مُتحرّك الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً. والمطلقاتُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ المطلقةُ العامَّةُ: وَهِيَ المطلقة العامَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالإطلاقِ العام. - والوجوديَّةُ اللأضروريَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ باللاضرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس بالإطلاقِ العامّ لَا بِالضَّرورة. - والوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذاتي كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالإطلاقِ العام لا دائماً . والممكنتانِ، وَهُمَا : الممكنة العامَّةُ : وَهِي التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النّسبةِ (١) ، كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ نار حارَّةٌ بالإمكان العام . والممكنه الخاصَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النسبة، وبعدم ضرورة خلافِهَا كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص . فهذهِ جملةُ القضايا المذكورة، واللا ضرورة فيها : إشارة إلى مُمكنةٍ عامة؛ مُخالفة للقضيَّةِ الَّتِي فُيِّدَتْ بها في الكيف، موافقة لها في الكم، واللادوامُ فيها إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة؛ مُخالفة للقضيَّةِ الَّتي قُيّدَتْ بها في الكيف، موافقة لها في الكم. وكُلُ قِسمِ مِنَ الأقسام الأربعةِ المذكورة أعمُّ مِمَّا قبلَهُ، فتكونُ الممكنتانِ أعم القضايا، وتكونُ الضَّروريّاتُ أخصَّها ، ويكونُ كُلَّ مِنَ الدَّوائم والمطلقاتِ أعمَّ مِنَ الَّذي قبلَهُ، وأخصَّ مِنَ الَّذي بعده، وأعمُ الضَّروريَّاتِ ؛ المنتشرة المطلقة، وأعمُ الدَّوائم؛ العرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ، وأخصُّ المطلقات الوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ، وأختُ الممكنتين؛ الممكنة الخاصة.
(١) كذا بالنسخ التي بين أيدينا، ولعلَّ الصَّواب وهي التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة خلاف النسبة، وتعرف الممكنة الخاصَّة بأنها التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة الجانب الموافق أيضاً، كما يأتي في الموجهات . ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي .
The absolute distributed: it is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged for an unspecified time, e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time.' And the distributed: it is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged for an unspecified time, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time, not always.' And the three permanents: they are the absolute permanent: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the essence of the subject exists, e.g., 'Every human is an animal always.' And the general conventional: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers always as long as he is a writer.' And the specific conventional: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always.' And the three absolutes: they are the general absolute: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense.' And the existential non-necessary: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, and it is qualified by non-necessity of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense, not necessarily.' And the existential non-permanent: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense, not always.' And the two possibles: they are: the general possible: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the relation (1), e.g., 'Every fire is hot with general possibility.' And the specific possible: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the relation and non-necessity of its opposite, e.g., 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility.' So this is the totality of the mentioned propositions; and the non-necessity therein is an indication of the general possible, differing from the proposition qualified by it in quality, agreeing in quantity; and the non-permanence therein is an indication of the general absolute, differing from the proposition qualified by it in quality, agreeing in quantity. And each section of the four mentioned sections is more general than that which precedes it, so the two possibles are the most general of propositions, and the necessities are the most specific, and each of the permanents and absolutes is more general than that which precedes it and more specific than that which follows it; and the most general of the necessities is the absolute distributed, and the most general of the permanents is the general conventional, and the most specific of the absolutes is the existential non-permanent, and the most specific of the two possibles is the specific possible.
(1) It is thus in the copies at hand, and perhaps the correct (reading) is: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the opposite of the relation, and the specific possible is known as that which is judged with non-necessity of the agreeing side as well, as will come in the modal propositions. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the various types of modal propositions in Aristotelian logic as adopted by the Islamic logical tradition. The author, a commentator, systematically defines each type with examples. He begins with the 'absolute distributed' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa), which affirms necessity at an unspecified time, like 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time.' He then defines the 'distributed' (al-muntashira) which adds the condition of non-permanence (so the breathing is necessary at some time but not always). Next are the three 'permanent' (duwa'im) propositions: the 'absolute permanent' affirms permanence as long as the subject's essence exists (e.g., 'Every human is an animal always'); the 'general conventional' affirms permanence as long as the subject's description exists (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer'); and the 'specific conventional' adds the condition of non-permanence of the essence (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not always'). Following are the three 'absolute' (mutlaqat) propositions: the 'general absolute' affirms mere actuality without modality (e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense'); the 'existential non-necessary' adds the condition of non-necessity (e.g., 'Every human breathes, not necessarily'); and the 'existential non-permanent' adds the condition of non-permanence (e.g., 'Every human breathes, not always'). Finally, the two 'possible' (mumkinatan) propositions: the 'general possible' affirms non-necessity (e.g., 'Every fire is hot with general possibility'), and the 'specific possible' affirms both non-necessity of the relation and non-necessity of its opposite (e.g., 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility'). A marginal note by al-Sharnubi corrects the definition of the general possible: it should be 'non-necessity of the opposite' rather than 'non-necessity of the relation'. After the definitions, the author explains that the terms 'non-necessity' and 'non-permanence' in quantified propositions refer to the general possible and general absolute, respectively, differing in quality but agreeing in quantity. He then presents a hierarchy: the two possibles are the most general, the necessities are the most specific, and the permanents and absolutes are intermediate. Within the necessities, the 'absolute distributed' is the most general; within the permanents, the 'general conventional'; within the absolutes, the 'existential non-permanent' is the most specific; and within the possibles, the 'specific possible' is the most specific.

The absolute distributed and distributed modal propositions capture necessary relations with specific temporal conditions.

The Three Permanents: Essence, Description, and Duration
Defines the three permanent types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of permanent modal propositions based on the condition of the subject's essence or description.

Core Idea
Permanent propositions affirm a relation holds as long as the subject's essence or a description of it exists.

The second category of modal propositions, the 'permanents' (duwa'im), concerns relations that endure continuously. The author defines three types: absolute permanent, general conventional, and specific conventional, each with a different condition for duration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common element
All three permanents use 'da'im' (always) and specify that the relation holds as long as something remains: the essence (dhat) or a description (wasf).
'Da'im' means permanent or always.
2
Compare the conditions
Absolute permanent: as long as the essence exists (e.g., 'Every human is an animal always'). General conventional: as long as the description exists (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers always as long as he is a writer'). Specific conventional: same as general but with added non-permanence of the essence (i.e., it is not always true by essence).
The specific conventional is a restricted version of the general conventional.
3
Analyze the examples
Human = animal holds as long as the human exists. Writer moving fingers holds only while being a writer. The specific conventional allows that when the description ends, the relation may cease, and additionally it is not essential.
These examples illustrate the difference between essential and accidental permanence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنتشرة المطلقةُ وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكمَ فيها بضرورة النسبة في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ - : كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالضرورة وقتاً ما . والمنتشرة : وَهِيَ وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ، وَقُيدَتْ باللادوامِ الذَّاتي كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالضَّرورة وقتاً ما لا دائماً . والدوائمُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ الدَّائمةُ المطلقةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فيها بِدوامِ النِّسبةِ مَا دامَ ذات الموضوع ، كَقولنا : كُلَّ إنسان حيوان دائماً . - والعُرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ : وَهِي الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوامِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع دائماً ما دامَ كاتباً . - والعُرفيَّةُ الخاصة : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوام النّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقيدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتب مُتحرّك الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً. والمطلقاتُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ المطلقةُ العامَّةُ: وَهِيَ المطلقة العامَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالإطلاقِ العام. - والوجوديَّةُ اللأضروريَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ باللاضرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس بالإطلاقِ العامّ لَا بِالضَّرورة. - والوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذاتي كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالإطلاقِ العام لا دائماً . والممكنتانِ، وَهُمَا : الممكنة العامَّةُ : وَهِي التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النّسبةِ (١) ، كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ نار حارَّةٌ بالإمكان العام . والممكنه الخاصَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النسبة، وبعدم ضرورة خلافِهَا كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص . فهذهِ جملةُ القضايا المذكورة، واللا ضرورة فيها : إشارة إلى مُمكنةٍ عامة؛ مُخالفة للقضيَّةِ الَّتِي فُيِّدَتْ بها في الكيف، موافقة لها في الكم، واللادوامُ فيها إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة؛ مُخالفة للقضيَّةِ الَّتي قُيّدَتْ بها في الكيف، موافقة لها في الكم. وكُلُ قِسمِ مِنَ الأقسام الأربعةِ المذكورة أعمُّ مِمَّا قبلَهُ، فتكونُ الممكنتانِ أعم القضايا، وتكونُ الضَّروريّاتُ أخصَّها ، ويكونُ كُلَّ مِنَ الدَّوائم والمطلقاتِ أعمَّ مِنَ الَّذي قبلَهُ، وأخصَّ مِنَ الَّذي بعده، وأعمُ الضَّروريَّاتِ ؛ المنتشرة المطلقة، وأعمُ الدَّوائم؛ العرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ، وأخصُّ المطلقات الوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ، وأختُ الممكنتين؛ الممكنة الخاصة.
(١) كذا بالنسخ التي بين أيدينا، ولعلَّ الصَّواب وهي التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة خلاف النسبة، وتعرف الممكنة الخاصَّة بأنها التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة الجانب الموافق أيضاً، كما يأتي في الموجهات . ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي .
The absolute distributed: it is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged for an unspecified time, e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time.' And the distributed: it is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged for an unspecified time, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time, not always.' And the three permanents: they are the absolute permanent: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the essence of the subject exists, e.g., 'Every human is an animal always.' And the general conventional: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers always as long as he is a writer.' And the specific conventional: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always.' And the three absolutes: they are the general absolute: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense.' And the existential non-necessary: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, and it is qualified by non-necessity of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense, not necessarily.' And the existential non-permanent: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense, not always.' And the two possibles: they are: the general possible: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the relation (1), e.g., 'Every fire is hot with general possibility.' And the specific possible: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the relation and non-necessity of its opposite, e.g., 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility.' So this is the totality of the mentioned propositions; and the non-necessity therein is an indication of the general possible, differing from the proposition qualified by it in quality, agreeing in quantity; and the non-permanence therein is an indication of the general absolute, differing from the proposition qualified by it in quality, agreeing in quantity. And each section of the four mentioned sections is more general than that which precedes it, so the two possibles are the most general of propositions, and the necessities are the most specific, and each of the permanents and absolutes is more general than that which precedes it and more specific than that which follows it; and the most general of the necessities is the absolute distributed, and the most general of the permanents is the general conventional, and the most specific of the absolutes is the existential non-permanent, and the most specific of the two possibles is the specific possible.
(1) It is thus in the copies at hand, and perhaps the correct (reading) is: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the opposite of the relation, and the specific possible is known as that which is judged with non-necessity of the agreeing side as well, as will come in the modal propositions. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the various types of modal propositions in Aristotelian logic as adopted by the Islamic logical tradition. The author, a commentator, systematically defines each type with examples. He begins with the 'absolute distributed' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa), which affirms necessity at an unspecified time, like 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time.' He then defines the 'distributed' (al-muntashira) which adds the condition of non-permanence (so the breathing is necessary at some time but not always). Next are the three 'permanent' (duwa'im) propositions: the 'absolute permanent' affirms permanence as long as the subject's essence exists (e.g., 'Every human is an animal always'); the 'general conventional' affirms permanence as long as the subject's description exists (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer'); and the 'specific conventional' adds the condition of non-permanence of the essence (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not always'). Following are the three 'absolute' (mutlaqat) propositions: the 'general absolute' affirms mere actuality without modality (e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense'); the 'existential non-necessary' adds the condition of non-necessity (e.g., 'Every human breathes, not necessarily'); and the 'existential non-permanent' adds the condition of non-permanence (e.g., 'Every human breathes, not always'). Finally, the two 'possible' (mumkinatan) propositions: the 'general possible' affirms non-necessity (e.g., 'Every fire is hot with general possibility'), and the 'specific possible' affirms both non-necessity of the relation and non-necessity of its opposite (e.g., 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility'). A marginal note by al-Sharnubi corrects the definition of the general possible: it should be 'non-necessity of the opposite' rather than 'non-necessity of the relation'. After the definitions, the author explains that the terms 'non-necessity' and 'non-permanence' in quantified propositions refer to the general possible and general absolute, respectively, differing in quality but agreeing in quantity. He then presents a hierarchy: the two possibles are the most general, the necessities are the most specific, and the permanents and absolutes are intermediate. Within the necessities, the 'absolute distributed' is the most general; within the permanents, the 'general conventional'; within the absolutes, the 'existential non-permanent' is the most specific; and within the possibles, the 'specific possible' is the most specific.

Key Terms

العرفية العامة
العرفية الخاصة

Key Takeaway
Permanent propositions vary by whether the relation endures with the essence, a description, or a description without essential permanence.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
The Three Absolutes: Actuality Without Modality
Defines the three absolute types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and differentiate the three absolute modal propositions using their temporal and necessity conditions.

Core Idea
Absolute propositions affirm actuality of the relation without necessity, possibly with restrictions of non-necessity or non-permanence.

The third category, 'absolute' (mutlaqat) propositions, expresses actuality (wujūd) of the relation without a modal modifier. The author defines three types: general absolute, existential non-necessary, and existential non-permanent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core feature
All three absolutes involve 'actual occurrence' (fu'liyya al-nisba), not necessity or permanence. The relation actually holds at some time(s).
The term 'mutlaqa' here means 'absolute' in the sense of lacking modal qualification.
2
Examine the restrictions
General absolute: no restriction (e.g., 'Every human breathes' without modality). Existential non-necessary: adds that the actuality is not necessary (e.g., '... not necessarily'). Existential non-permanent: adds that it is not permanent (e.g., '... not always').
These restrictions make the propositions more specific.
3
Contrast with distributed and permanent
Unlike distributed (necessity) and permanent (always), absolutes only assert that the relation actually holds, not how or for how long.
