12 min
The Two Possibles and the Generality Hierarchy
Defines the two possibles and describes the hierarchy of all modal types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general and specific possible propositions and arrange all modal types in their order of generality.

Core Idea
Possible propositions are the most general; necessities are the most specific; permanents and absolutes lie in between.

The final modal category is the 'possible' (mumkin). The author defines two types: general and specific. Then he explains the hierarchy of all modal propositions, showing that the possibles are the most general and the necessities are the most specific.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of general possible
The general possible (al-mumkina al-'amma) is defined as 'non-necessity of the relation.' However, a footnote by al-Sharnubi corrects this to 'non-necessity of the opposite.' Examine both definitions.
Al-Sharnubi's correction: it should be 'non-necessity of the opposite' (i.e., the relation could be false).
2
Define the specific possible
The specific possible (al-mumkina al-khassa) adds 'non-necessity of the opposite' as well, meaning both the relation and its opposite are not necessary. That is true possibility (contingency).
Example: 'Every human is a writer' is possible but not necessary; its opposite is also possible.
3
Analyze the hierarchy
The author states: possibles are most general, then permanents/absolutes, then necessities. Within each group, some types are more general/specific. Memorize the order: possibilities > general absolute? Wait, read carefully: 'Each section of the four mentioned sections is more general than that which precedes it.' So order: necessities (most specific) < permanents < absolutes < possibles (most general).
Check the text: 'the two possibles are the most general of propositions, and the necessities are the most specific'.
4
Identify the extremes within each section
Within necessities: absolute distributed is most general. Within permanents: general conventional. Within absolutes: existential non-permanent is most specific. Within possibles: specific possible is most specific.
This helps in understanding logical strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنتشرة المطلقةُ وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكمَ فيها بضرورة النسبة في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ - : كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالضرورة وقتاً ما . والمنتشرة : وَهِيَ وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ في وقتٍ غيرِ مُعيَّنٍ، وَقُيدَتْ باللادوامِ الذَّاتي كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالضَّرورة وقتاً ما لا دائماً . والدوائمُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ الدَّائمةُ المطلقةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فيها بِدوامِ النِّسبةِ مَا دامَ ذات الموضوع ، كَقولنا : كُلَّ إنسان حيوان دائماً . - والعُرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ : وَهِي الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوامِ النِّسبة ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع دائماً ما دامَ كاتباً . - والعُرفيَّةُ الخاصة : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بدوام النّسبة ما دامَ وصف الموضوع، وقيدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذَّاتي، كَقولنا : كُلُّ كاتب مُتحرّك الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً. والمطلقاتُ الثَّلاثُ : وَهِيَ المطلقةُ العامَّةُ: وَهِيَ المطلقة العامَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفس بالإطلاقِ العام. - والوجوديَّةُ اللأضروريَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ باللاضرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ كَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّس بالإطلاقِ العامّ لَا بِالضَّرورة. - والوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حكم فيها بِفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، وَقُيِّدَتْ بِاللادوامِ الذاتي كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بِالإطلاقِ العام لا دائماً . والممكنتانِ، وَهُمَا : الممكنة العامَّةُ : وَهِي التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النّسبةِ (١) ، كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ نار حارَّةٌ بالإمكان العام . والممكنه الخاصَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النسبة، وبعدم ضرورة خلافِهَا كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص . فهذهِ جملةُ القضايا المذكورة، واللا ضرورة فيها : إشارة إلى مُمكنةٍ عامة؛ مُخالفة للقضيَّةِ الَّتِي فُيِّدَتْ بها في الكيف، موافقة لها في الكم، واللادوامُ فيها إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة؛ مُخالفة للقضيَّةِ الَّتي قُيّدَتْ بها في الكيف، موافقة لها في الكم. وكُلُ قِسمِ مِنَ الأقسام الأربعةِ المذكورة أعمُّ مِمَّا قبلَهُ، فتكونُ الممكنتانِ أعم القضايا، وتكونُ الضَّروريّاتُ أخصَّها ، ويكونُ كُلَّ مِنَ الدَّوائم والمطلقاتِ أعمَّ مِنَ الَّذي قبلَهُ، وأخصَّ مِنَ الَّذي بعده، وأعمُ الضَّروريَّاتِ ؛ المنتشرة المطلقة، وأعمُ الدَّوائم؛ العرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ، وأخصُّ المطلقات الوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ، وأختُ الممكنتين؛ الممكنة الخاصة.
(١) كذا بالنسخ التي بين أيدينا، ولعلَّ الصَّواب وهي التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة خلاف النسبة، وتعرف الممكنة الخاصَّة بأنها التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة الجانب الموافق أيضاً، كما يأتي في الموجهات . ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي .
The absolute distributed: it is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged for an unspecified time, e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time.' And the distributed: it is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged for an unspecified time, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time, not always.' And the three permanents: they are the absolute permanent: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the essence of the subject exists, e.g., 'Every human is an animal always.' And the general conventional: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers always as long as he is a writer.' And the specific conventional: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always.' And the three absolutes: they are the general absolute: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense.' And the existential non-necessary: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, and it is qualified by non-necessity of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense, not necessarily.' And the existential non-permanent: it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged, and it is qualified by non-permanence of the essence, e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense, not always.' And the two possibles: they are: the general possible: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the relation (1), e.g., 'Every fire is hot with general possibility.' And the specific possible: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the relation and non-necessity of its opposite, e.g., 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility.' So this is the totality of the mentioned propositions; and the non-necessity therein is an indication of the general possible, differing from the proposition qualified by it in quality, agreeing in quantity; and the non-permanence therein is an indication of the general absolute, differing from the proposition qualified by it in quality, agreeing in quantity. And each section of the four mentioned sections is more general than that which precedes it, so the two possibles are the most general of propositions, and the necessities are the most specific, and each of the permanents and absolutes is more general than that which precedes it and more specific than that which follows it; and the most general of the necessities is the absolute distributed, and the most general of the permanents is the general conventional, and the most specific of the absolutes is the existential non-permanent, and the most specific of the two possibles is the specific possible.
(1) It is thus in the copies at hand, and perhaps the correct (reading) is: it is that which is judged with non-necessity of the opposite of the relation, and the specific possible is known as that which is judged with non-necessity of the agreeing side as well, as will come in the modal propositions. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the various types of modal propositions in Aristotelian logic as adopted by the Islamic logical tradition. The author, a commentator, systematically defines each type with examples. He begins with the 'absolute distributed' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa), which affirms necessity at an unspecified time, like 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time.' He then defines the 'distributed' (al-muntashira) which adds the condition of non-permanence (so the breathing is necessary at some time but not always). Next are the three 'permanent' (duwa'im) propositions: the 'absolute permanent' affirms permanence as long as the subject's essence exists (e.g., 'Every human is an animal always'); the 'general conventional' affirms permanence as long as the subject's description exists (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer'); and the 'specific conventional' adds the condition of non-permanence of the essence (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not always'). Following are the three 'absolute' (mutlaqat) propositions: the 'general absolute' affirms mere actuality without modality (e.g., 'Every human breathes in the general absolute sense'); the 'existential non-necessary' adds the condition of non-necessity (e.g., 'Every human breathes, not necessarily'); and the 'existential non-permanent' adds the condition of non-permanence (e.g., 'Every human breathes, not always'). Finally, the two 'possible' (mumkinatan) propositions: the 'general possible' affirms non-necessity (e.g., 'Every fire is hot with general possibility'), and the 'specific possible' affirms both non-necessity of the relation and non-necessity of its opposite (e.g., 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility'). A marginal note by al-Sharnubi corrects the definition of the general possible: it should be 'non-necessity of the opposite' rather than 'non-necessity of the relation'. After the definitions, the author explains that the terms 'non-necessity' and 'non-permanence' in quantified propositions refer to the general possible and general absolute, respectively, differing in quality but agreeing in quantity. He then presents a hierarchy: the two possibles are the most general, the necessities are the most specific, and the permanents and absolutes are intermediate. Within the necessities, the 'absolute distributed' is the most general; within the permanents, the 'general conventional'; within the absolutes, the 'existential non-permanent' is the most specific; and within the possibles, the 'specific possible' is the most specific.

Key Takeaway

Classifying the Basmala as a Logical Proposition
Author classifies the basmala as nominal or verbal and then as particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the basmala as a nominal or verbal proposition and determine when it qualifies as a particular (shakhsiyya) proposition.

Core Idea
The basmala's logical type hinges on syntax and reference, not just its popular form.

We have learned about propositions as the second part of tasdiq. Now we apply that framework to the basmala, the most recited phrase in Islam. This passage shows how a classical logician parses the basmala into nominal or verbal and then singles out the particular case. By mastering this parsing, you gain a model for analyzing any Islamic sentence as a proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible sentence types
The author says the basmala is either a nominal sentence (ismiyya) or a verbal sentence (fi'liyya). Recognize that the choice depends on the grammatical structure: if it begins with a noun or pronoun, it is nominal; if with a verb, verbal.
In Arabic, 'Bismillah' is typically considered nominal because it starts with a preposition and noun.
2
Focus on the nominal case and examine the subject
If the basmala is nominal, the subject (musnad ilayh) may be in a genitive construction (idafa) like 'bi-ismi' (with the name). Note that the subject is 'my beginning' (ibtida'i) in the example.
The author uses 'ibtida'i' as a placeholder for the act of beginning.
3
Check the reference of the genitive construction
If the genitive indicates 'present reference' (al-'ahd al-huduri), then the proposition is particular (shakhsiyya). This means the phrase refers to a specific instance: 'this specific beginning is with the name of Allah'.
Present reference ties the phrase to a concrete, contextually present individual.
4
Recall the definition of a particular proposition
The author reminds us that a particular proposition is one whose subject is a specific individual. Compare with universal propositions: here the subject is not a general kind but a particular act of beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عَلِمْتَ هذا؛ فاعلَمْ أنَّ جملة البسملة : إمَّا اسميَّةٌ، أو فعليَّةٌ . فالاسميَّةُ إنْ كانَ المسند إليه فيها مضافاً كابتدائي؛ فهي: شخصيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَتِ الإضافة للعهد الحضوري ؛ إذ المراد : هذا الابتداء المعين كائن بسم الله ... إلخ ، والشَّخصيَّةُ : - على مَا مَرَّ - مَا مَوضوعُهَا مُشخَصٌ مُعيَّنٌ .
If you know this, then know that the sentence of the basmala is either nominal or verbal. The nominal, if the subject in it is a genitive construction like 'my beginning', then it is particular: if the addition is for present reference, because what is meant is 'this specific beginning is with the name of Allah... etc.' And the particular—as has passed—is that whose subject is a specific individual.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the basmala (the phrase 'Bismillah') as a logical proposition. The author states that the basmala is either a nominal sentence (murakkab ismi) or a verbal sentence (fi'li). Focusing on the nominal case, if the subject (musnad ilayh) is a genitive construction (mudaf) like 'bi-ismi' (with the name), then the proposition is particular (shakhsiyya) if the addition is for present reference (al-'ahd al-huduri). That is, the intended meaning is 'this specific beginning is with the name of Allah'. The particular proposition is defined as one whose subject refers to a specific individual, as previously explained.

8 min
The Two Possible Propositions and Their Hierarchy
Defines two possible propositions and ranks modal divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define الممكنة العامة and الممكنة الخاصة and rank the four modal divisions from most general to most specific.

Core Idea
Possible propositions are the broadest; necessary are the narrowest.

In logic, propositions are classified by modality—whether the predicate holds necessarily, permanently, absolutely, or possibly. This passage defines two types of possible judgments and then arranges the four main divisions in a clear hierarchy. Mastering this structure is essential for evaluating the scope of any modal claim in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the two definitions: الممكنة العامة judges the relation as non-necessary; الممكنة الخاصة judges both the relation and its opposite as non-necessary. Note the Arabic phrasing 'حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النسبة' and 'بعدم ضرورة خلافها'.
The example for general possible: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility'; for specific possible: 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility'.
2
Extract the indicators
The passage explains that 'لا ضرورة' (non-necessary) in a proposition indicates a general possible, and 'لا دوام' (non-permanent) indicates a general absolute. These indicators differ in quality but agree in quantity.
Quality here refers to affirmation/negation; quantity to universality/particularity.
3
Establish the hierarchy
The four divisions—possible, absolute, permanent, necessary—are arranged from most general (possible) to most specific (necessary). Each division is more general than the one before it.
The passage states: 'وكل قسم من الأقسام الأربعة المذكورة أعم مما قبله'.
4
Identify the extreme types
Within each division, the passage names the most general subtype: for necessary it is المنتشرة المطلقة; for permanent it is العرفية العامة; for absolute it is الوجودية اللادائمة; and the sister of the two possible is الممكنة الخاصة.
These specific terms help further refine the hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والممكنتانِ، وَهُمَا : الممكنة العامَّةُ : وَهِي التي حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النّسبةِ، كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ نار حارَّةٌ بالإمكان العام . والممكنه الخاصَّةُ : وَهِيَ الَّتي حُكم فيها بعدم ضرورة النسبة، وبعدم ضرورة خلافِهَا كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص . فَهذهِ جملةُ القضايا المذكورة، واللا ضرورة فيها : إشارة إلى مُمكنةٍ عامة؛ مُخالفة للقضيَّةِ الَّتِي فُيِّدَتْ بها في الكيف، موافقة لها في الكم، واللادوامُ فيها إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة؛ مُخالفة للقضيَّةِ الَّتي قُيّدَتْ بها في الكيف، موافقة لها في الكم. وكُلُ قِسمِ مِنَ الأقسام الأربعةِ المذكورة أعمُّ مِمَّا قبلَهُ، فتكونُ الممكنتانِ أعم القضايا، وتكونُ الضَّروريّاتُ أخصَّها ، ويكونُ كُلَّ مِنَ الدَّوائم والمطلقاتِ أعمَّ مِنَ الَّذي قبلَهُ، وأخصَّ مِنَ الَّذي بعده، وأعمُ الضَّروريَّاتِ ؛ المنتشرة المطلقة، وأعمُ الدَّوائم؛ العرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ، وأخصُّ المطلقات الوجوديَّةُ اللادائمةُ، وأختُ الممكنتين؛ الممكنة الخاصة.
And the two possible [propositions]: they are: the general possible: which is the one judged with the non-necessity of the relation, like our saying: Every fire is hot with general possibility. And the specific possible: which is the one judged with the non-necessity of the relation and the non-necessity of its opposite, like our saying: Every human is a writer with specific possibility. So this is the summary of the mentioned propositions. And 'the non-necessary' in them is an indication of a general possible; differing from the proposition with which it was restricted in quality, agreeing with it in quantity. And 'the non-permanent' in them is an indication of a general absolute; differing from the proposition with which it was restricted in quality, agreeing with it in quantity. And every division of the mentioned four divisions is more general than the one before it. So the two possible propositions are the most general of propositions, and the necessary propositions are the most specific of them. And each of the permanent and absolute is more general than the one before it and more specific than the one after it. The most general of the necessary is the absolute expansive; the most general of the permanent is the general customary; the most specific of the absolute is the non-permanent existential; and the sister of the two possible is the specific possible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of possible propositions (الممكنة العامة والخاصة) and then establishes a hierarchy among the four divisions of modal propositions: necessary (ضرورية), permanent (دائمة), absolute (مطلقة), and possible (ممكنة). It begins by defining the general possible (الممكنة العامة) as a proposition that judges the relation to be non-necessary, i.e., it is possible that the predicate holds for the subject, but not necessarily. Example: 'Every fire is hot' – fire can be hot but not necessarily; it is possible. Then the specific possible (الممكنة الخاصة) is defined as a proposition that judges both the relation and its opposite to be non-necessary, i.e., the predicate could equally hold or not hold. Example: 'Every human is a writer' – it is not necessary nor impossible. Then the passage explains that the terms 'non-necessary' (لا ضرورة) and 'non-permanent' (لا دوام) in propositions signify the general possible and general absolute respectively, and they differ in quality but agree in quantity. Finally, it states that among the four divisions, each is more general than the one before it, with the possible being the most general and necessary the most specific. It further specifies the most general of each division: among necessary, the absolute expansive; among permanent, the general customary; among absolute, the non-permanent existential; and the sister of the two possible is the specific possible. This hierarchical understanding is crucial for evaluating the scope of modal propositions.

الممكنة الخاصة
الضروريات
الدوائم
المطلقات
المنتشرة المطلقة
العرفية العامة
الوجودية اللادائمة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of proposition is the most general?
A) الضروريات (Necessary)
B) الممكنتان (The two possible)
C) الدوائم (Permanent)
D) المطلقات (Absolute)

Key Takeaway
The possible propositions are the broadest modal category, and within the hierarchy, each division is more specific than the preceding one, with necessary being the most specific.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Classifying the Basmala into Four Proposition Types
Author analyzes the Basmala sentence as a logical proposition and divides it into four types based on the nature of its subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any nominal or verbal sentence from the Quran or Hadith into personal, universal, partial, or indefinite based on the quantifier indicated by idafa or 'alif lam'.

Core Idea
The Basmala can be read as four different logical propositions depending on its quantifier.

Before this, we learned that propositions are divided by quantity. Now, we apply that division to the very first verse of the Quran: Basmala. The author shows how the same sentence can be personal, universal, partial, or indefinite, depending on the meaning of the idafa or 'alif lam' in its subject. This is not just grammatical hair-splitting; it affects how we understand the scope of the statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify sentence type
First, determine whether the Basmala is treated as a nominal sentence (subject + predicate) or verbal sentence (verb + doer). The author covers both. For nominal, focus on the subject (musnad ilayh).
2
Examine the subject's construction
If the subject is an idafa (e.g., 'ibtida'i'), note the meaning of the idafa: is it for a specific individual (عهد حضوري), for comprehensiveness (استغراق), for genus within an ambiguous individual (جنس ضمن فرد مبهم), or merely for genus probabilistically?
The quantifier (سور) is often a word, but here it is the idafa itself.
3
Classify the nominal proposition
Based on the idafa meaning, classify the proposition: personal (if عهد حضوري), universal (if استغراق), partial (if genus within ambiguous individual), indefinite (if genus merely probabilistically).
The author references al-'Adawi's similar analysis of 'Al-hamdu lillah'.
4
Handle subjects with 'alif lam'
If the subject is defined with 'alif lam' (e.g., 'al-ibtida'), the same four types apply: personal if 'alif lam' is for covenant, universal if for comprehensiveness, partial if for genus within ambiguous individual, indefinite if for genus probabilistically.
This mirrors the idafa analysis.
5
Classify verbal sentences
For verbal sentences, look at the doer (fa'il). If it is a specific pronoun (e.g., 'abda''s implied 'I'), a proper noun, or a demonstrative, the proposition is personal. If the doer indicates generality (e.g., indefinite noun in context of generalization), it is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عَلِمْتَ هذا ؛ فاعلَمْ أنَّ جملة البسملة : إمَّا اسميَّةٌ، أو فعليَّةٌ . فالاسميَّةُ إنْ كانَ المسند إليه فيها مضافاً كابتدائي؛ فهي: شخصيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَتِ الإضافة للعهد الحضوري ؛ إذ المراد : هذا الابتداء المعين كائن بسم الله ... إلخ ، والشَّخصيَّةُ : - على مَا مَرَّ - مَا مَوضوعُهَا مُشخَصٌ مُعيَّنٌ . وكُلِّيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَتِ الإضافة للاستغراق ؛ بمعنى : إِنَّ كُلَّ ابتداء من ابتداءِ التَّأليف كائن بسم الله ... إلخ، وَسُورُها الإضافةُ الدَّالَّة على العموم؛ إذِ السُّورُ ما دلَّ على الإحاطةِ بِكُلِّ الأفراد أو بعضها ؛ لفظاً كان أو لا، ولكنَّ الغَالِب كونه لفظاً، فَمَنْ عَرَّفَهُ بِاللَّفظ الدال على كَمِّيَّةِ الأفرادِ كُلا أو بعضاً؛ جرى على الغالب. - وجُزئيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَتْ لِلجنسِ في ضِمْنِ فردٍ مُبهم، وَسُورها الإضافةُ الدَّالَّة الإحاطة ببعض الأفرا الأفراد. قال بعضُ الفُضَلاء : ومُهملةٌ إِنْ كانَتْ لِلجنسِ ، وَلَو على سبيل الاحتمال بأن فقط، أو محتملةٌ لهُ وَلِغيرِهِ مِنَ العهدِ والاستغراق، وذلكَ لِمَا تَقرَّرَ أَنَّ الإضافة تأتي لما تأتي له اللام. وذكر شيخُنَا العدوي فى جُملة «الحمدُ للهِ : أَنَّ «ألْ» فيها إنْ كانَت للجنس ؛ تَكُن القضيَّةُ شَخصيَّةٌ ؛ لأنَّ الجنس هو الحقيقة المعيَّنةُ في الذهنِ؛ أي: المشخّصة فيهِ، وَكَذا يُقالُ هنا . وإنْ كانَ المسند إليه فيها مُعرَّفاً بـ: «أل» نحو: الابتداء؛ فَشخصيَّةٌ : إِنْ كَانَتْ أَلْ للعهدِ، وَكُلِّيَّةٌ إِنْ كانَت للاستغراق، وجزئيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَت للجنس في ضمن فردٍ مُبهم، ومُهملةٌ : إنْ كانَت لِلجنسِ وَلَو على سبيل الاحتمال، ويأتي فيهِ مَا لشيخِنَا العلامة. والفعليّةُ شَخصيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَ فاعلُ الفعل ضميراً معيَّناً كَضميرِ الفعل المضارع المبدوء بالهمزةِ كَابدأ ، أوْ عَلَماً كَبَدأ زيدٌ بِسم الله ... إلخ، أو اسم إشارةٍ كبدأ هذا بِسمِ الله ... إلخ؛ لتعيَّنَ مَوضوعُهَا ، وَكُلِّيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَ غَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرَ دالا على التَّعْمِيمِ
When you know this, know that the Basmala sentence is either nominal or verbal. The nominal: if the predicate to it is an idafa like 'ibtida'i' (my beginning), then it is personal if the idafa is for the present covenant, meaning: this specific beginning is with the name of Allah... etc., and the personal proposition is as previously – its subject is a specified individual. And it is universal if the idafa is for comprehensiveness, meaning: every beginning of authorship is with the name of Allah... etc. And its quantifier is the idafa indicating generality; for the quantifier is that which indicates encompassment of all individuals or some of them, whether word or not, but usually it is a word; so whoever defines it as a word indicating the quantity of individuals, all or some, follows the usual. And it is partial if it is for the genus within an ambiguous individual, and its quantifier is the idafa indicating encompassment of some individuals. Some of the learned said: It is indefinite if it is for the genus, even probabilistically, only, or probabilistically for it and for other than it of covenant and comprehensiveness; that is due to the established point that idafa comes for what the lam comes for. And our teacher al-'Adawi mentioned in the sentence 'Al-hamdu lillah' that if 'alif lam' in it is for the genus, then the proposition is personal because the genus is the specific reality in the mind, i.e., specified in it. And similarly is said here. And if the predicate to it is defined with 'alif lam' like 'al-ibtida' (the beginning), then it is personal if 'alif lam' is for covenant, universal if for comprehensiveness, partial if for genus within an ambiguous individual, and indefinite if for genus even probabilistically; and what our teacher said applies to it. And the verbal [sentence] is personal if the doer of the verb is a definite pronoun like the pronoun of the present verb beginning with hamza, e.g., 'abda' (I begin), or a proper noun like 'Zaid began with the name of Allah...', or a demonstrative pronoun like 'this began with the name of Allah...', because its subject is determined; and it is universal if other than what was mentioned indicates generalization.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage applies the logical classification of propositions to the specific example of the Basmala (بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم). The author first distinguishes nominal and verbal sentences. For nominal sentences, he considers three cases based on the subject: if it is an idafa (like 'ibtida'i'), the proposition can be personal (if the idafa is for the present covenant), universal (if for comprehensiveness), or partial (if for the genus within an ambiguous individual). He also mentions an indefinite type if the idafa is for the genus merely probabilistically. This is further supported by a reference to al-'Adawi's analysis of 'Al-hamdu lillah'. For subjects defined with 'alif lam', similar classifications apply. For verbal sentences, the proposition is personal if the doer is a specific pronoun, proper noun, or demonstrative, and universal if the doer indicates generalization. This detailed analysis shows how logical categories can be applied to the very first sentence of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The quantifier in a proposition can be implicit in the grammatical structure (idafa or 'alif lam'), and the same sentence can be classified as personal, universal, partial, or indefinite based on the intended meaning of that quantifier.

Classifying Propositions by the Definite Article
Author defines four proposition types based on 'al' meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a proposition as personal, universal, particular, or indefinite based on the intended meaning of the definite article 'al' in the subject.

Core Idea
The definite article 'al' is ambiguous: it can signal a personal, universal, particular, or indefinite proposition.

In Arabic logic, the quantifier of a proposition can be expressed by the definite article 'al' in the subject. This passage from the commentary on the Tadhheeb shows that 'al' is not always universal; it can indicate covenant, inclusion, genus within an individual, or genus by probability. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly interpreting logical statements in Islamic texts, especially when analyzing definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject with 'al'
Locate the subject of the proposition that is defined by the definite article 'al'. For example, in 'al-ibtida'' (الابتداء), the subject is 'al-ibtida''.
2
Determine the intended meaning of 'al'
Ask: Is 'al' for covenant (a specific known referent), for inclusion (all individuals of the species), for the genus (the whole species as a mental concept), or for the genus with possibility? The passage provides criteria: if 'al' is for the genus, it can be particular (if within an indefinite individual) or indefinite (if merely probable).
3
Classify the proposition type
Based on the meaning of 'al': personal (shakhsiyya) if covenant, universal (kulliyya) if inclusion, particular (juz'iyya) if genus within an indefinite individual, indefinite (muhmala) if genus by probability.
4
Note the quantifier
The quantifier (sawr) is often implied by the idafa construct. The passage explains that the quantifier can be a word or not; it is what indicates encompassing all or some individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكُلِّيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَتِ الإضافة للاستغراق ؛ بمعنى : إِنَّ كُلَّ ابتداء من ابتداءِ التَّأليف كائن بسم الله ... إلخ، وَسُورُها الإضافةُ الدَّالَّة على العموم؛ إذِ السُّورُ ما دلَّ على الإحاطةِ بِكُلِّ الأفراد أو بعضها ؛ لفظاً كان أو لا، ولكنَّ الغَالِب كونه لفظاً، فَمَنْ عَرَّفَهُ بِاللَّفظ الدال على كَمِّيَّةِ الأفرادِ كُلا أو بعضاً؛ جرى على الغالب. - وجُزئيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَتْ لِلجنسِ في ضِمْنِ فردٍ مُبهم، وَسُورها الإضافةُ الدَّالَّة الإحاطة ببعض الأفراد. قال بعضُ الفُضَلاء : ومُهملةٌ إِنْ كانَتْ لِلجنسِ ، وَلَو على سبيل الاحتمال بأن فقط، أو محتملةٌ لهُ وَلِغيرِهِ مِنَ العهدِ والاستغراق، وذلكَ لِمَا تَقرَّرَ أَنَّ الإضافة تأتي لما تأتي له اللام. وذكر شيخُنَا العدوي فى جُملة «الحمدُ للهِ : أَنَّ «ألْ» فيها إنْ كانَت للجنس ؛ تَكُن القضيَّةُ شَخصيَّةٌ ؛ لأنَّ الجنس هو الحقيقة المعيَّنةُ في الذهنِ؛ أي: المشخّصة فيهِ، وَكَذا يُقالُ هنا . وإنْ كانَ المسند إليه فيها مُعرَّفاً بـ: «أل» نحو: الابتداء؛ فَشخصيَّةٌ : إِنْ كَانَتْ أَلْ للعهدِ، وَكُلِّيَّةٌ إِنْ كانَت للاستغراق، وجزئيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَت للجنس في ضمن فردٍ مُبهم، ومُهملةٌ : إنْ كانَت لِلجنسِ وَلَو على سبيل الاحتمال، ويأتي فيهِ مَا لشيخِنَا العلامة.
And universal: if the idafa is for inclusion, meaning that every beginning of authorship is with the name of God... etc., and its quantifier is the idafa indicating generality; since the quantifier is what denotes encompassing all individuals or some of them, whether a word or not, but mostly it is a word. So whoever defined it as the word indicating the quantity of individuals, all or some, followed the majority. And particular: if it is for the genus within an indefinite individual, and its quantifier is the idafa indicating encompassing some of the individuals. Some virtuosi said: And indefinite: if it is for the genus, even by way of probability alone, or possible for it and for other than it from covenant and inclusion, because it has been established that the idafa comes for the same purposes as the definite article 'al'. And our teacher al-Adwi mentioned in the phrase 'Alhamdu lillah' that the 'al' in it, if it is for the genus, then the proposition becomes personal, because the genus is the specific reality in the mind, i.e., it is specified in it. And similarly it is said here. And if the subject in it is defined by 'al', like 'al-ibtida'', then it is personal if the 'al' is for covenant, and universal if for inclusion, and particular if for the genus within an indefinite individual, and indefinite if for the genus even by way of probability. And what our teacher the scholar said applies in it.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This is a commentary passage explaining the classification of propositions based on the quantifier in the subject. The commentator begins by defining a 'universal' proposition as one where the idafa is for inclusion (istiqhraq), meaning the phrase 'every beginning' is used. The quantifier here is the idafa indicating generality. He notes that a quantifier can be a word or not, but is mostly a word. Then he defines 'particular' propositions where the idafa is for the genus within an indefinite individual, indicating some individuals. Then he brings a quote from 'some virtuosi' who add 'indefinite' propositions: if the idafa is for the genus alone, even by way of probability, it is indefinite. Then he mentions the example of 'Alhamdu lillah' from Shaykh al-Adwi: if the 'al' is for the genus, it becomes a personal proposition because the genus itself is a specific mental reality (i.e., the genus 'al-hamd' is a specific concept). The same applies here. Then he summarizes the classification when the subject is defined by 'al' (e.g., 'al-ibtida''): it can be personal (if 'al' is for covenant), universal (if for inclusion), particular (if for genus within an individual), indefinite (if for genus even by probability). This passage teaches the nuanced distinction of types of quantified propositions in logic, emphasizing that the definite article is not always universal.

The type of proposition with a definite subject depends on the specific meaning of the definite article 'al', which can be for covenant, inclusion, genus within an individual, or genus by probability.

Propositions

Verbal Propositions and Modalities of the Basmala
Author extends classification to verbal propositions and analyzes basmala's modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a verbal proposition based on its subject and determine which modal categories apply to the basmala.

Core Idea
The basmala is not necessary but existential non-permanent, allowing multiple modal interpretations.

In this passage, the commentator first extends the classification of propositions to verbal ones, defining personal, universal, particular, and indefinite types. He then applies this framework to the basmala and explores its modal nuances. This shows how logical analysis illuminates a central Islamic phrase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the verbal proposition
In a verbal proposition like 'bada'a Zaidun bismillah', the agent 'Zaidun' is the subject. Note whether the agent is specific (pronoun, proper noun, demonstrative) or general.
The subject determines the proposition's type.
2
Determine the type of verbal proposition
If the subject is a specific pronoun or proper noun, it is personal (shakhsiyya). If it includes 'kull' (every) or a universal quantifier, it is universal (kulliyya). If 'ba'd' (some), it is particular (juz'iyya). If neither, it is indefinite (muhmala).
This classification mirrors that of nominal propositions.
3
Apply the two interpretations of the 'ba' in the basmala
If 'ba' is original (meaning 'with'), the basmala is a verbal proposition with the agent as the subject. If 'ba' is a connector (sila), the meaning becomes 'the name of God is begun with', making it a nominal proposition.
The choice of interpretation affects the classification.
4
Understand the modal hierarchy and assign modalities to the basmala
The basmala is an 'existential non-permanent' proposition. This is more specific than the absolute and possible modalities. Therefore, it can be expressed as general absolute, non-necessary existential, general possible, or special possible. It cannot be necessary because the beginning is not always with God's name.
The hierarchy: existential non-permanent < absolutes < possibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفعليّةُ شَخصيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَ فاعلُ الفعل ضميراً معيَّناً كَضميرِ الفعل المضارع المبدوء بالهمزةِ كَابدأ ، أوْ عَلَماً كَبَدأ زيدٌ بِسم الله ... إلخ، أو اسم إشارةٍ كبدأ هذا بِسمِ الله ... إلخ؛ لتعيَّنَ مَوضوعُهَا ، وَكُلِّيَّةٌ : إنْ كانَ غَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرَ دالا على التَّعْمِيمِ كَبَدأ كُلُّ مُؤلّفٍ بِسم الله ... إلخ، وجزئية : إن كان دالا على التبعيض كبدأ بعض المؤلّفينَ بِسم الله ... إلخ، ومُهملة : إن لم يدلّ على تعميم ولا على تبعيض مُؤلِّفٌ الله . . . إلخ ، فإنْ كانَ الفاعل مُعرَّفاً بـ «أل» فَنيهِ مَا مَرْ مِنَ الاحتمالات. وهذا كُلَّهُ عَلَى جَعْل الباءِ أصليَّةٌ ، وأمَّا عَلَى جَعْلِهَا صِلَةٌ، ويكون المعنى : اسمُ اللهِ مَبدوءٌ بِهِ؛ فَفِيها مَا مَرَّ في الإضافة، وكيفيَّةُ نسبة جملة البسملة الإطلاق المقيَّد بِاللأدوام الذاتي، فتصلحُ أن تكونَ وُجوديَّةٌ لا دائمةً بأنْ يُقال: ابتدائي كانن بِسمِ الله ... إلخ، بالإطلاقِ العام لا دائماً، ويلزمُ مِن ذلكَ صحة توجيهها بجهة المطلقة العامَّةِ بأنْ يُقالَ: ابتدائي كائن بسم الله ... إلخ، بالإطلاق العام، وبجهة الوجودية اللأضروريَّةِ بأن يُقالَ : ابتدائي كائن بسم الله ... إلخ، بالإطلاق العام لَا بِالضَّرورة، وَبِجهةِ الممكنة العامَّةِ بأنْ يُقالَ : ابتدائي كائنُ بِسم اللهِ . . . إلخ، بالإمكان العام. وَبِجهةِ الممكنة الخاصة بأنْ يُقالَ : ابتدائي كائنٌ بِسم الله ... إلخ، بالإمكان الخاص؛ لأنَّ الوجوديَّةَ اللادائمةَ أخصُّ مِنَ القضايا المذكورةِ لِمَا علمْتَ مِنْ أَنَّ الوجوديَّةَ اللادائمةَ أخصُّ مِنَ المطلقاتِ الَّتِي هِيَ أخصُّ مِنَ (١) الممكنتَين، ويلزمُ مِن وُجودِ الأخصّ وجودُ الأعمّ، وَلَا يصح توجيه جملة البسملة بجهةِ الضَّرورة؛ إذْ أعم جهاتِ الضَّروريَّاتِ جهة المنتشرة المطلقة لما علمتَ أنَّ أعم الضَّروريَّاتِ المنتشرة المطلقةُ، وَلَا يصحُ التَّوجيه بتلك الجهةِ ؛ لأنَّ ثبوت كَونِ الابتداء ببسم الله للابتداء ليس بضروري في وقتٍ .
And the verbal proposition is personal: if the agent of the verb is a specific pronoun like the pronoun of the present verb starting with hamza, like 'abda'u', or a proper noun like 'bada'a Zaidun bismillah...', or a demonstrative pronoun like 'bada'a hadha bismillah...', because its subject is determined. And universal: if what is mentioned indicates generality, like 'bada'a kullu mu'allifin bismillah...'. And particular: if it indicates partiality, like 'bada'a ba'du al-mu'allifina bismillah...'. And indefinite: if it indicates neither generality nor partiality, like 'mu'allifun bismillah...'. If the agent is defined by 'al', then it has the same possibilities as previously mentioned. All this is on the assumption that the 'ba' is original. As for making it a connector (sila), the meaning is 'the name of God is begun with'; then it has what was mentioned in the idafa. And the mode of the relationship of the basmala sentence is an absolute (mutlaq) restricted by essential permanence (al-adawam al-dhati). So it is valid for it to be an existential non-permanent (wujudiyya la da'ima) proposition, such that one says: 'My beginning is with the name of God...' with the general absolute, not permanent. And from that follows the validity of directing it with the modality of the general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma), that one says: 'My beginning is with the name of God...' with the general absolute; and with the modality of the non-necessary existential (al-wujudiyya la daruriyya), that one says: 'My beginning is with the name of God...' with the general absolute not by necessity; and with the modality of the general possible (al-mumkina al-'amma), that one says: 'My beginning is with the name of God...' with the general possibility; and with the modality of the special possible (al-mumkina al-khassa), that one says: 'My beginning is with the name of God...' with the special possibility; because the existential non-permanent is more specific than the mentioned propositions, since you know that the existential non-permanent is more specific than the absolutes which are more specific than the two possibles. And from the existence of the more specific follows the existence of the more general. And it is not valid to direct the basmala sentence with the modality of necessity, because the most general of the necessary modalities is the modality of the absolute pervasive (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa), as you know that the most general of the necessary modalities is the absolute pervasive. And it is not valid to direct it with that modality, because the affirmation of the beginning being with the name of God for the beginning is not necessary at any time.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage first extends the classification of propositions to verbal ones. A verbal proposition is 'personal' if the subject (agent) is a specific pronoun, proper noun, or demonstrative; 'universal' if the subject indicates generality (like 'kullu mu'allifin'); 'particular' if it indicates partiality (like 'ba'du al-mu'allifin'); 'indefinite' if it indicates neither (like 'mu'allifun'). If the agent is defined by 'al', then the same possibilities as for nominal propositions apply. The commentator then applies this to the basmala phrase 'bismillah'. He considers two possibilities for the preposition 'ba': if it is original (meaning 'with'), then the analysis above applies. If it is a connector (sila), then the meaning becomes 'the name of God is begun with', which is a nominal proposition with the same classification. Then he discusses the modalities of the basmala as a proposition: it is an 'existential non-permanent' proposition, meaning the beginning is with God's name in a general sense but not always necessarily. From this, it can be directed with various modal categories: the general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma), the non-necessary existential (wujudiyya la daruriyya), the general possible (mumkina 'amma), and the special possible (mumkina khassa). He explains the hierarchy: existential non-permanent is more specific than absolutes, which are more specific than possibles. Therefore, the existence of the more specific implies the existence of the more general. However, it cannot be directed with necessity because the beginning is not necessarily with God's name at every moment. This shows a sophisticated application of modal logic to the basmala.

مطلقة عامة

Propositions

Modal Classification of the Basmala Sentence
Author classifies the basmala proposition into applicable and inapplicable modal categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given proposition (e.g., the basmala) into its appropriate modal categories (absolute, possible, necessary, perpetual) using the principle that the existence of the more specific implies the more general and the negation of the more general implies the negation of the more specific.

Core Idea
The basmala is not necessary or perpetual; it is only absolute or possible.

In this lesson, we examine the logical status of the basmala sentence 'My beginning is with the name of Allah'. The commentator systematically tests which modal categories apply: absolute, possible, necessary, and perpetual. He shows that only the first two are valid, and explains why the latter two are excluded using a core logical principle. This analysis sharpens your ability to think modally about propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core proposition
The proposition under analysis is: 'My beginning is with the name of Allah' (ابتدائي كائن بسم الله). Note the subject (beginning) and predicate (with the name of Allah).
2
Check the broadest necessary (المنتشرة المطلقة)
The author identifies the broadest necessary modality as 'absolute widespread' (منتشرة مطلقة). This would mean the proposition is true necessarily at some time. Ask: Is it necessary that 'My beginning is with the name of Allah' at any time? The answer is no, because the beginning could be without the basmala at some times (e.g., when eating).
The principle: if the broadest is negated, all narrower necessities are also negated.
3
Check the broadest perpetual (العرفية العامة)
The broadest perpetual modality is 'general customary' (عرفية عامة), meaning the proposition holds at all times (eternally). Is the basmala true at all times? No, because the beginning is not always with the basmala. So the broadest perpetual is false, and thus all narrower perpetuals are negated.
Perpetuity implies truth at every moment; the basmala is not perpetual.
4
Verify absolute and possible categories
Since the proposition is not necessary or perpetual, it may be absolute (true at least once) or possible (could be true or false). The author shows it is a non-permanent existential (وجودية لا دائمة), which is a type of absolute. From that, general absolute, general possible, and special possible also follow because the more specific implies the more general.
The existence of the more specific (non-permanent existential) entails the more general (general absolute, etc.).
5
Consider the time-specific absolute from hadith compliance
Some scholars argue that by considering a specific time (e.g., when beginning to read a hadith), the basmala could be necessary at that moment (وقتية مطلقة). This would then allow a widespread absolute (منتشرة مطلقة) because time-specific is more specific than widespread.
This is an additional refinement; the baseline analysis excludes necessity and perpetuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كَبدأ كُلُّ مُؤلّفٍ بِسم الله ... إلخ، وجزئية : إن كان دالا على التبعيض كبدأ بعض المؤلّفينَ بِسم الله ... إلخ، ومُهملة : إن لم يدلّ على تعميم ولا على تبعيض مُؤلِّفٌ الله . . . إلخ ، فإنْ كانَ الفاعل مُعرَّفاً بـ «أل» فَنيهِ مَا مَرْ مِنَ الاحتمالات. وهذا كُلَّهُ على جَعْل الباءِ أصليَّةٌ ، وأمَّا عَلَى جَعْلِهَا صِلَةٌ، ويكون المعنى : اسمُ اللهِ مَبدوءٌ بِهِ؛ فَفِيها مَا مَرَّ في الإضافة، وكيفيَّةُ نسبة جملة البسملة الإطلاق المقيَّد بِاللأدوام الذاتي، فتصلحُ أن تكونَ وُجوديَّةٌ لا دائمةً بأنْ يُقال: ابتدائي كانن بِسمِ الله ... إلخ، بالإطلاقِ العام لا دائماً، ويلزمُ مِن ذلكَ صحة توجيهها بجهة المطلقة العامَّةِ بأنْ يُقالَ: ابتدائي كائن بسم الله ... إلخ، بالإطلاق العام، وبجهة الوجودية اللأضروريَّةِ بأن يُقالَ : ابتدائي كائن بسم الله ... إلخ، بالإطلاق العام لَا بِالضَّرورة، وَبِجهةِ الممكنة العامَّةِ بأنْ يُقالَ : ابتدائي كائنُ بِسم اللهِ . . . إلخ، بالإمكان العام. وَبِجهةِ الممكنة الخاصة بأنْ يُقالَ : ابتدائي كائنٌ بِسم الله ... إلخ، بالإمكان الخاص ؛ لأنَّ الوجوديَّةَ اللادائمةَ أخصُّ مِنَ القضايا المذكورةِ لِمَا علمْتَ مِنْ أَنَّ الوجوديَّةَ اللادائمةَ أخصُّ مِنَ المطلقاتِ الَّتِي هِيَ أخصُّ مِنْ الممكنتَين، ويلزمُ مِن وُجودِ الأخصّ وجودُ الأعمّ، وَلَا يصح توجيه جملة البسملة بجهةِ الضَّرورة؛ إذْ أعم جهاتِ الضَّروريَّاتِ جهة المنتشرة المطلقة لما علمتَ أنَّ أعم الضَّروريَّاتِ المنتشرة المطلقةُ، وَلَا يصحُ التَّوجيه بتلك الجهةِ ؛ لأنَّ ثبوت كَونِ الابتداء ببسم الله للابتداء ليس بضروري في وقتٍ . وحينئذ ; فَلَا يصحُ التَّوجيه بجهةٍ بقيَّةِ الضَّروريَّاتِ ; إذْ يلزمُ مِن نفي الأعم نفي الأخص ، وَكَذا لا يصحُ تَوجيهها بجهةِ الدَّوامِ ; إذ أعم جهاتِ الدَّوائم جهةُ العُرفيَّةِ العامَّةِ ; لما علمتَ أنَّ العُرفيَّةَ العامَّةَ أعمُ الدَّوائمِ، وَلَا يصح التوجيه بتلك الجهةِ; لأنَّ ثبوت كونِ الابتداء ببسم الله للابتداء ليسَ بدائم، وحينئذ ; فَلَا يصح التَّوجيه بجهةٍ ببقيَّةِ الدَّوائمِ ; إذْ يلزمُ مِن نفي الأعم نفي الأخص، فظهر أنَّ جملة البسملة يصح أن تكونَ مِنَ المطلقاتِ الثَّلاث، وأنْ تكونَ مِنَ الممكنتَيْنِ، وَلَا يصحُ أنْ تكونَ مِنَ الضَّروريَّاتِ السَّبْعِ ، وَلَا مِنَ الدَّوائم الثلاث، أفادَهُ بعضُ الفُضَلاء. واستظهر بعضُهُم أَنَّهُ يَصحُ أنْ تكونَ وقتيَّةٌ مُطلقة بملاحظة امتثال الحديث، والضَّرورة بحسبِهِ، فيُقالُ حينئذ: ابتدائي كائن بسم الله ... إلخ بِالضَّرورة وقتَ الامتثال بالحديثِ، وحينئذٍ يصح أن تكونَ مُنتشرةً مُطلقةً بأنْ يُقال: ابتدائي كائن الله ... إلخ بالضَّرورة في وقتٍ مَا ; لأنَّ الوقتيَّة المطلقة أخصُّ مِنَ المنتشرة المطلقة، ويلزمُ مِن وجودِ الأخص وجود الأعمّ، وَكَذا يصح أن تكونَ وقتيَّةٌ بأنْ يُقال : ابتدائي كائن بسم الله ... إلخ بالضرورة وقت الامتثال لا دائماً، وأنْ تكونَ مُنتشرة بأنْ يُقالَ : ابتدائي كائن بسم الله ... إلخ بِالضَّرورة وقتاً ما لا دائماً ، تَأَمَّلْ.
The beginning of every author with Bismillah ... etc., and particular: if it indicates partiality, like 'some authors began with Bismillah ... etc.,' and indefinite: if it indicates neither generality nor partiality, 'an author with Allah ... etc.' If the subject is defined with 'al', then within it are the possibilities that have passed. All of this is on the basis that the 'ba' is essential. As for on the basis that it is a conjunction, and the meaning becomes 'the name of Allah is begun with', then in it is what passed regarding the idafa. And the modality of the relation of the basmala sentence is absolute restricted to non-perpetuity essentiality, so it is valid to be a non-permanent existential, by saying: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' with general absoluteness not perpetually. And from that follows the validity of directing it by the modality of general absolute, by saying: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' with general absoluteness, and by the modality of non-necessary existential, by saying: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' with general absoluteness not by necessity, and by the modality of general possible, by saying: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' with general possibility, and by the modality of special possible, by saying: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' with special possibility. Because the non-permanent existential is more specific than the mentioned propositions, since you know that the non-permanent existential is more specific than the absolutes which are more specific than the two possibles, and from the existence of the more specific follows the existence of the more general. And it is not valid to direct the basmala sentence by the modality of necessity, because the broadest of the modalities of necessities is the modality of absolute widespread, since you know that the broadest of necessities is the absolute widespread. And directing by that modality is not valid because the establishment of the beginning being with Bismillah for the beginning is not necessary at a time. Then it is not valid to direct by any [other] modality of the remaining necessities, because from the negation of the more general follows the negation of the more specific. Similarly, it is not valid to direct it by the modality of perpetuity, because the broadest of the modalities of perpetuities is the modality of general customary, since you know that the general customary is the broadest of the perpetuities. And directing by that modality is not valid because the establishment of the beginning being with Bismillah for the beginning is not perpetual. Then it is not valid to direct by any [other] modality of the remaining perpetuities, because from the negation of the more general follows the negation of the more specific. So it appears that the basmala sentence is valid to be among the three absolutes and among the two possibles, and it is not valid to be among the seven necessities nor among the three perpetuities. This was reported by some scholars. And some of them considered that it is valid to be a time-specific absolute by taking into account compliance with the hadith, and the necessity is according to it. So then it is said: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' by necessity at the time of compliance with the hadith. And then it is valid to be an absolute widespread, by saying: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' by necessity at some time, because the time-specific absolute is more specific than the absolute widespread, and from the existence of the more specific follows the existence of the more general. And similarly it is valid to be time-specific, by saying: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' by necessity at the time of compliance not perpetually, and to be widespread, by saying: 'my beginning is with the name of Allah ... etc.,' by necessity at some time not perpetually. Ponder.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of the basmala sentence in terms of modal propositions (qadaya mawjiha). The commentator first discusses the quantification of the subject 'بدأ' (beginning) as general, particular, or indefinite. Then, assuming the preposition 'ب' is essential (أصلي), he examines the modal status of the claim that 'my beginning is with the name of Allah.' He argues that it can be a non-permanent existential (وجودية لا دائمة), which is a specific type of absolute proposition, and from that follows the validity of classifying it as a general absolute (مطلقة عامة), a non-necessary existential (وجودية لاضرورية), a general possible (ممكنة عامة), and a special possible (ممكنة خاصة). He explains that the non-permanent existential is more specific than the absolutes and the possibles, and the rule is: if the more specific exists, the more general exists. Then he rules out necessary (ضرورية) and perpetual (دائمة) modals. For necessity, the broadest necessary is the general widespread (منتشرة مطلقة), but the basmala is not necessary at any time. Since the broader is negated, all narrower necessities are also negated. Similarly for perpetuity, the broadest perpetual is the general customary (عرفية عامة), and the basmala is not perpetual, so all perpetuals are invalid. Therefore, the basmala can only be one of the three absolutes or the two possibles. Some later scholars added that it could be a time-specific absolute (وقتية مطلقة) by considering compliance with the hadith at a specific time, which then allows a general widespread (منتشرة مطلقة) because the specific implies the general. The passage ends with 'تأمل' (ponder). All examples are from the basmala itself, a core Islamic invocation.

Definition of the Predicative Relation
Author defines the predicative relation and argues for one interpretation over another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of the predicative relation (al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya) and identify which one the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) adopt, along with the reason for their choice.

Core Idea
The predicative relation is the common substrate of both affirmation and negation, not two separate relations.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi clarifies the nature of the predicative relation, a foundational concept in logic. He presents two competing definitions and argues for the one that makes the relation a single entity underlying both positive and negative propositions. By parsing his Arabic and following his reasoning, you will understand why the verifiers' definition is preferred and how it affects the analysis of propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the Arabic carefully and locate the two definitions of the predicative relation. The first is introduced with 'عند المحققين' (according to the verifiers) and the second with 'قيل' (it is said). Write them down in both Arabic and English.
The first definition says the relation is 'thubūt al-maḥmūl li-l-mawḍūʿ fī l-mūjaba wa-l-sāliba' (affirmation of the predicate to the subject in both affirmative and negative). The second says it is 'thubūt ... fī l-mūjaba wa-intifāʾ ... fī l-sāliba' (affirmation in affirmatives and negation in negatives).
2
Locate the preferred definition
Find where al-Dasuqi states his preference. He says 'وإنما كان التحقيق الأول' (and the first verification is correct). Note the reason he gives: 'لأن مورد الإيجاب والسلب هو النسبة' (because the locus of affirmation and negation is the relation).
3
Understand the argument for preference
Al-Dasuqi elaborates that affirmation (ījāb) and negation (salb) are perceptions of the relation's occurrence or non-occurrence. If the relation were two different things (one for affirmatives, another for negatives), it could not be the single locus of both acts. Grasp this logical point.
The relation must be one thing that can either occur or not occur. In negative propositions, we still have the same relation, but we perceive that it does not hold.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Take a simple proposition like 'الصلاة واجبة' (Prayer is obligatory). The predicative relation is the connection of obligation to prayer. In the negative 'الصلاة ليست واجبة' (Prayer is not obligatory), the same relation exists but is denied. This shows the relation is one thing, not two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن الدسوقي كلِّ مِنَ حُكْمِيَّةٌ إلَّا بعد تعلَّقِ الحكم بها لا ،قبله، فَلَو قال المصنِّفُ : لِلنِّسبة الخبريَّةِ بدل الحكميَّة؛ كانَ أَوْلَى والنِّسبةُ الحُكميَّةُ هي ثبوت المحمول للموضوع في ، : القضيَّةِ الموجبة والسالبة عند المحققين . وقِيلَ: إنَّها ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع في الموجبة، وانتفاء المحمول عن الموضوع في السالبة، وعليهِ مَشَى الشَّارحُ فيما يأتي في قولِهِ : وَلَا شَكٍّ أنَّ مَنْ أدرك ... إلخ . وإنَّما كانَ التَّحقيق الأوَّل ؛ لأنَّ موردَ الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ، والإيقاع والانتزاع هو النّسبةُ، والإيجاب والإيقاع عبارة عن إدراكِ وقوعِهَا ؛ أي: مُطابقتها للواقع، والسَّلْبُ والانتزاع عبارة عن إدراكِ عدم وقوعِهَا ؛ أي: عدمُ مُطابقتها للواقع ونفْسِ الأمر، ولا تكونُ النّسبةُ مَورداً لِما ذكر إلا إذا كانت بمعنى ثبوت المحمول
The introduction of the matn. Al-Dasuqi: Every ... a predicative [relation] except after the judgment attaches to it, not before. So if the author had said 'relational proposition' (al-nisba al-khabariyya) instead of 'predicative', it would have been better. The predicative relation is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject in both affirmative and negative propositions according to the verifiers. And it is said: It is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject in the affirmative, and the negation of the predicate from the subject in the negative. And the commentator followed this [second view] in what follows in his statement: 'And there is no doubt that whoever perceives...' etc. And the first verification is correct because the locus of affirmation and negation, and of assertion and withdrawal, is the relation. Affirmation and assertion are expressions for the perception of its occurrence, i.e., its correspondence to reality. Negation and withdrawal are expressions for the perception of its non-occurrence, i.e., its non-correspondence to reality and the actual state of affairs. And the relation is not a locus for what was mentioned unless it is in the sense of the affirmation of the predicate.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the predicative relation (al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya). The commentator al-Dasuqi begins by noting an improvement to the author's wording: instead of 'predicative relation', it would have been better to say 'relational proposition' (al-nisba al-khabariyya). He then gives two interpretations of the predicative relation. The first, held by the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn), is that the predicative relation is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject in both affirmative and negative propositions. The second, which the commentator himself follows elsewhere, is that it is affirmation in affirmatives and negation in negatives. Al-Dasuqi then argues that the first interpretation is correct because the relation is the locus of affirmation and negation, assertion and withdrawal. Affirmation and assertion are the perception of the relation's occurrence (i.e., correspondence to reality), while negation and withdrawal are the perception of its non-occurrence. For the relation to be the locus of these acts, it must be understood as a single thing that can be either affirmed or negated, not as two different relations (one of affirmation and one of negation). Hence, the predicative relation is the common element of the subject-predicate connection that underlies both affirmation and negation.

ثبوت المحمول

D) Thubūt al-maḥmūl li-l-mawḍūʿ aw intifāʾuhu

Key Takeaway
The predicative relation is a single mental connection that can be either affirmed (in positive propositions) or denied (in negative propositions), and it is not two separate relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'الزكاة واجبة على كل مسلم يملك النصاب' (Zakat is obligatory for every Muslim who possesses the nisab). Identify the subject and predicate, and explain the predicative relation in both the affirmative form and its negation.
?
Compare the two definitions of the predicative relation given in the passage. Argue which one better explains the logical structure of the Quranic verse 'لا إكراه في الدين' (There is no compulsion in religion).

The Relation as Subject of Judgment in Affirmative and Negative Propositions
Author defines the roles of nisba in affirmation and negation judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of the nisba in affirmative and negative propositions and correctly classify a given proposition as affirmative or negative based on the perception of the relation.

Core Idea
Judgment in logic is always about the relation (nisba), not the terms themselves.

In this lesson, you will dive into the heart of logical judgment. The passage from the Tadhheeb clarifies what exactly we are affirming or negating when we make a proposition. It is not the subject or predicate individually, but the relation that binds them. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing any proposition in Islamic legal theory and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'النسبة' (nisba), 'الإيجاب' (ijab), and 'السلب' (salb) in the Arabic text. These are the core concepts.
2
Parse the definition of affirmation
The author says 'الإيجاب والإيقاع عبارة عن إدراكِ وقوعِهَا'. This means affirmation is perceiving the occurrence of the relation (its correspondence to reality).
3
Parse the definition of negation
Similarly, 'السَّلْبُ والانتزاع عبارة عن إدراكِ عدم وقوعِهَا' means negation is perceiving the non-occurrence of the relation (its lack of correspondence).
4
Understand the condition for the relation as subject
The relation is only the subject of these judgments if it means 'ثبوت المحمول للموضوع' (the subsistence of the predicate for the subject). This clarifies that the relation is not any connection, but specifically the predicative link.
5
Apply to sample propositions
Take an affirmative proposition like 'الزكاة واجبة' (Zakat is obligatory). Identify the nisba: the subsistence of 'obligatory' for 'zakat'. In an affirmative judgment, we perceive that this relation holds in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّما كانَ التَّحقيق الأوَّل ؛ لأنَّ موردَ الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ، والإيقاع والانتزاع هو النّسبةُ، والإيجاب والإيقاع عبارة عن إدراكِ وقوعِهَا ؛ أي: مُطابقتها للواقع، والسَّلْبُ والانتزاع عبارة عن إدراكِ عدم وقوعِهَا ؛ أي: عدمُ مُطابقتها للواقع ونفْسِ الأمر، ولا تكونُ النّسبةُ مَورداً لِما ذكر إلا إذا كانت بمعنى ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، فذلك الثبوت تدركُ مُطابَقَته للواقع في القضيَّةِ الموجبة، ويدرك عدمُ مطابقتِه للواقع في القضيَّةِ السَّالبة.
Indeed the first verification is because the subject of affirmation and negation, and of positing and withdrawal, is the relation. Affirmation and positing are terms for perceiving its occurrence, i.e., its correspondence to reality. Negation and withdrawal are terms for perceiving its non-occurrence, i.e., its lack of correspondence to reality and to the thing itself. The relation is only a subject for what was mentioned if it is in the sense of the subsistence of the predicate for the subject. That subsistence is perceived in the affirmative proposition, and its non-correspondence to reality is perceived in the negative proposition.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines the conceptual foundation of judgment in logic. The author explains that the primary subject of affirmation (ijab) and negation (salb) is the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate. Affirmation and positing (iqa') refer to the perception that this relation actually corresponds to reality; negation and withdrawal (intiza') refer to the perception that it does not. The relation only serves as the subject of these judgments when it is understood as the subsistence (thubut) of the predicate for the subject. In an affirmative proposition, we perceive that this subsistence matches the way things are; in a negative proposition, we perceive that it does not. This clarifies that judgment is always about the link between concepts, not about the concepts themselves.

Key Terms
النسبة
الإيجاب
السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the subject of affirmation and negation?
A) The subject of the proposition
B) The predicate of the proposition
C) The relation (nisba) between subject and predicate
D) The truth value of the proposition

Key Takeaway
The relation (nisba) between subject and predicate, understood as the subsistence of the predicate for the subject, is the true subject of affirmation and negation in any proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'الصلاة واجبة' (Prayer is obligatory). Identify the nisba, and explain what is being perceived in an affirmative judgment.
?
Create a similar analysis for a negative proposition: 'الخمر ليست مباحة' (Wine is not permissible).

Distinguishing Salb al-Umum from Umum al-Salb
Al-Sharnubi distinguishes two logical operations: negation of universality and universality of negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between salb al-umum and umum al-salb and identify the correct logical form (particular negative vs universal negative) in Arabic statements.

Core Idea
Negation of universality is not the same as universality of negation; one is particular, the other is universal.

Al-Sharnubi's footnote provides a crucial distinction that is often confused: the difference between saying 'not all' and 'none'. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly interpreting logical statements in Islamic texts, especially when dealing with universal or particular negations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Arabic phrases
Locate 'salb al-umum' (سلب العموم) and 'umum al-salb' (عموم السلب) in the Arabic passage. These are the two operations being compared.
2
Understand the direction of negation
For salb al-umum, the negation is directed to some individuals only (particular). For umum al-salb, the negation covers every individual (universal).
3
Convert to logical forms
Salb al-umum is equivalent to a particular negative: 'Some X is not Y'. Umum al-salb is equivalent to a universal negative: 'No X is Y'.
Example: 'Not every animal is created in vain' (salb al-umum) vs 'No animal is created in vain' (umum al-salb).
4
Apply to the given examples
The matn's statement 'thought is not always correct' is a case of salb al-umum (some thought is not correct). Al-Sharnubi's additional example 'I have not mastered all the sciences' also illustrates salb al-umum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُهُ : سلب العموم) الفرق بينه وبين عموم السَّلب أن الأوّل: موجة فيه السبب لبعض الأفراد كما هنا وكقولك : لم أتقن كلَّ العلوم، فهو في قوة السالبة الجزئية أي : بعض الفكر ليس بصواب وبعض العلوم لم أتقنها، والثَّاني : هو عموم السَّلب قد يوجه فيه السَّلب إلى كلّ الأفراد نحو كل حيوان لم يخلق عبثاً فهو في قوَّة السَّالبة الكلية : أي لا شيء من الحيوان بمخلوق عبثاً . ا . هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
(His statement: 'negation of universality') The difference between it and universality of negation is that the first: the negation is directed to some individuals, as here and as your saying: 'I have not mastered all the sciences.' So it is equivalent to a particular negative: i.e., some thought is not correct, and some sciences I have not mastered. The second is universality of negation: the negation may be directed to all individuals, like 'Every animal was not created in vain.' So it is equivalent to a universal negative: i.e., no animal is created in vain. End. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This footnote by al-Sharnubi clarifies the logical distinction between two operations that are often confused. 'Negation of universality' (salb al-umum) means negating the universality of a predicate, resulting in a particular negative proposition: 'Not every X is Y' or 'Some X is not Y'. The example from the matn is 'thought is not always correct' meaning some thought is not correct. Another example: 'I have not mastered all the sciences' meaning some sciences I have not mastered. On the other hand, 'universality of negation' (umum al-salb) means negating the predicate from every individual, resulting in a universal negative: 'No X is Y'. Example: 'Every animal was not created in vain' meaning no animal is created in vain. This distinction is crucial because the matn's statement is a case of the first kind, not the second. Understanding this prevents misinterpretation of the logical scope of the statement.

Key Terms
سلب العموم
عموم السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Sharnubi, the statement 'لم أتقن كل العلوم' (I have not mastered all the sciences) is equivalent to which logical form?
A) Universal negative
B) Particular negative
C) Universal affirmative
D) Particular affirmative

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that salb al-umum negates the predicate from some individuals (particular negative), while umum al-salb negates it from all (universal negative).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following Arabic sentences as either salb al-umum or umum al-salb: (1) ليس كل طالب مجتهدًا. (2) لا شيء من الطلاب بمجتهد. (3) بعض الناس ليسوا صادقين.
?
Translate the Quranic verse 'وَمَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا لَاعِبِينَ' (21:16) into a logical proposition and determine if it is salb al-umum or umum al-salb.

Defining Khabar: The Proposition in Islamic Logic
Author defines khabar as a complete composite that can be true or false and addresses a famous objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a khabar (proposition) in Islamic logic, correctly apply the condition 'min haythu huwa' to exclude external factors, and defend the definition against the famous objection that no statement can admit both truth and falsity.

Core Idea
A khabar can be true or false when considered in itself, not because of external evidence.

This passage defines the fundamental unit of logical reasoning: the khabar, or proposition. But the definition immediately runs into a famous puzzle: how can a statement 'admit both truth and falsity' when every actual statement is either true or false? The commentators walk you through the solution, showing that the key lies in considering the statement's essence, abstracted from all external factors.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence in the Arabic that defines khabar. It states: 'a complete composite (al-murakkab al-tamm) that admits truth and falsity in itself (min haythu huwa).' Identify the genus (complete composite) and differentia (admitting truth and falsity in itself).
The definition appears at the beginning of the passage: 'إِمَّا (خَبَرٌ) إن احتمل الصدق والكذب من حيث هو'
2
Understand the objection
The objector argues that the definition fails because any actual khabar is either true or false. If it is true, it cannot be false; if false, it cannot be true. Therefore, no khabar can admit both truth and falsity, so the definition applies to nothing.
This is a famous puzzle in Islamic logic, raised by al-'Attar.
3
Analyse the answer
The answer distinguishes between actual truth-value and rational possibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli). 'Admission' here means that the mind can conceive of the khabar being true or false when considering its essence alone, disregarding external factors like the speaker, evidence, or actual reality. The qualification 'min haythu huwa' (in itself) ensures this abstraction.
Al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar both emphasize that the khabar's essence does not necessitate its truth or falsity; both are rationally possible.
4
Apply the resolution
Take the khabar 'The world is temporally originated.' Consider it in itself, without any evidence. Is it rationally possible for this statement to be true or false? Yes, because the concept does not force either outcome. Thus, it meets the definition even if, in reality, it is true due to evidence.
This shows how the definition includes statements whose truth or falsity is actually certain, as long as they are not necessary in themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِمَّا (خَبَرٌ) إن احتمل الصدق والكذب من حيث هو، وهو العمدة في بابِ التصديقات. الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : إن احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ) أي : إن تصوَّر العقل صدق مضمونه وكذبه. (قَوْلُهُ : من حيث هو) أي : من حيث ذاتُه؛ بقطع النظر عن قائله، فهذهِ الحيثيَّةُ لإدخال الأقوال المقطوع بصدقها، والأقوال المقطوع بكذبها. (قَوْلُهُ : وهو العمدة) أي : ما يُعتمدُ عليه، وقوله: (في باب التصديقات)؛ أي: في باب الموصل لِلتَّصديقات؛ لأنَّ المفيد لِلتَّصديقات إنَّما هو الخبرُ، كما أنَّ الموصل لِلتَّصوراتِ هو المركَّبُ التَّقييدي. العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : إن احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ) ههنا سؤالٌ مَشهور؛ وهو أنَّ هذا التعريف لا يصدقُ على شيءٍ مِنَ الأخبار بحسب الظاهرِ؛ لأنَّ الخبرَ إِمَّا أن يكونَ مُطَابِقاً للواقع أم لا، فإنْ كانَ الأوَّلُ لم يحتمل الكذبَ، وإنْ كانَ الثَّاني لم يحتمل الصدق، فهو إما صادق دائماً أو كاذب دائماً، فلم يصدقِ التَّعريف على شيءٍ. لا يُقالُ : الواو بمعنى «أو»؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : يلغو حينئذ ذكر الاحتمال. وأُجيب عن أصل الإشكال بحمل الاحتمال على الجواز العقليّ بالنَّظر إلى مفهوم المركَّب وماهيتِهِ؛ مع قطع النَّظَرِ عن جميع الأمور الخارجة عنها كخصوصيَّةِ القابلِ والدَّليلِ والطَّرفَيْنِ، وهو وقوع ثبوتِ شيءٍ لشيءٍ أو لا وقوعه إذعاناً في الحمليَّاتِ، ووقوع اتصال قضيَّةٍ بقضيَّةٍ أو لا وقوعُهُ إذعاناً في المتّصلاتِ، ووقوع انفصال قضيَّةٍ عن قضيَّةٍ أو لا وقوعُهُ إذعاناً في المنفصلات. ومن المعلوم أنَّ كُلَّ خَبَرٍ جائز الصدق والكذب عند العقلِ بالنَّظرِ إلى مجرَّدِ ماهيّتِهِ. وأمَّا ما أوردَ مِن الدَّورِ المشهورِ في التَّعريف فَجَوابُهُ مَشهورٌ مثله. (قَوْلُهُ: من حيث هو) الحيثيَّةُ لِلإطلاق؛ أي: احتمالُهُ الصَّدقَ والكَذِبَ من حيث ذاتُهُ؛ لا لخصوصيَّةٍ فيهِ ولا في قائلِه، فدخل ما هو مقطوع بصدقه أو كذبه لأمرٍ خارج عن ماهِيَّةِ الخَبَرِ. (قَوْلُهُ: وهو العمدة) أي: المعتمدُ عليهِ في بابِ التصديقات، أرادَ بِبابِ التصديقاتِ جميع مباحثها، ومن جملة تلك المباحثِ البحثُ عن المركَبِ التّام.
Or (a khabar) if it admits truth and falsity in itself (min haythu huwa), and it is the mainstay in the chapter of assents (tasdiqat). Al-Dasuqi: His saying 'if it admits truth and falsity' means that the mind conceives the truth of its content and its falsity. His saying 'in itself' means in its essence, abstracting from its speaker; this qualification is to include statements whose truth or falsity is certain. His saying 'and it is the mainstay' means that upon which reliance is placed. His saying 'in the chapter of assents' means in the chapter of what leads to assents, because what benefits assents is only the khabar, just as what leads to concepts is the restrictive composite. Al-'Attar: His saying 'if it admits truth and falsity' — here there is a famous objection: that this definition does not apply to any khabar in appearance, because a khabar is either in conformity with reality or not. If the first, it does not admit falsity; if the second, it does not admit truth. So it is either always true or always false, thus the definition does not apply to anything. One cannot say: the waw is in the sense of 'or', because we say: then the mention of probability becomes pointless. The answer to the original problem is given by taking 'admission' as rational possibility with respect to the concept of the composite and its essence, disregarding all external matters such as the specificity of the recipient, the evidence, and the terms. It is the occurrence of a thing's being affirmed of a thing or not occurring, assented to in categoricals, and the occurrence of connection of one proposition with another or not occurring, assented to in conditionals, and the occurrence of disconnection of one proposition from another or not occurring, assented to in disjunctives. It is known that every khabar is possible of truth and falsity in the mind with respect to its mere essence. As for the famous vicious circle raised concerning the definition, its answer is similarly famous. His saying 'in itself' is for absoluteness; i.e., its admission of truth and falsity is from its essence, not due to something particular in it or its speaker, so what is certain of truth or falsity due to something outside the essence of the khabar is included. His saying 'and it is the mainstay' means that upon which reliance is placed in the chapter of assents; by 'chapter of assents' he intends all its investigations, and among those investigations is the investigation of the complete composite.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section on 'khabar' (statement/proposition), a type of complete composite. The main text defines khabar as a complete composite that admits truth and falsity in itself (min haythu huwa), and states that it is the mainstay in the chapter of assents (tasdiqat). The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that 'admits truth and falsity' means the mind can conceive of its content being true or false. 'In itself' abstracts from the speaker, so that statements whose truth or falsity is known with certainty from external evidence are still included under the definition, because the definition considers the essence. Al-'Attar then presents a famous objection: In reality, a khabar is either true or false, so it cannot admit both. The answer is that 'admission' here means rational possibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli) when considering the mere concept of the composite, disregarding all external factors. For example, the categorical proposition 'A is B' in itself involves the possibility of A being B or not being B. Similarly for conditionals and disjunctives. Thus every khabar is rationally possible of both truth and falsity when considering its essence alone. Al-'Attar also mentions a famous vicious circle in the definition, but notes that its answer is well-known. The passage concludes by reiterating that 'in itself' absolves from external particularities, and that 'mainstay' indicates that khabar is central to the study of assents.
