خبر
احتمال الصدق والكذب
من حيث هو
عمدة
تصديقات

D) It limits the definition to categorical propositions only.

Key Takeaway
The definition of khabar as a statement that admits truth and falsity in itself is defended by understanding 'admission' as rational possibility when considering the essence, not actual truth-value.

8 min
Definition of Khabar and the Famous Objection
Al-Dasuqi and al-Attar define khabar and address the objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of khabar as 'that which admits truth and falsehood in itself' and how it overcomes the famous objection regarding actual truth/falsity.

Core Idea
The khabar is defined by intellectual possibility, not actual truth.

This passage defines the central term of logic, khabar (proposition), which is the foundation of all assent (tasdiq). The definition must be precise: a khabar is that which admits truth and falsehood in its essence. But a famous objection arises: every actual statement is either true or false, so how can it admit both? The commentators resolve this by distinguishing between the essence of the statement and external factors.

Guided Path
1
Locate the core definition
Find the precise wording: 'إِمَّا (خَبَرٌ) إن احتمل الصدق والكذب من حيث هو'. This is the definiens: 'if it admits truth and falsehood in itself'.
The phrase 'من حيث هو' is crucial.
2
Understand the qualifier 'in itself'
Al-Dasuqi explains that 'in itself' means considering the essence of the khabar, disregarding the speaker or evidence. This allows including statements that are known to be true or false due to external factors.
This prevents the definition from excluding most actual statements.
3
Analyze the famous objection
Al-Attar presents the objection: every actual khabar is either always true (if it matches reality) or always false (if it does not). So it cannot simultaneously admit both truth and falsehood. The definition appears to apply to no real statement.
This is a serious challenge to the definition.
4
See the resolution
The answer interprets 'admission' as intellectual possibility (jawaz 'aqli) with respect to the mere essence of the khabar, ignoring external matters like the actual state of affairs or evidence. For example, 'Zayd is standing' is in itself possible to be true or false, even if we know it is true.
Thus every khabar, by its essence, allows both truth and falsehood in the intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِمَّا (خَبَرٌ) إن احتمل الصدق والكذب من حيث هو، وهو العمدة في باب التَّصديقاتِ.
الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : إنِ احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ أي : إِنْ تَصَوَّرَ العقل صدق مضمونه وكذبه. (قَوْلُهُ : مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ) أي : من حيث ذاتُه؛ بقطع النظر عن قائله، فهذهِ الحيثيَّةُ لإدخال الأقوال المقطوع بصدقها، والأقوال المقطوع بكذبها. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الْعُمْدَةُ) أي : ما يُعتمدُ عليه، وقوله: (في باب التصديقات)؛ أي: في باب الموصل لِلتَّصديقات؛ لأنَّ المفيد لِلتَّصديقات إنَّما هو الخبرُ، كما أنَّ الموصل لِلتَّصوراتِ هو المركَّبُ التَّقييدي.
العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : إنِ احْتَمَلَ الصَّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ هَهُنا سؤالٌ مَشهور؛ وهو أنَّ هذا التعريف لا يصدقُ على شيءٍ مِنَ الأخبار بحسب الظاهرِ؛ لأنَّ الخبرَ إِمَّا أن يكونَ مُطَابِقاً للواقع أم لا، فإنْ كانَ الأوَّلُ لم يحتمل الكذبَ، وإنْ كانَ الثَّاني لم يحتمل الصدق، فهو إما صادق دائماً أو كاذب دائماً، فَلَمْ يصدقِ التَّعريف على شيءٍ. لا يُقالُ : الواو بمعنى «أو»؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : يلغُو حينئذ ذكر الاحتمال، وأُجيب عن أصل الأشكال بحمل الاحتمال على الجواز العقليّ بالنَّظر إلى مفهوم المركَّب وماهيتِهِ؛ مع قطع النَّظَرِ عَن جَميعِ الأمور الخارجة عنها كَخصوصيَّةِ القابلِ والدَّليلِ والطَّرفَيْنِ، وهو وقوع ثبوتِ شيءٍ لشيء أو لا وقوعه إذعاناً في الحمليَّاتِ، وَوقوع اتصال قضيَّةٍ بقضيَّةٍ أو لا وقوعُهُ إذعاناً في المتّصلاتِ، ووقوع انفصال قضيَّةٍ عن قضيَّةٍ أو لَا وُقوعُهُ إذعاناً في المنفصلات. ومن المعلوم أنَّ كُلَّ خَبَرٍ جائز الصدق والكذب عند العقلِ بالنَّظرِ إلى مجرَّدِ ماهيّتِهِ. وأمَّا مَا أوردَ مِن الدَّورِ المشهورِ في التَّعريف فَجَوابُهُ مَشهورٌ مثله. (قَوْلُهُ: مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ) الحيثيَّةُ لِلإطلاق؛ أي: احتمالُهُ الصَّدقَ والكَذِبَ مِن حيث ذاتُهُ؛ لا لخصوصيَّةٍ فيهِ ولا في قائِلِه، فدخل ما هو مقطوع بصدقه أو كذبه لأمرٍ خارج عَن ماهِيَّةِ الخَبَرِ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ العُمْدَةُ) أي : المعتمدُ عليهِ في بابِ التصديقات، أرادَ بِبابِ التصديقاتِ جميع مباحثها، ومن جملة تلك المباحثِ البحثُ عَنِ المركَبِ التّام.
Either (khabar) if it admits truth and falsehood in itself, and it is the foundation in the chapter of tasdiqat. al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'if it admits truth' means: if the intellect conceives the truth of its content and its falsehood. His statement: 'in itself' means: in terms of its essence, disregarding its speaker; this perspective is to include statements that are certain to be true and statements that are certain to be false. His statement: 'and it is the foundation' means: that which is relied upon. His statement: 'in the chapter of tasdiqat' means: in the chapter of what leads to tasdiqat, because that which benefits tasdiqat is only khabar, just as that which leads to tasawwurat is the restrictive compound. al-Attar: His statement: 'if it admits truth and falsehood' here is a famous objection: this definition does not apply to any khabar in appearance, because khabar either corresponds to reality or not. If the first, it does not admit falsehood; if the second, it does not admit truth. So it is either always true or always false, so the definition does not apply to anything. It cannot be said: the waw means 'or', because we say: then the mention of admission becomes pointless. The answer to the original difficulty is by interpreting 'admission' as intellectual possibility with respect to the concept of the compound and its essence, disregarding all external matters such as the particularity of the subject, the proof, and the two extremes. It is the occurrence of something for something or not occurring, assented to in categoricals, and the occurrence of connection of a proposition with a proposition or not occurring, assented to in conditionals, and the occurrence of disjunction of a proposition from a proposition or not occurring, assented to in disjunctives. And it is known that every khabar is possible of truth and falsehood in the intellect with respect to its mere essence. As for the famous circularity mentioned in the definition, its answer is equally famous. His statement: 'in itself' is for absoluteness, i.e., its admission of truth and falsehood in terms of its essence, not due to a particularity in it or in its speaker, so it includes what is known to be true or false due to an external matter that is not part of the essence of khabar. His statement: 'and it is the foundation' means: that which is relied upon in the chapter of tasdiqat. He intended by 'chapter of tasdiqat' all its topics, and among those topics is the investigation of the complete compound.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines 'khabar' (statement/proposition) and defends it against two famous objections. The definition states that a khabar is 'that which admits truth and falsehood in itself' and is the foundation of the chapter on tasdiqat (assents). Al-Dasuqi first clarifies the terms: 'admits truth' means the intellect can conceive both truth and falsehood of its content; 'in itself' means considering its essence alone, not its speaker—this allows including statements that are actually known to be true or false but are still khabar because their essence allows both possibilities; 'foundation' means it is relied upon in the study of tasdiqat, just as restrictive compounds are relied upon for tasawwurat. Then al-Attar raises a famous objection: every actual khabar is either true (if it matches reality) or false (if it does not), so it cannot both be possible of truth and falsehood at the same time. The answer is to understand 'admission' as intellectual possibility (jawaz 'aqli) when considering the mere essence of the khabar, not its actual state in reality. For example, the statement 'Zayd is standing' is in itself possible to be true or false, even if we know it is true because we see him standing. The definition includes such known statements because the certainty comes from an external factor (our observation), not from the essence of the statement itself. Al-Attar then explains that this conceptual possibility applies to all types of propositions: categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive. He also mentions another objection about circularity (al-dawr al-mashhur), but says its answer is equally famous. Finally, he reiterates that the perspective 'in itself' is for absoluteness, excluding any particularities. The lesson demonstrates how a rigorous definition must consider the essence of a thing, not its accidental properties.

Key Terms
خبر
من حيث هو
احتمال
باب التصديقات
الجواز العقلي

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, what makes a statement a khabar?
A) The statement must be true.
B) The statement must be false.
C) The statement must intellectually admit both truth and falsehood in its essence.
D) The statement must be spoken by a trustworthy person.

Key Takeaway
The definition of khabar depends on intellectual possibility of truth/falsehood in its essence, not its actual truth value, which allows it to include both known true and false statements.

?

The Verb as Predicate: Grammar vs. Logic
Author responds to the objection that verbs are not independent, contrasting logical and grammatical views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the grammatical and logical criteria for the independence of a predicate and explain why the logician considers the verb alone sufficient.

Core Idea
A verb can be a predicate in logic even if grammarians require an agent.

This passage addresses a common objection: verbs need their agents to form a complete sentence, so how can they be considered independent terms? The response reveals a fundamental difference between grammarians and logicians: grammarians focus on syntactic structure, while logicians focus on meaning. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing propositions in Islamic logic (al-mantiq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the objection in the Arabic: 'إن قلت: الفعل لا يخبر به وحده بل مع فاعله'. The objector claims that a verb cannot be a predicate by itself; it requires its agent (subject) to form a meaningful report.
2
Recognize the grammatical perspective
The author responds that this is the view of grammarians (النحاة). They require a pronoun in the predicate referring back to the subject (or something that substitutes) for syntactic linking (الربط). For grammarians, the verb alone is syntactically incomplete.
The pronoun is not new information but a grammatical necessity.
3
Recognize the logical perspective
Logicians (المناطقة) consider the predicate to be the verb itself because they attend to meanings. The meaning of the verb is what is affirmed of the subject. For example, in 'Zayd stands', 'stands' alone denotes the act of standing attributed to Zayd.
The logician does not require a separate pronoun because the verb's meaning already implies its relation to the subject.
4
Synthesize the two views
Both perspectives are valid but operate on different levels: grammar deals with verbal forms and syntactic rules; logic deals with concepts and their relations. The verb's independence in logic is defined by its meaning, not by syntactic completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنْ قُلْتَ: الفعلُ لا يخبرُ بِهِ وحدَهُ بل مع فاعله ; قُلْتُ : هذا مذهبُ النُّحاة; لأنَّهم ينظرونَ لِلألفاظ، فَلَا بُدَّ من ضمير في الخبر إذا كان فعلاً يعود على المبتدأ ; لأجل ربط الكلام، أو ما يقومُ مقامَ الضَّميرِ، وأمَّا المناطقةُ; فالخبر عندَهم نَفْسُ الفعل ; لأنَّهم إنَّما يلتفتونَ للمعاني، والمثبتُ للمبتدأ هو معنى الفعل، تأمل.
If you say: The verb is not reported by itself but with its agent; I say: This is the doctrine of the grammarians, because they consider the wording. So there must be a pronoun in the predicate if it is a verb referring back to the subject, for the sake of linking the speech, or something that takes the place of the pronoun. As for the logicians, the predicate with them is the verb itself, because they only attend to meanings, and what is affirmed for the subject is the meaning of the verb. Reflect.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the classification of the verb as an independent term (mustaqill). The objector argues that a verb cannot be used alone for predication; it requires its agent (fa'il) to form a meaningful sentence. For example, 'Zayd stands' requires both the subject and the verb. The response distinguishes between the perspective of grammarians (nahah) and logicians (mantiqiyyun). Grammarians focus on the wording (alfaz): they require a pronoun in the predicate that refers back to the subject to create a proper syntactic link. Thus, in 'Zayd stands', the verb 'stands' is considered together with the subject. Logicians, however, focus on meanings (ma'ani). For them, the predicate is the verb itself, because the meaning of the verb is what is affirmed of the subject. In 'Zayd is standing', the standing is predicated of Zayd, and the verb alone carries that meaning. The marginal note further clarifies that the pronoun (the agent) in the grammatical analysis is essentially the subject itself, not new information. Therefore, there is no real dispute: both perspectives are valid but operate on different levels. The lesson highlights that independence in predication is defined by meaning, not by syntactic completeness.

Key Terms
فعل
النحاة
المناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because they attend to meanings, and the verb's meaning is what is affirmed of the subject.
D) Because the verb is always in the past tense.

Key Takeaway
A verb can be a logically independent predicate because its meaning alone is sufficient for predication, even if grammar requires a separate pronoun for syntactic completeness.

Restricting Logical Rules for Universal Concepts
Author discusses the application of the equality rule to universal concepts like 'thing' and 'possible' and restricts the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a logical rule must be restricted for universal concepts and articulate the justification for such restriction.

Core Idea
Universal concepts like 'thing' break normal logical rules — so we restrict the rule to avoid paradox.

In logic, we often assume rules apply universally. But what about concepts like 'thing' that apply to everything? Al-Attar shows that the rule about contradictories fails for such concepts, so the claim must be restricted. This lesson unpacks that move and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal concepts
The passage mentions 'thing' (al-shay') and 'possible general' (al-mumkin al-'āmm). Recognize that these concepts apply to every concept (shāmila), meaning they are universal in scope.
In logic, a universal concept is one that is predicated of everything.
2
Understand why the rule fails
Since 'thing' and 'possible' apply to all concepts, their contradictories 'not-thing' and 'not-possible' cannot apply to any concept. The usual rule that every concept's contradictory applies to something is false here.
This is because the universal concept's contradictory would have to apply to something that is not a concept, which is impossible.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The passage presents a conditional: 'If not every not-thing is not possible, then some not-thing is not not-possible, so some not-thing would be possible.' This shows that denying the original claim leads to an absurdity (that something impossible is possible).
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite of the desired conclusion leads to a contradiction.
4
Analyze the restriction (takhṣīṣ al-da'wā)
The response is to restrict the claim to non-universal concepts. For universal concepts, their contradictories never apply, so the rule does not hold. The proof is then completed for all other concepts.
Takhṣīṣ al-da'wā means 'restricting the claim' to a subset of cases.
5
Justify the restriction
Rational rules are generalized according to purposes (bi-ḥasab al-maqāṣid). Since the philosophical sciences (ʿulūm ḥikmiyya) never use propositions with subjects or predicates that are contradictories of universal concepts, there is no harm in excluding such cases from logical rules.
Logic is an instrument (āla) for the sciences; it does not need to cover irrelevant cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار الشَّيء والممكن العام، فإنَّ الشَّيء والممكن العام لَمَّا وَجَبَ صدقُهُما على كُلِّ مفهوم بحسب نفس الأمر ; امتنعَ صِدْقُ اللاشيء واللَّامُمَّكن بحسبها على مفهوم مِنَ المفهومات، فإذا قُلْتَ : لَو لم يصدُقُ كُلُّ لَا شيءٍ لا ممكن؛ لَصَدَقَ بعضُ اللاشيء ليسَ بِلا ممكن، فيكون بعضُ اللاشيء ممكناً ; اتَّجَة المنع المذكور، وأُجيب بتخصيص الدعوى بغير نقائض المفهوماتِ الشاملة، فإنَّ نقائض غيرها يصدقُ لَا محالة على شيء ما ; ويتم البرهان. لا يُقالُ : يلزم تخصيص القواعد العقلية; لأنا نقولُ : تَعميمُها إِنَّما هو بحسب المقاصدِ، وليس لنا زيادة غرض في معرفة أحوال نقائض الأمور العامة; إذْ ليس في العُلوم الحكميَّةِ قضيَّةٌ موضوعها أو محمولها نقيضُ الأمور الشاملة، وهذا الفن آلة لتلك العلوم، فلا بأس بإخراجها عن قواعده،
Al-Attar: The thing and the possible general, for since the thing and the possible general necessarily apply to every concept according to the thing in itself, the application of not-thing and not-possible to any concept is impossible. So if you say: If not every not-thing is not possible, then some not-thing is not not-possible, so some not-thing would be possible. The mentioned prevention follows, and it was answered by restricting the claim to other than the contradictories of all-inclusive concepts, for the contradictories of others necessarily apply to something. And the proof is completed. It cannot be said: This requires restricting rational rules; for we say: Their generalization is only according to purposes, and we have no further objective in knowing the states of contradictories of general matters; since there is in the philosophical sciences no proposition whose subject or predicate is a contradictory of all-inclusive concepts, and this art is an instrument for those sciences, so there is no harm in excluding it from its rules.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage addresses a problem that arises when applying the previous rule about equality and contradictories to all-inclusive concepts like 'thing' and 'possible general'. Since 'thing' and 'possible' apply to everything, their contradictories 'not-thing' and 'not-possible' cannot apply to anything. The commentator al-Attar explains that if you suppose that not every 'not-thing' is 'not possible' (i.e., some not-thing is not not-possible), then some not-thing would be possible, which is false. The objection is that the original rule about contradictories would then fail for these universal concepts. The response is to restrict the claim to non-universal concepts; for universal concepts, their contradictories never apply, so the rule does not hold. It is also argued that this restriction does not violate rational rules because these rules are only generalized according to the purposes of logic, which serves the philosophical sciences. Since those sciences never use propositions with subjects or predicates that are contradictories of universal concepts, there is no harm in excluding such cases from the logical rules.

Key Terms
الشيء والممكن العام
نقائض المفهومات الشاملة
تخصيص الدعوى

Key Takeaway
For universal concepts, logical rules may need to be restricted to avoid paradox, and such restriction is justified by the practical purpose of logic as an instrument for the sciences.

Distinguishing modified-predicate from negative-predicate propositions
Author classifies 'some not-human is not non-rational' via double negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a modified-predicate proposition from a negative-predicate proposition and apply double negation to determine its underlying affirmative force.

Core Idea
A double-negated predicate reveals the hidden affirmative force of a seemingly negative proposition.

In this passage, al-Dawwani and later commentators analyze a tricky proposition: 'some not-human is not non-rational'. Is this a simple negative or does it imply an affirmative? The answer hinges on whether the predicate is modified (negation embedded in the term) or merely negated (negation of the copula). By tracing the double negation, we uncover that the proposition actually affirms something about some non-humans. This lesson will equip you to perform the same analysis on any categorical proposition and correctly classify its logical type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject, predicate, and negation operators
In the proposition 'some not-human is not non-rational', the subject is 'not-human' (a modified subject because it includes 'not'), the copula is 'is not' (negative copula), and the predicate is 'non-rational' (which contains 'non-' as a negation). There are three negation operations: one in the subject, one in the copula, and one in the predicate.
The term 'non-rational' is itself a negation of 'rational'.
2
Determine if the predicate is modified or simply negated
A modified predicate has the negation as part of the term (e.g., 'non-rational' is a single term). A negative predicate has the negation on the copula (e.g., 'is not rational'). Here, 'is not non-rational' has 'not' on the copula and 'non-' in the predicate term. So the predicate term 'non-rational' is modified, but the copula 'is not' makes the proposition a negative-predicate proposition? Actually, al-Dawwani argues that the 'not' in 'non-rational' is for negation, not modification, so 'non-rational' means 'not rational'. Then the copula 'is not' attaches to that negative term, yielding double negation.
Deciding whether the internal negation is integral to the predicate term or separate affects the classification.
3
Apply the principle 'negation of negation is affirmation'
When 'not' (the copula) is combined with 'non-rational' (a negation), the two negations cancel out, resulting in an affirmation. So 'some not-human is not non-rational' is equivalent to 'some not-human is rational'. This is the core logical move in the passage.
This principle is valid in classical logic.
4
Compare the logical force: negative-predicate vs affirmative
A negative-predicate proposition (e.g., 'some S is not P') is true even if the subject does not exist (it does not require the subject to be instantiated). An affirmative proposition (e.g., 'some S is P') requires the existence of the subject. By showing that the double negation yields an affirmative, al-Dawwani proves that the original proposition is logically equivalent to an affirmative, thus it has the same existential import.
This distinction is important for evaluating the truth of propositions in contexts like kalam where the existence of subjects may be debated.
5
Classify the proposition definitively
According to al-Dawwani, the proposition is not truly modified-predicate because the internal negation is separable; it is a negative-predicate proposition that, after the double negation analysis, functions as an affirmative. This classification resolves the apparent confusion.
The passage also notes that later logicians invented the term 'negative-predicate proposition', but the idea existed earlier.

شيخنا . العطار العالية بل اعتبارُها يوجب اختلالاً، وأجابَ الدَّوَّاني بجواب آخَرَ، وهو أنَّ القضيَّةَ المذكورة لَيسَتْ مَعدولة المحمول ; بَلْ سالبه المحمول، والموجبة السالبة المحمول في قوَّة السالبة، فتصدقُ بانتفاء الموضوع فتكون سالبة السالبة المحمول في قوَّةِ الموجبة ومُستلزمةً لَهَا .ا.هـ. وأرادَ بالقضيَّةِ المذكورة عكس الدعوى، وهي الجزئية المعدولةُ المحمول القائلةُ : بعضُ اللاإنسانِ ليسَ بِلا ناطق، وتوضيحهُ كَمَا في حاشيةِ الشّيرانسي أنَّ لا في لَا) ناطق ; ليسَتْ لِلعدول بلْ هِي لِلسَّلْبِ، فمعنى اللأناطقِ ليس بناطقِ، فالقضيَّةُ المذكورة بدونِ اعتبار دخول ليس على اللاناطقِ سالبة المحمول، وهِيَ في قوَّةِ السَّالبة في عدمِ اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع وصدقها بانتفاء الموضوع، فبعد اعتبار دخول ليس على اللاناطقِ تكونُ القضيَّةُ في قوَّةِ الموجبة في اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع ومُستلزمةٌ لَهَا ; لأنَّهُ لَمَّا كانَ اللأناطقُ سَلباً ثم دخل عليهِ حرفُ السَّلْبِ، أعني ليس في قولنا : بعضُ اللاإنسانِ «ليسَ بِلا ناطق; أفادَ إيجابُ النَّاطِقِ لبعض اللا إنسانِ، فإِنَّ سَلْبَ السَّلْبِ إيجاب ا.هـ. قال عبد الحكيم: إنَّ القضيَّةَ السَّالبة المحمول اخترعها المتأخُرُونَ مَعَ أَنَّ مباحث هذهِ النِّسبِ مذكورة في كلام المتقدمين . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مِنْ جَانِبِ لا يخفَى أَنَّ التَّصادِقَ الكُلِّيَّ مِن جانبٍ يُغايرُ التَّصادِقَ الكُلَّيَّ مِنَ الجانبين ولا يُنافيه، والقيودُ إنَّما تخرج ما ينافيها لا مَا يُغايرُها ، فلا يترتب حينئذ قوله : (فأعم وأخص مطلقاً، فكانَ عليهِ أنْ يزيد لفظَةَ : فقط، وكأنَّهُ اعتمد في فَهم ذلك على كلمة «أو» ; القاضية على ما هو المتبادر منها يتنافي ما قبلها وما بعدها ; قالَهُ البعضُ .
Our teacher al-Attar: Rather, considering it causes disruption. Al-Dawwani answered with another answer: that the mentioned proposition is not modified in predicate, but rather negative in predicate. And the affirmative with negative predicate is in force of a negative, so it is true by the non-existence of the subject, so it becomes a negative with negative predicate in force of an affirmative and implying it. End. He intended by the mentioned proposition the converse of the claim, which is the particular modified-predicate proposition saying: Some not-human is not non-rational. Its clarification is as in the gloss of al-Shiransi: that 'not' in 'non-rational' is not for modification but for negation, so the meaning of 'non-rational' is 'not rational'. So the mentioned proposition without considering the entry of 'not' onto 'non-rational' is a negative-predicate proposition, and it is in force of a negative in not requiring the existence of the subject and being true by the non-existence of the subject. After considering the entry of 'not' onto 'non-rational', the proposition becomes in force of an affirmative in requiring the existence of the subject and implying it; because since 'non-rational' is a negation, then the negative particle, that is 'not' in our saying 'some not-human is not non-rational', entered upon it, conveying affirmation of 'rational' for some not-human, for negation of a negation is affirmation. End. 'Abd al-Hakim said: The negative-predicate proposition was invented by later scholars, while the discussions of these relations are mentioned in the words of the earlier scholars. His saying: 'Or from one side' - it is not hidden that complete mutual application from one side is different from complete mutual application from both sides and does not contradict it. Conditions only exclude what contradicts them, not what differs from them. So then his statement (thus general and specific absolute) does not follow. He should have added the word 'only'. It seems he relied in understanding that on the word 'or', which according to its apparent meaning entails contradiction between what is before and after it. Some said so.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific proposition: 'Some not-human is not non-rational'. Al-Dawwani argues that this proposition is not truly a 'modified-predicate' proposition (ma'dulat al-mahmul) but rather a 'negative-predicate' proposition (salibat al-mahmul). He explains that if we treat it as negative-predicate, it is in force of a simple negative and true when the subject does not exist. However, when we interpret the 'not' in 'non-rational' as a negation, the double negation results in an affirmation: 'some not-human is rational'. The commentator cites al-Shiransi to clarify that 'non-rational' is a negation of 'rational', so 'is not non-rational' becomes a negation of a negation, which is equivalent to an affirmation. The passage then shifts to a note on the classification from the previous lesson: 'or from one side' is not contradictory to 'from both sides', but merely different. The author criticizes that the phrase 'general and specific absolute' should be qualified with 'only' to avoid confusion, since 'or' often implies contradiction.

Key Terms
قضية معدولة المحمول
قضية سالبة المحمول
سلب السلب إيجاب
تصادق كلي

Checkpoint
According to al-Dawwani, why is the proposition 'some not-human is not non-rational' not considered a true modified-predicate proposition?
A) Because the subject is modified, not the predicate.
B) Because the 'not' in 'non-rational' is a separate negation, not part of the predicate term.
C) Because the proposition is particular, not universal.
D) Because the copula is affirmative.

Key Takeaway
A proposition with a double negation (e.g., 'is not non-P') is logically equivalent to an affirmative, and careful parsing of where the negation resides determines its classification as modified-predicate or negative-predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the proposition 'Some intoxicant is not non-haram' (meaning 'some intoxicant is not forbidden'). Determine whether it is a modified-predicate or negative-predicate proposition, and rewrite it as an affirmative proposition using double negation where applicable.
?

The Precise Condition for Absolute General-Specific
Commentator critiques matn's wording for the condition of absolute general-specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the precise condition for two terms to be absolutely general-specific (aʿamm wa aḫaṣ mutlaq) and explain why the matn should include 'only' (faqaṭ) to avoid ambiguity.

Core Idea
One-sided co-extensiveness alone does not guarantee absolute general-specific; exclusivity requires the word 'only'.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement made by commentators on the matn's formulation of the condition for two terms to be absolutely general-specific. The matn states that if two universals are co-extensive from one side, they are absolutely general and absolutely specific. However, the commentator argues that this wording is ambiguous: the phrase 'from one side' does not preclude the possibility of also being co-extensive from the other side, which would make them equal. Therefore, the matn should have added 'only' (faqaṭ) to make the condition exclusive. We will dissect this logical nuance and understand how restrictions work in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations
The matn discusses two cases: co-extensiveness from both sides (equality) and from one side (general-specific). Note how 'or' (aw) is used to connect them.
The word 'or' often signals a contrast, but can also join non-exclusive alternatives.
2
Recognize the ambiguity
The commentator points out that 'from one side' does not contradict 'from both sides'; it merely differs. So stating 'from one side' as a condition does not exclude the possibility that there might also be co-extensiveness from the other side.
This ambiguity arises because the condition is not explicitly exclusive.
3
Understand the role of restrictions
In definitions, restrictions are meant to exclude what is incompatible, not what is merely different. Since one-sided co-extensiveness is not incompatible with two-sided co-extensiveness, the condition alone does not guarantee that the terms are not equal.
If a condition does not exclude a conflicting case, it is not a proper restriction.
4
Apply the correction
The commentator suggests adding 'only' (faqaṭ) to make the condition exclusive. Thus, the condition becomes: co-extensiveness from one side only, not from both. This ensures the terms are not equal.
This illustrates a general principle: ambiguous wording can lead to incorrect classifications.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات أَوْ مِنْ جَانِبِ عطفٌ على قوله : (من الجانبين)؛ أي: إن تصادقا تصادقاً كُلِّيَّا من الجانبين ؛ فهما متساويان كما مرَّ . وإن تصادقا تصادقاً كليا من جانب واحد، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنْ تَصَادَقَا تَصَادُقَاً كُلِّيَّا مِنْ جَانِبِ وَاحِدٍ) أي: وهو العام فقط. اهـ. شيخنا . العطار العالية بل اعتبارُها يوجب اختلالاً، وأجابَ الدَّوَّاني بجواب آخَرَ، وهو أنَّ القضيَّةَ المذكورة لَيسَتْ مَعدولة المحمول ؛ بَلْ سالبه المحمول، والموجبة السالبة المحمول في قوَّة السالبة، فتصدقُ بانتفاء الموضوع فتكون سالبة السالبة المحمول في قوَّةِ الموجبة ومُستلزمةً لَهَا .ا.هـ. وأرادَ بالقضيَّةِ المذكورة عكس الدعوى، وهي الجزئية المعدولةُ المحمول القائلةُ : بعضُ اللاإنسانِ ليسَ بِلا ناطق، وتوضيحهُ كَمَا في حاشيةِ الشّيرانسي أنَّ لا في لَا) ناطق ؛ ليسَتْ لِلعدول بلْ هِي لِلسَّلْبِ، فمعنى اللأناطقِ ليس بناطقِ، فالقضيَّةُ المذكورة بدونِ اعتبار دخول ليس على اللاناطقِ سالبة المحمول، وهِيَ في قوَّةِ السَّالبة في عدمِ اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع وصدقها بانتفاء الموضوع، فبعد اعتبار دخول ليس على اللاناطقِ تكونُ القضيَّةُ في قوَّةِ الموجبة في اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع ومُستلزمةٌ لَهَا ؛ لأنَّهُ لَمَّا كانَ اللأناطقُ سَلباً ثم دخل عليهِ حرفُ السَّلْبِ، أعني ليس في قولنا : بعضُ اللاإنسانِ «ليسَ بِلا ناطق؛ أفادَ إيجابُ النَّاطِقِ لبعض اللا إنسانِ، فإِنَّ سَلْبَ السَّلْبِ إيجاب ا.هـ. قال عبد الحكيم: إنَّ القضيَّةَ السَّالبة المحمول اخترعها المتأخُرُونَ مَعَ أَنَّ مباحث هذهِ النِّسبِ مذكورة في كلام المتقدمين . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مِنْ جَانِبِ لا يخفَى أَنَّ التَّصادِقَ الكُلِّيَّ مِن جانبٍ يُغايرُ التَّصادِقَ الكُلَّيَّ مِنَ الجانبين ولا يُنافيه، والقيودُ إنَّما تخرج ما ينافيها لا مَا يُغايرُها ، فلا يترتب حينئذ قوله : (فأعم وأخص مطلقاً، فكانَ عليهِ أنْ يزيد لفظَةَ : فقط، وكأنَّهُ اعتمد في فَهم ذلك على كلمة «أو» ؛ القاضية على ما هو المتبادر منها يتنافي ما قبلها وما بعدها ؛ قالَهُ البعضُ .
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts. 'Or from one side' is a conjunction to his statement: (from both sides). That is: If they are co-extensive universally from both sides, then they are equal, as previously. And if they are co-extensive universally from one side. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'And if they are co-extensive universally from one side') meaning: and that one is the general only. End. Our Shaykh. Al-ʿAṭṭār al-ʿĀliyya: Rather, considering it causes a defect. And al-Dawwani answered with another answer: that the mentioned proposition is not a privative of the predicate (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl), but rather a negative of the predicate (salibat al-maḥmūl). And the affirmative negative of the predicate is in the power of the negative, so it is true by the non-existence of the subject, so it becomes a negative of the negative of the predicate in the power of the affirmative and entailing it. He intended by the mentioned proposition the converse of the claim, which is the particular privative of the predicate saying: 'Some non-human is not non-speaking'. And its clarification as in the gloss of al-Shiransi: that 'lā' in 'lā nāṭiq' is not for privation but for negation. So the meaning of 'lā nāṭiq' is 'not speaking'. So the mentioned proposition without considering the entry of 'not' upon 'lā nāṭiq' is a negative of the predicate, and it is in the power of the negative in not requiring the existence of the subject and its truth by the non-existence of the subject. After considering the entry of 'not' upon 'lā nāṭiq', the proposition becomes in the power of the affirmative in requiring the existence of the subject and entailing it, because since 'lā nāṭiq' is a negation, then the negative particle entered it, namely 'not' in our statement 'Some non-human is not non-speaking'; it yields the affirmation of 'speaking' for some non-human, for the negation of negation is affirmation. End. ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm said: The negative of the predicate proposition was invented by the later scholars, although the discussions of these relations are mentioned in the speech of the earlier scholars. (His statement: 'Or from one side') It is not hidden that co-extensiveness from one side differs from co-extensiveness from both sides and does not contradict it. And restrictions only exclude what contradicts them, not what differs from them. So then his statement '(so absolutely general and absolutely specific)' does not follow. He should have added the word 'only'. And it is as if he relied for understanding that on the word 'or', which according to its apparent meaning indicates that what precedes and what follows are mutually exclusive. So said some.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) that critically examines a point in the main text about the condition for absolute general-specific. The main text (matn) states: if two terms are co-extensive (taṣāduq) universally from both sides, they are equal; if from one side, then one is general and the other specific. Al-Dasuqi comments that 'from one side' means the general only. Then a problem is raised: considering the condition 'from one side' causes a defect. Al-Dawwani responds by analyzing a particular proposition: 'Some non-human is not non-speaking' (baʿḍ al-lā-insān laysa bi-lā nāṭiq). The objection concerns whether this proposition is privative predicate (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl) or negative predicate (sālibat al-maḥmūl). Al-Dawwani argues it is negative predicate, not privative. In 'lā nāṭiq', the 'lā' is for negation, not for privation; thus 'lā nāṭiq' means 'not speaking'. When we say 'some non-human is not non-speaking', without the second negation, the proposition is a negative predicate (sālibat al-maḥmūl) and does not require the existence of the subject; it can be true even if 'non-human' is empty. But when we include the second 'not' (laysa), it negates the negation, resulting in an affirmation: some non-human is speaking. This double negation effectively makes the proposition entail the existence of the subject. Thus the objection is resolved. ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm notes that the concept of 'sālibat al-maḥmūl' was introduced by later logicians, though earlier scholars discussed similar relations. Finally, a point is made about the wording of the matn: the condition 'from one side' (min jānib) is different from 'from both sides', not contradictory, so the author's conclusion 'so absolute general and absolute specific' might not automatically follow; the author should have added 'only' (faqaṭ) to clarify that the one-sided co-extensiveness is the condition for absolute general-specific, and the word 'or' (aw) in the matn might have created the impression that the two cases are mutually exclusive.

Key Terms
تصادق كلي من جانب
سالبة المحمول
معدولة المحمول

B) Because 'from one side' does not exclude 'from both sides', so it fails to be an exclusive condition.
C) Because the matn was wrongly attributed.
D) Because the author intended 'from both sides'.

Key Takeaway
Logical conditions must be exclusive to avoid ambiguity; the word 'only' ensures the condition for absolute general-specific is properly restrictive.

Distinguishing Sālibat al-Maḥmūl from Maʿdūlat al-Maḥmūl
Al-Dawwani responds to an objection by distinguishing negative predicate from privative predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a negative predicate proposition (sālibat al-maḥmūl) and a privative predicate proposition (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl), and demonstrate how double negation transforms a negative predicate proposition into an affirmative that entails existence of the subject.

Core Idea
A double negation ('laysa bi-lā nāṭiq') turns a negative predicate into an affirmation, changing the existential import.

In this lesson, we analyze al-Dawwani's response to an objection concerning the proposition 'Some non-human is not non-speaking'. The objection claimed that this proposition is a privative predicate (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl) and therefore causes a defect in the condition. Al-Dawwani argues that it is actually a negative predicate (sālibat al-maḥmūl) because the 'lā' in 'lā nāṭiq' is a negation, not a privation. He shows that without the second negation, the proposition does not require existence, but with the second negation ('laysa'), it negates the negation, resulting in an affirmative that entails existence. This resolution saves the original condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition in question
The proposition is 'Some non-human is not non-speaking' (baʿḍ al-lā-insān laysa bi-lā nāṭiq). This appears to be a particular privative proposition, but its classification is disputed.
'Non-human' (lā-insān) is a privative subject, and 'non-speaking' (lā nāṭiq) is the predicate.
2
Determine the type of negation in the predicate
Al-Dawwani argues that the 'lā' in 'lā nāṭiq' is a negation (salb), not a privation (ʿadal). So 'lā nāṭiq' means 'not speaking', not 'non-speaking' as a privative term. This makes the predicate a negative predicate (sālibat al-maḥmūl).
A privative predicate would treat the negation as part of the term itself (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl).
3
Analyze the proposition without the second negation
If we consider the proposition without the outer negation (i.e., just 'some non-human is non-speaking'), it would be an affirmative with a privative predicate. But here, the outer 'laysa' (not) is present. Al-Dawwani first analyzes the inner part: 'some non-human is non-speaking' as a negative predicate: 'some non-human is not speaking' (since 'lā nāṭiq' = 'not speaking'). This is a negative predicate proposition, which does not require the existence of the subject; it can be true even if 'non-human' is empty.
Negative predicate propositions (sālibat al-maḥmūl) have the same force as simple negative propositions.
4
Analyze the proposition with the second negation
Now consider the full proposition: 'some non-human is not non-speaking'. The outer 'laysa' negates the negative predicate. This is a double negation: not (not speaking). The negation of negation yields affirmation: 'some non-human is speaking'. This affirmative proposition does require the existence of the subject (non-human).
Thus, the proposition shifts from not requiring existence to requiring it due to double negation.
5
Understand the effect on existential import
Al-Dawwani's analysis shows that the proposition is not a privative causing a defect; rather, it is a negative predicate that, under double negation, becomes an affirmative with existential import. This resolves the objection to the condition.
This illustrates the importance of distinguishing negation types in logical analysis.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات أَوْ مِنْ جَانِبِ عطفٌ على قوله : (من الجانبين)؛ أي: إن تصادقا تصادقاً كُلِّيَّا من الجانبين ؛ فهما متساويان كما مرَّ . وإن تصادقا تصادقاً كليا من جانب واحد، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنْ تَصَادَقَا تَصَادُقَاً كُلِّيَّا مِنْ جَانِبِ وَاحِدٍ) أي: وهو العام فقط. اهـ. شيخنا . العطار العالية بل اعتبارُها يوجب اختلالاً، وأجابَ الدَّوَّاني بجواب آخَرَ، وهو أنَّ القضيَّةَ المذكورة لَيسَتْ مَعدولة المحمول ؛ بَلْ سالبه المحمول، والموجبة السالبة المحمول في قوَّة السالبة، فتصدقُ بانتفاء الموضوع فتكون سالبة السالبة المحمول في قوَّةِ الموجبة ومُستلزمةً لَهَا .ا.هـ. وأرادَ بالقضيَّةِ المذكورة عكس الدعوى، وهي الجزئية المعدولةُ المحمول القائلةُ : بعضُ اللاإنسانِ ليسَ بِلا ناطق، وتوضيحهُ كَمَا في حاشيةِ الشّيرانسي أنَّ لا في لَا) ناطق ؛ ليسَتْ لِلعدول بلْ هِي لِلسَّلْبِ، فمعنى اللأناطقِ ليس بناطقِ، فالقضيَّةُ المذكورة بدونِ اعتبار دخول ليس على اللاناطقِ سالبة المحمول، وهِيَ في قوَّةِ السَّالبة في عدمِ اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع وصدقها بانتفاء الموضوع، فبعد اعتبار دخول ليس على اللاناطقِ تكونُ القضيَّةُ في قوَّةِ الموجبة في اقتضاء وجودِ الموضوع ومُستلزمةٌ لَهَا ؛ لأنَّهُ لَمَّا كانَ اللأناطقُ سَلباً ثم دخل عليهِ حرفُ السَّلْبِ، أعني ليس في قولنا : بعضُ اللاإنسانِ «ليسَ بِلا ناطق؛ أفادَ إيجابُ النَّاطِقِ لبعض اللا إنسانِ، فإِنَّ سَلْبَ السَّلْبِ إيجاب ا.هـ. قال عبد الحكيم: إنَّ القضيَّةَ السَّالبة المحمول اخترعها المتأخُرُونَ مَعَ أَنَّ مباحث هذهِ النِّسبِ مذكورة في كلام المتقدمين . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مِنْ جَانِبِ لا يخفَى أَنَّ التَّصادِقَ الكُلِّيَّ مِن جانبٍ يُغايرُ التَّصادِقَ الكُلَّيَّ مِنَ الجانبين ولا يُنافيه، والقيودُ إنَّما تخرج ما ينافيها لا مَا يُغايرُها ، فلا يترتب حينئذ قوله : (فأعم وأخص مطلقاً، فكانَ عليهِ أنْ يزيد لفظَةَ : فقط، وكأنَّهُ اعتمد في فَهم ذلك على كلمة «أو» ؛ القاضية على ما هو المتبادر منها يتنافي ما قبلها وما بعدها ؛ قالَهُ البعضُ .
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts. 'Or from one side' is a conjunction to his statement: (from both sides). That is: If they are co-extensive universally from both sides, then they are equal, as previously. And if they are co-extensive universally from one side. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'And if they are co-extensive universally from one side') meaning: and that one is the general only. End. Our Shaykh. Al-ʿAṭṭār al-ʿĀliyya: Rather, considering it causes a defect. And al-Dawwani answered with another answer: that the mentioned proposition is not a privative of the predicate (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl), but rather a negative of the predicate (salibat al-maḥmūl). And the affirmative negative of the predicate is in the power of the negative, so it is true by the non-existence of the subject, so it becomes a negative of the negative of the predicate in the power of the affirmative and entailing it. He intended by the mentioned proposition the converse of the claim, which is the particular privative of the predicate saying: 'Some non-human is not non-speaking'. And its clarification as in the gloss of al-Shiransi: that 'lā' in 'lā nāṭiq' is not for privation but for negation. So the meaning of 'lā nāṭiq' is 'not speaking'. So the mentioned proposition without considering the entry of 'not' upon 'lā nāṭiq' is a negative of the predicate, and it is in the power of the negative in not requiring the existence of the subject and its truth by the non-existence of the subject. After considering the entry of 'not' upon 'lā nāṭiq', the proposition becomes in the power of the affirmative in requiring the existence of the subject and entailing it, because since 'lā nāṭiq' is a negation, then the negative particle entered it, namely 'not' in our statement 'Some non-human is not non-speaking'; it yields the affirmation of 'speaking' for some non-human, for the negation of negation is affirmation. End. ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm said: The negative of the predicate proposition was invented by the later scholars, although the discussions of these relations are mentioned in the speech of the earlier scholars. (His statement: 'Or from one side') It is not hidden that co-extensiveness from one side differs from co-extensiveness from both sides and does not contradict it. And restrictions only exclude what contradicts them, not what differs from them. So then his statement '(so absolutely general and absolutely specific)' does not follow. He should have added the word 'only'. And it is as if he relied for understanding that on the word 'or', which according to its apparent meaning indicates that what precedes and what follows are mutually exclusive. So said some.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) that critically examines a point in the main text about the condition for absolute general-specific. The main text (matn) states: if two terms are co-extensive (taṣāduq) universally from both sides, they are equal; if from one side, then one is general and the other specific. Al-Dasuqi comments that 'from one side' means the general only. Then a problem is raised: considering the condition 'from one side' causes a defect. Al-Dawwani responds by analyzing a particular proposition: 'Some non-human is not non-speaking' (baʿḍ al-lā-insān laysa bi-lā nāṭiq). The objection concerns whether this proposition is privative predicate (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl) or negative predicate (sālibat al-maḥmūl). Al-Dawwani argues it is negative predicate, not privative. In 'lā nāṭiq', the 'lā' is for negation, not for privation; thus 'lā nāṭiq' means 'not speaking'. When we say 'some non-human is not non-speaking', without the second negation, the proposition is a negative predicate (sālibat al-maḥmūl) and does not require the existence of the subject; it can be true even if 'non-human' is empty. But when we include the second 'not' (laysa), it negates the negation, resulting in an affirmation: some non-human is speaking. This double negation effectively makes the proposition entail the existence of the subject. Thus the objection is resolved. ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm notes that the concept of 'sālibat al-maḥmūl' was introduced by later logicians, though earlier scholars discussed similar relations. Finally, a point is made about the wording of the matn: the condition 'from one side' (min jānib) is different from 'from both sides', not contradictory, so the author's conclusion 'so absolute general and absolute specific' might not automatically follow; the author should have added 'only' (faqaṭ) to clarify that the one-sided co-extensiveness is the condition for absolute general-specific, and the word 'or' (aw) in the matn might have created the impression that the two cases are mutually exclusive.

معدولة المحمول
سلب السلب إيجاب

A) It is a privative of the predicate (maʿdūlat al-maḥmūl).
B) It is a negative of the predicate (sālibat al-maḥmūl) that, with double negation, becomes an affirmative entailing existence.
C) It is a universal negative.
D) It is a tautology.

Key Takeaway
Distinguishing between negative and privative predicates is essential for understanding existential import in logical propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Propositions
beginner
5 min
The Definition of a Proposition (Qadiyya)
Author defines a proposition as a statement admitting truth and falsity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of a proposition (qadiyya) as a statement that admits truth and falsity, and distinguish it from other types of utterances.

Core Idea
A proposition is defined by its own capacity for truth and falsity, not by the speaker's relation.

The author defines the proposition as 'a statement that admits truth and falsity'. This definition is carefully chosen to avoid defining it by its relation to the speaker, and to exclude utterances that are not genuine reports, such as those of madmen and sleepers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined, which is 'al-qadiyya' (القضية).
2
Extract the definition components
The definition has two parts: the genus, which is 'statement' (قول), and the differentia, which is 'admits truth and falsity' (يحتمل الصدق والكذب).
3
Contrast with an alternative definition
The commentator mentions an alternative definition: 'a statement it is correct to say of its utterer that he is truthful or lying'. The chosen definition is superior because it defines the proposition by its own property, not by the state of its relatum (the speaker).
4
Apply the exclusion condition
The definition excludes utterances of madmen and sleepers because they are not considered reports (khabar) or constructions (insha'), even if accidentally true. Their speech is like melodies of birds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[تعريف القضيّةِ] (القَضِيَّةُ : قَوْلٌ يَحتمل الصّدْقَ وَالكَذِبَ) الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : الصِّدْقَ . . . إِلَخ) المراد بالصّدقِ : مُطابقةُ النِّسبة الكلامية؛ إيجابيَّة أو سلبية للواقع، والكذب: عدم مطابقتها له المطار (قَوْلُهُ : الْقَضِيَّةُ) فعيلة بمعنى مفعولة، سُمِّيَتْ بِذلِكَ لاشتمالها على الحكم الذي يُسمَّى قضاء، قال تعالى: ﴿وَقَضَى رَبُّكَ أَلَّا تَعْبُدُوا إِلَّا إِيَّاهُ ﴾ [الإسراء: ۲۳]، وقال الشَّاعِرُ : قَضَى الله يا أسماء أنْ لَسْتُ زائِلاً أُحبُّكِ حتَّى يُعْمِضَ العينَ مُغْمِضُ وقد يُطلقُ القضاء على أداءِ الدَّيْنِ؛ قالَ الشَّاعِرُ : قَضَى كُلُّ ذِي دَيْنِ فَوفَّى غريمه وعزةٌ مَمطولٌ مُعنَّى غريمُهَا وأخذُ القضيَّةِ مِن هذا مُستَبعَدٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : قَوْلُ يَحْتَمِلُ الصَّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ) عدل عن قول الأصل: إنَّها قول يصح أن يُقالَ لِقائِلِهِ : إِنَّهُ صادقٌ فيه أو كاذب؛ لِسلامة مَا هَهُنا عما أورد عليه بأنَّهُ تعريف للشَّيء بحال متعلّقه، وما هنا : تعريف له بحال نفسه؛ قاله عبد الحكيم، ولم يقل في التّعريف : قولٌ يُقالُ ... إلخ؛ إذْ لا يلزمُ في القضيَّةِ أنْ يُقالَ بالفعل لقائله : إِنَّهُ صادق فيه أو كاذب، ولم يقل : قول قائله صادق أو كاذب؛ ليخرج قول المجنون والنائم، زيدٌ قائم، فإنَّ كُلا منهما، وإنْ كانَ في نفس الأمر صادقاً في كلامه أو كاذباً؛ إِلَّا أَنَّه لا يُقالُ لهما إنَّهُ صادق أو كاذب فى العرفِ؛ لأنَّ كَلامَهُما مُلحق بألحان الطُّيور؛ ليسَ بخبرٍ وَلَا إنشاء، نصَّ عليه في التَّلويح .ا.هـ. وأورد على التَّعريف المذكور لزوم الدَّورِ؛ لأنَّ الصِّدقَ مُطابقةُ الخبر للواقع، والكذب عدمُ مُطابقتِهِ له، والقضيَّةُ مُرادفةٌ للخبر، وأجيب بأجوبة منها: أنَّ الصدق والكذب بديهيَّانِ، ولو سلم أَنَّهما نظريَّانِ؛ فيجوزُ أنْ يعرف الخبرُ تَعريفاً تنبيهيًا بالصِّدقِ المعرَّفِ بالخبر تعريفاً كسبيا؛ اللازم منهُ توقُفُ الخبر عند المدركة على حصولِ الصِّدقِ المتوقفِ على حصول الخبر ابتداء.
[Definition of the proposition] (The proposition is a statement that admits truth and falsity) Dusooqi: His statement: 'truth... etc.' the intended meaning of truth is: conformity of the verbal relation, affirmative or negative, with reality; and falsity is non-conformity with it. Al-‘Attar: His statement: 'the proposition' is fa‘īla in the sense of maf‘ūla, named thus because it includes the judgment which is called qaḍā'. Allah said: 'And your Lord has decreed [qaḍā] that you worship none but Him' (Qur’an 17:23). And the poet said: 'Allah decreed, O Asma, that I shall not cease to love you...' And qaḍā' is also used for the payment of a debt; the poet said: 'Every debtor repaid and fulfilled his due, but ‘Azza’s debtor is delayed and tormented.' Deriving 'proposition' from this is far-fetched. His statement: 'a statement that admits truth and falsity' is a modification from the original text's: 'a statement which it is correct to say of its utterer that he is truthful in it or lying' to avoid the objection that it defines the thing by the state of its relatum; here it defines it by its own state. This was stated by ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm. He did not say in the definition: 'a statement which it is said...' etc. because it is not necessary for a proposition that it is actually said of its utterer that he is truthful or lying. And he did not say: 'a statement whose utterer is truthful or lying' to exclude the speech of a madman or a sleeper, e.g. 'Zayd is standing', because for each of them, even if in reality he is truthful or lying in his speech, it is not said in convention that he is truthful or lying; for their speech is like the melodies of birds, not a report (khabar) nor a construction (inshā'), as stated in al-Talwīḥ. An objection was raised against the aforementioned definition that it entails circularity, because truth is conformity of a report (khabar) with reality, and falsity is non-conformity, and proposition is synonymous with report. It was answered with several answers, among them: that truth and falsity are self-evident (badīhiyyān); and if they were theoretical, it would still be permissible to define report by a reminder definition (ta‘rīf tanbīhī) using truth defined by report with a acquired definition, leading to a dependence of the report on obtaining truth which depends on obtaining the report initially.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) as a statement that admits truth and falsity. The commentator first explains the linguistic derivation: the word qadiyya comes from qada', meaning judgment, as in the Qur'anic verse 'Your Lord has decreed...'. He then contrasts this definition with an alternative that defines it as 'a statement it is correct to say of its utterer that he is truthful or lying in it'. The commentator points out that the chosen definition is superior because it defines the proposition by its own property (admitting truth/falsity) rather than by its relation to its speaker. This avoids the objection that the alternative defines a thing by the state of its relatum. Additionally, the definition excludes utterances of madmen and sleepers, which are not considered speech acts (khabar or insha'), even if they accidentally correspond to reality. Finally, the passage addresses a circularity objection: truth is defined as conformity of a report with reality, but a proposition is a report. The commentator replies that truth and falsity are self-evident concepts, so no circularity arises; even if they were theoretical, a 'reminder definition' would be acceptable where the definition is initially based on ordinary understanding. This passage is crucial for understanding the nature of propositions and the careful linguistic and logical distinctions made in Islamic logic.

Key Terms

الصدق
الكذب
الخبر

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator prefer the definition 'a statement that admits truth and falsity' over 'a statement it is correct to say of its utterer that he is truthful or lying'?
A) Because the first definition is shorter.
B) Because the second defines the proposition by the state of its relatum, not by its own state.
C) Because the first definition excludes commands and questions.
D) Because the second definition applies only to speech in court.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is defined by its own capacity for truth and falsity, independent of the speaker's state, and excludes utterances not intended as informative reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement 'Allah exists' uttered by a pious believer. Does it meet the definition of a proposition? Explain using the definition given.
?
Identify which of the following is a proposition according to the author: a) 'Close the door!' b) 'The sun is shining' c) 'A dream I had last night' (uttered by a sleeper during sleep). Give reasons.

Propositions
intermediate
5 min
The Circularity Objection and Its Response
Author addresses circularity objection against definition of proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the circularity objection against the definition of proposition and evaluate the response that truth and falsity are self-evident.

Core Idea
The definition of proposition avoids circularity because truth and falsity are self-evident concepts.

Some object that defining a proposition as admitting truth and falsity is circular, since truth is defined as conformity of a report with reality. The author responds that truth and falsity are self-evident, or a reminder definition suffices, thus preserving the definition's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection is that the definition is circular: truth is defined as conformity of a report (khabar) with reality, but a proposition is synonymous with report. So the definition uses truth, which depends on report, which is the same as proposition.
2
Find the response
The answer given is that truth and falsity are self-evident (badīhiyyān), so no circularity arises. Alternatively, even if they were theoretical, a 'reminder definition' (ta‘rīf tanbīhī) would be acceptable, where the definition is based on prior ordinary understanding.
3
Evaluate the response
Think about whether calling truth self-evident resolves the logical dependence. In Islamic logic, foundational concepts are accepted as primitive. The response shows that the definition does not need to be circular because the definiens is directly understood.

[تعريف القضيّةِ] (القَضِيَّةُ : قَوْلٌ يَحتمل الصّدْقَ وَالكَذِبَ) الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : الصِّدْقَ . . . إِلَخ) المراد بالصّدقِ : مُطابقةُ النِّسبة الكلامية؛ إيجابيَّة أو سلبية للواقع، والكذب: عدم مطابقتها له المطار (قَوْلُهُ : الْقَضِيَّةُ) فعيلة بمعنى مفعولة، سُمِّيَتْ بِذلِكَ لاشتمالها على الحكم الذي يُسمَّى قضاء، قال تعالى: ﴿وَقَضَى رَبُّكَ أَلَّا تَعْبُدُوا إِلَّا إِيَّاهُ ﴾ [الإسراء: ۲۳]، وقال الشَّاعِرُ : قَضَى الله يا أسماء أنْ لَسْتُ زائِلاً أُحبُّكِ حتَّى يُعْمِضَ العينَ مُغْمِضُ وقد يُطلقُ القضاء على أداءِ الدَّيْنِ؛ قالَ الشَّاعِرُ : قَضَى كُلُّ ذِي دَيْنِ فَوفَّى غريمه وعزةٌ مَمطولٌ مُعنَّى غريمُهَا وأخذُ القضيَّةِ مِن هذا مُستَبعَدٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : قَوْلُ يَحْتَمِلُ الصَّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ) عدل عن قول الأصل: إنَّها قول يصح أن يُقالَ لِقائِلِهِ : إِنَّهُ صادقٌ فيه أو كاذب؛ لِسلامة مَا هَهُنا عما أورد عليه بأنَّهُ تعريف للشَّيء بحال متعلّقه، وما هنا : تعريف له بحال نفسه؛ قاله عبد الحكيم، ولم يقل في التّعريف : قولٌ يُقالُ ... إلخ؛ إذْ لا يلزمُ في القضيَّةِ أنْ يُقالَ بالفعل لقائله : إِنَّهُ صادق فيه أو كاذب، ولم يقل : قول قائله صادق أو كاذب؛ ليخرج قول المجنون والنائم، زيدٌ قائم، فإنَّ كُلا منهما، وإنْ كانَ في نفس الأمر صادقاً في كلامه أو كاذباً؛ إِلَّا أَنَّه لا يُقالُ لهما إنَّهُ صادق أو كاذب فى العرفِ؛ لأنَّ كَلامَهُما مُلحق بألحان الطُّيور؛ ليسَ بخبرٍ وَلَا إنشاء، نصَّ عليه في التَّلويح .ا.هـ. وأورد على التَّعريف المذكور لزوم الدَّورِ؛ لأنَّ الصِّدقَ مُطابقةُ الخبر للواقع، والكذب عدمُ مُطابقتِهِ له، والقضيَّةُ مُرادفةٌ للخبر، وأجيب بأجوبة منها: أنَّ الصدق والكذب بديهيَّانِ، ولو سلم أَنَّهما نظريَّانِ؛ فيجوزُ أنْ يعرف الخبرُ تَعريفاً تنبيهيًا بالصِّدقِ المعرَّفِ بالخبر تعريفاً كسبيا؛ اللازم منهُ توقُفُ الخبر عند المدركة على حصولِ الصِّدقِ المتوقفِ على حصول الخبر ابتداء.
[Definition of the proposition] (The proposition is a statement that admits truth and falsity) Dusooqi: His statement: 'truth... etc.' the intended meaning of truth is: conformity of the verbal relation, affirmative or negative, with reality; and falsity is non-conformity with it. Al-‘Attar: His statement: 'the proposition' is fa‘īla in the sense of maf‘ūla, named thus because it includes the judgment which is called qaḍā'. Allah said: 'And your Lord has decreed [qaḍā] that you worship none but Him' (Qur’an 17:23). And the poet said: 'Allah decreed, O Asma, that I shall not cease to love you...' And qaḍā' is also used for the payment of a debt; the poet said: 'Every debtor repaid and fulfilled his due, but ‘Azza’s debtor is delayed and tormented.' Deriving 'proposition' from this is far-fetched. His statement: 'a statement that admits truth and falsity' is a modification from the original text's: 'a statement which it is correct to say of its utterer that he is truthful in it or lying' to avoid the objection that it defines the thing by the state of its relatum; here it defines it by its own state. This was stated by ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm. He did not say in the definition: 'a statement which it is said...' etc. because it is not necessary for a proposition that it is actually said of its utterer that he is truthful or lying. And he did not say: 'a statement whose utterer is truthful or lying' to exclude the speech of a madman or a sleeper, e.g. 'Zayd is standing', because for each of them, even if in reality he is truthful or lying in his speech, it is not said in convention that he is truthful or lying; for their speech is like the melodies of birds, not a report (khabar) nor a construction (inshā'), as stated in al-Talwīḥ. An objection was raised against the aforementioned definition that it entails circularity, because truth is conformity of a report (khabar) with reality, and falsity is non-conformity, and proposition is synonymous with report. It was answered with several answers, among them: that truth and falsity are self-evident (badīhiyyān); and if they were theoretical, it would still be permissible to define report by a reminder definition (ta‘rīf tanbīhī) using truth defined by report with a acquired definition, leading to a dependence of the report on obtaining truth which depends on obtaining the report initially.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) as a statement that admits truth and falsity. The commentator first explains the linguistic derivation: the word qadiyya comes from qada', meaning judgment, as in the Qur'anic verse 'Your Lord has decreed...'. He then contrasts this definition with an alternative that defines it as 'a statement it is correct to say of its utterer that he is truthful or lying in it'. The commentator points out that the chosen definition is superior because it defines the proposition by its own property (admitting truth/falsity) rather than by its relation to its speaker. This avoids the objection that the alternative defines a thing by the state of its relatum. Additionally, the definition excludes utterances of madmen and sleepers, which are not considered speech acts (khabar or insha'), even if they accidentally correspond to reality. Finally, the passage addresses a circularity objection: truth is defined as conformity of a report with reality, but a proposition is a report. The commentator replies that truth and falsity are self-evident concepts, so no circularity arises; even if they were theoretical, a 'reminder definition' would be acceptable where the definition is initially based on ordinary understanding. This passage is crucial for understanding the nature of propositions and the careful linguistic and logical distinctions made in Islamic logic.

الصدق
الكذب
الخبر

Checkpoint
What is the main answer given to the circularity objection?
A) The definition is not circular because truth and falsity are self-evident.
B) The definition was changed to avoid the objection.
C) The objection is invalid because the definition does not use truth.
D) The definition applies only to reports, not propositions.

Key Takeaway
The definition of proposition does not involve circularity because truth and falsity are known intuitively, eliminating the need for a definitional chain.

Propositions
intermediate
8 min
Definition of al-Qadiyya: Proposition as Statement Admitting Truth and Falsehood
Commentary establishes the definition of proposition and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential features that define a proposition (qadiyya) and distinguish it from performative and nonsensical utterances.

Core Idea
A proposition is defined by its capacity for truth and falsehood, not by the speaker's credibility.

The passage defines the proposition as a statement that admits truth and falsehood. This definition avoids tying truth to the speaker's state and focuses on the statement's intrinsic property. We will analyze how this definition works and what it excludes.

Guided Path
1
Find the definiendum
The passage defines 'al-qadiyya' (the proposition) as 'qawlun yahtamilu al-sidqa wal-kidhiba' (a statement that admits truth and falsehood). Identify this definition in the text.
The term 'qawl' implies a meaningful linguistic utterance, not just any sound.
2
Understand why the definition was changed
The commentary explains that the original definition said 'a statement of which it is valid to say of its utterer that he is truthful or lying'. This was changed because it defined the proposition by the state of its object (the speaker) rather than by itself.
The new definition defines the proposition by its own capacity, not external attribution.
3
Identify what the definition excludes
The definition excludes the speech of the madman and the sleeper, even if their words happen to be true/false in reality, because such utterances are not considered reports (khabar) or performatives (insha) in custom.
This shows that logical propositions require a responsible agent, but the definition itself does not depend on that.
4
Distinguish from performative statements
The passage later notes that performative utterances (insha) do not admit truth/falsehood because they are not representations (hikaaya) of a real state of affairs.
This reinforces that only statements that claim something about reality can be true or false.

(قَوْلُهُ : قَوْلُ يَحْتَمِلُ الصَّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ) عدل عن قول الأصل: إنَّها قول يصح أن يُقالَ لِقائِلِهِ : إِنَّهُ صادقٌ فيه أو كاذب؛ لِسلامة مَا هَهُنا عما أورد عليه بأنَّهُ تعريف للشَّيء بحال متعلّقه، وما هنا : تعريف له بحال نفسه ؛ قاله عبد الحكيم، ولم يقل في التّعريف : قولٌ يُقالُ ... إلخ؛ إذْ لا يلزمُ في القضيَّةِ أنْ يُقالَ بالفعل لقائله : إِنَّهُ صادق فيه أو كاذب، ولم يقل : قول قائله صادق أو كاذب ؛ ليخرج قول المجنون والنائم، زيدٌ قائم، فإنَّ كُلا منهما ، وإنْ كانَ في نفس الأمر صادقاً في كلامه أو كاذباً ؛ إِلَّا أَنَّه لا يُقالُ لهما إنَّهُ صادق أو كاذب فى العرفِ ؛ لأنَّ كَلامَهُما مُلحق بألحان الطُّيور ؛ ليسَ بخبرٍ وَلَا إنشاء، نصَّ عليه في التَّلويح .ا.هـ. وأورد على التَّعريف المذكور لزوم الدَّورِ ؛ لأنَّ الصِّدقَ مُطابقةُ الخبر للواقع، والكذب عدمُ مُطابقتِهِ له، والقضيَّةُ مُرادفةٌ للخبر، وأجيب بأجوبة منها : أنَّ الصدق والكذب بديهيَّانِ، ولو سلم أَنَّهما نظريَّانِ؛ فيجوزُ أنْ يعرف الخبرُ تَعريفاً تنبيهيًا بالصِّدقِ المعرَّفِ بالخبر تعريفاً كَسبيا ؛ اللازم منهُ توقُفُ الخبر عند المدركة على حصولِ الصِّدقِ المتوقفِ على حصول الخبر ابتداء، فالخبر في حضورِهِ مَوقوف على حصولِ الصِّدقِ، وفي حصوله ابتداء موقوف عليه لهُ ، فَكَونُهُ موقوفاً وموقوفاً عليه ليس من جهةٍ واحدةٍ ؛ فَلا دور ; لأنَّ الغرضَ مِنَ التّعريفِ التَّنبيهيّ : إحضارُ الشَّيء في المدركةِ بعد حصوله في الخزانة، ويجوز أن يحصل هذا الغرضُ مِن أمرٍ يتوقَّفُ في الحصول على ذلكَ الشَّيء، ومنها أنَّهُ لا يلزمُ مِن جريان الدورِ في الخبر جريانه في القضيَّة; إذ قد يكون هذا المفهوم ; معلوماً هُنَا ; من حيثُ كونه خبراً مجهولاً ، من حيثُ كونِهِ قضيَّةٌ, فَلَا يتوقَّفُ الصِّدقُ والكذب على القضيَّةِ المجهولة; بل على الخبر المعلوم، ومنها أنَّ الصدق هو مطابقةُ الأمر الذهني، ونظر فيه الجلالُ بأنَّ التَّصوراتِ مُطابقةٌ ولا توصفُ بالصَّدق أصلاً، وأجابَ مير زاهد بأنَّ المراد مطابقةُ النِّسبةِ الَّتِي هِيَ حكايةٌ عن الواقع، وهذه المطابقة غير المطابقة التي في التَّصوراتِ , فإِنَّ مَرجِعَ المطابقة فيها ; الحملُ على ذي الصُّورة أو على المأخذ، ومرجع هذه المطابقة هو الوقوع في نفس الأمر , قال : ويظهرُ مِن ذلكَ أنَّ المطابقة أوَّلاً وبالذَّاتِ للنّسبةِ، وثانياً وبالعرض للخبر المشتمل عليها .ا.هـ. والمراد باحتمال الصدقِ والكذب ; تجويز العقل لهما بالنَّظر إلى المفهوم مع قطعِ النَّظَرِ عَمَّا هو في الواقع، ومنشأ ذلك اشتماله على نسبةٍ هِيَ حكايةٌ عَن أمر واقع , فَإِنَّ شأن الحكاية أن توصفَ بالمطابقة وعدمها ; بخلافِ النّسب الإنشائية ; فإنَّها ليست حكاية عن أمرٍ واقع, فلا يجري فيها الصِّدقُ والكذبُ , فإن قلت : ما ذلك الأمر الواقع? قُلْنَا : هو في الحمليَّاتِ ; كون الموضوع في نفسه، بحيثُ يصح الحكم بأنَّه هو المحمول، وهذه الحيثيَّةُ حسب اختلاف الحمل ; مثلاً في حملِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ : نفس حيثية ذات الموضوع، وفي حمل الوجودِ : حيثيَّة استناده إلى الجاعل , وفي حمل الأوصاف العينية : قيام مبدأ
The proposition: a statement that admits truth and falsehood. Dusuqi: His statement: truth ... etc. The intended meaning of truth is the conformity of the linguistic relation, positive or negative, to reality, and falsehood is the lack of conformity thereof. al-Mutar: His statement: the proposition is of the pattern fa'eela in the sense of maf'oola; it was named thus for its inclusion of the judgment that is called decree, as God said: 'And your Lord has decreed that you worship none but Him' [al-Isra 23], and the poet said: 'God decreed, O Asma, that I will not cease loving you until the one who closes his eyes closes them .' And decree may be used for the payment of a debt; the poet said: 'Every debtor paid and fulfilled to his creditor, but Izza's debtor is delayed and his creditor is distressed.' And deriving the proposition from this is far-fetched. His statement: a statement that admits truth and falsehood. He deviated from the statement of the original that it is a statement of which it is valid to say of its utterer that he is truthful in it or lying, because of the soundness of what is here from the objection raised against it that it is a definition of a thing by the state of its object, whereas here it is a definition of it by its own state; said by Abdul Hakim. And he did not say in the definition: a statement that is said... etc., because it is not necessary in the proposition that it actually be said of its utterer that he is truthful in it or lying, and he did not say: a statement of which its utterer is truthful or lying, in order to exclude the speech of the madman and the sleeper, 'Zaid is standing', for each of them, even if in reality he is truthful in his speech or lying, yet it is not said of them that he is truthful or lying in custom, because their speech is attached to the melodies of birds; it is not a report nor a performative, as stated in al-Talweeh. End. And there was raised against the mentioned definition the necessity of circularity, because truth is the conformity of the report to reality, and falsehood is the non-conformity thereof, and the proposition is synonymous with the report. And it was answered with answers, among them: that truth and falsehood are self-evident, and even if they are granted to be theoretical, it is permissible for the report to be known by a definition of reminder (tanbeehi) through truth that is defined by the report through an acquisitive definition; the necessary consequence of which is the dependence of the report, when present in the mind, on the obtaining of truth, which depends on the obtaining of the report in the first place. So the report in its presence is dependent on the obtaining of truth, and in its initial obtaining it is dependent on it, so its being both dependent and depended upon is not from one aspect, so there is no circularity, because the purpose of the definition of reminder is to bring the thing to mind after it has been obtained in the storehouse, and it is permissible for this purpose to be obtained from a thing that depends on that thing in its obtaining. And among them: that the occurrence of circularity in the report does not necessitate its occurrence in the proposition, for this concept may be known here insofar as it is a report but unknown insofar as it is a proposition, so truth and falsehood do not depend on the unknown proposition but on the known report. And among them: that truth is the conformity of the mental thing, and al-Jalal objected to this that concepts are conforming and are not described as truth at all. And Mir Zahid answered that what is meant is the conformity of the relation that is a representation of reality, and this conformity is different from the conformity that is in concepts, for the reference of conformity in concepts is the predication on the possessor of the form or on the source, whereas the reference of this conformity is the occurrence in the very fact. He said: and it appears from this that conformity primarily and essentially belongs to the relation, and secondarily and accidentally to the report that contains it. End. And the intended meaning of the admission of truth and falsehood is the intellect's allowance of them with respect to the concept, disregarding what is in reality. And the source of this is its inclusion of a relation that is a representation of a real matter; for the property of representation is that it is described by conformity and its lack, unlike performative relations, for they are not a representation of a real matter, so truth and falsehood do not apply to them. If you say: what is that real matter? We say: it is in categorical propositions: the subject being in itself such that it is correct to judge that it is the predicate, and this aspect differs according to the type of predication; for example, in the predication of essential attributes: the very aspect of the subject's essence, and in the predication of existence: the aspect of its dependency on the maker, and in the predication of accidental attributes: the existence of the principle.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of al-qadiyya (proposition) found in the matn. It begins by stating the definition: a proposition is a statement that admits truth and falsehood. Then multiple commentators expand on this definition. al-Dusuqi clarifies that truth means correspondence of the linguistic relation (positive or negative) to reality, and falsehood is lack of such correspondence. al-Mutar provides a linguistic note: the word qadiyya is a verbal noun in the pattern fa'eela with the sense of maf'oola, and it is named for containing a judgment (qada). He quotes Quranic and poetic evidence, then dismisses a possible derivation from debt repayment as far-fetched. The next explanation notes that the definition deliberately avoids saying 'a statement that is said to be true or false of its utterer' because that would be a definition by the state of the object (the speaker) rather than the proposition itself. Instead, it defines the proposition by its own nature. The definition also excludes the speech of the madman and sleeper, because customarily truth/falsity are not attributed to them. Then an objection is raised: the definition is circular because truth is defined as conformity of the report (khabar) to reality, but qadiyya is synonymous with khabar, so truth is defined by the report and the report by truth. Several answers are given. The first answer argues that truth and falsehood are self-evident; even if they are theoretical, a 'reminder definition' can use truth to recall the report, and the dependency is not from the same aspect, so no circularity. The second answer distinguishes between report and proposition: circularity may hold for report but not for proposition because they are considered under different aspects (known/unknown). The third answer, supported by Mir Zahid, distinguishes between conformity of concepts and conformity of relations. Conformity of a concept means the concept corresponds to its object; that is not described as truth. Truth is conformity of the relational judgment (nisba) to reality. The passage concludes by clarifying that 'admission of truth and falsehood' means the intellect allows both possibilities when considering the concept alone, ignoring actual reality. This is because the proposition includes a representation of a real state of affairs, which can correspond or not. Performative statements (insha) lack this representational function. Finally, the passage explains what 'real matter' is in categorical propositions: it is the subject being in itself such that the predicate can truly be predicated of it, varying according to the type of predication (essential, existential, accidental).

الصدق
الكذب
الخبر

Key Takeaway
A proposition is defined by its intrinsic capacity to be true or false, not by external factors like the speaker's condition.

?

Propositions
