Resolving Circularity in Defining Proposition via Truth and Falsehood
Commentary resolves the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the circularity objection against the definition of proposition and evaluate the validity of the responses given by the commentators.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided when definition is seen as a reminder and dependencies are from different aspects.

The definition of proposition via truth and falsehood faces a circularity objection: truth is defined by report, and proposition is report. The passage presents multiple responses: self-evidence, different aspects, and two kinds of conformity. We will analyze these responses and see how they defend the definition.

Guided Path
1
Formulate the circularity
Truth = conformity of report (khabar) to reality. Proposition = khabar. So defining proposition by truth uses khabar, which was defined by truth. This is a circle.
This objection is raised against the definition given in the matn.
2
Examine the first answer: self-evidence
Truth and falsehood are said to be self-evident. Even if theoretical, a reminder definition (tanbeehi) can use truth to bring the proposition to mind without requiring a full definition; the circle is not vicious.
A reminder definition assumes prior knowledge of the definiendum and just recalls it.
3
Examine the second answer: different aspects
The circle may hold for 'report' but not for 'proposition' because the concept is known under one aspect (report) and unknown under another (proposition). The dependency is not reciprocal in the same respect.
This uses the distinction between what is known and unknown in the mind.
4
Examine the third answer: two kinds of conformity
Truth is conformity of the relational judgment (nisba) to reality. The conformity of concepts (e.g., mental image matching object) is different and not called truth. So the definition does not rely on the same conformity used for truth.
Mir Zahid and others make this distinction.
5
Assess the overall resolution
The commentators conclude that the definition is valid; the circularity is only apparent or can be resolved by the above distinctions.
This shows the sophistication of Islamic logical analysis.

مثلاً في حملِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ : نفس حيثية ذات الموضوع، وفي حمل الوجودِ : حيثيَّة استناده إلى الجاعل , وفي حمل الأوصاف العينية : قيام مبدأ
The proposition: a statement that admits truth and falsehood. Dusuqi: His statement: truth ... etc. The intended meaning of truth is the conformity of the linguistic relation, positive or negative, to reality, and falsehood is the lack of conformity thereof. al-Mutar: His statement: the proposition is of the pattern fa'eela in the sense of maf'oola; it was named thus for its inclusion of the judgment that is called decree, as God said: 'And your Lord has decreed that you worship none but Him' [al-Isra 23], and the poet said: 'God decreed, O Asma, that I will not cease loving you until the one who closes his eyes closes them .' And decree may be used for the payment of a debt; the poet said: 'Every debtor paid and fulfilled to his creditor, but Izza's debtor is delayed and his creditor is distressed.' And deriving the proposition from this is far-fetched. His statement: a statement that admits truth and falsehood. He deviated from the statement of the original that it is a statement of which it is valid to say of its utterer that he is truthful in it or lying, because of the soundness of what is here from the objection raised against it that it is a definition of a thing by the state of its object, whereas here it is a definition of it by its own state; said by Abdul Hakim. And he did not say in the definition: a statement that is said... etc., because it is not necessary in the proposition that it actually be said of its utterer that he is truthful in it or lying, and he did not say: a statement of which its utterer is truthful or lying, in order to exclude the speech of the madman and the sleeper, 'Zaid is standing', for each of them, even if in reality he is truthful in his speech or lying, yet it is not said of them that he is truthful or lying in custom, because their speech is attached to the melodies of birds; it is not a report nor a performative, as stated in al-Talweeh. End. And there was raised against the mentioned definition the necessity of circularity, because truth is the conformity of the report to reality, and falsehood is the non-conformity thereof, and the proposition is synonymous with the report. And it was answered with answers, among them: that truth and falsehood are self-evident, and even if they are granted to be theoretical, it is permissible for the report to be known by a definition of reminder (tanbeehi) through truth that is defined by the report through an acquisitive definition; the necessary consequence of which is the dependence of the report, when present in the mind, on the obtaining of truth, which depends on the obtaining of the report in the first place. So the report in its presence is dependent on the obtaining of truth, and in its initial obtaining it is dependent on it, so its being both dependent and depended upon is not from one aspect, so there is no circularity, because the purpose of the definition of reminder is to bring the thing to mind after it has been obtained in the storehouse, and it is permissible for this purpose to be obtained from a thing that depends on that thing in its obtaining. And among them: that the occurrence of circularity in the report does not necessitate its occurrence in the proposition, for this concept may be known here insofar as it is a report but unknown insofar as it is a proposition, so truth and falsehood do not depend on the unknown proposition but on the known report. And among them: that truth is the conformity of the mental thing, and al-Jalal objected to this that concepts are conforming and are not described as truth at all. And Mir Zahid answered that what is meant is the conformity of the relation that is a representation of reality, and this conformity is different from the conformity that is in concepts, for the reference of conformity in concepts is the predication on the possessor of the form or on the source, whereas the reference of this conformity is the occurrence in the very fact. He said: and it appears from this that conformity primarily and essentially belongs to the relation, and secondarily and accidentally to the report that contains it. End. And the intended meaning of the admission of truth and falsehood is the intellect's allowance of them with respect to the concept, disregarding what is in reality. And the source of this is its inclusion of a relation that is a representation of a real matter; for the property of representation is that it is described by conformity and its lack, unlike performative relations, for they are not a representation of a real matter, so truth and falsehood do not apply to them. If you say: what is that real matter? We say: it is in categorical propositions: the subject being in itself such that it is correct to judge that it is the predicate, and this aspect differs according to the type of predication; for example, in the predication of essential attributes: the very aspect of the subject's essence, and in the predication of existence: the aspect of its dependency on the maker, and in the predication of accidental attributes: the existence of the principle.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of al-qadiyya (proposition) found in the matn. It begins by stating the definition: a proposition is a statement that admits truth and falsehood. Then multiple commentators expand on this definition. al-Dusuqi clarifies that truth means correspondence of the linguistic relation (positive or negative) to reality, and falsehood is lack of such correspondence. al-Mutar provides a linguistic note: the word qadiyya is a verbal noun in the pattern fa'eela with the sense of maf'oola, and it is named for containing a judgment (qada). He quotes Quranic and poetic evidence, then dismisses a possible derivation from debt repayment as far-fetched. The next explanation notes that the definition deliberately avoids saying 'a statement that is said to be true or false of its utterer' because that would be a definition by the state of the object (the speaker) rather than the proposition itself. Instead, it defines the proposition by its own nature. The definition also excludes the speech of the madman and sleeper, because customarily truth/falsity are not attributed to them. Then an objection is raised: the definition is circular because truth is defined as conformity of the report (khabar) to reality, but qadiyya is synonymous with khabar, so truth is defined by the report and the report by truth. Several answers are given. The first answer argues that truth and falsehood are self-evident; even if they are theoretical, a 'reminder definition' can use truth to recall the report, and the dependency is not from the same aspect, so no circularity. The second answer distinguishes between report and proposition: circularity may hold for report but not for proposition because they are considered under different aspects (known/unknown). The third answer, supported by Mir Zahid, distinguishes between conformity of concepts and conformity of relations. Conformity of a concept means the concept corresponds to its object; that is not described as truth. Truth is conformity of the relational judgment (nisba) to reality. The passage concludes by clarifying that 'admission of truth and falsehood' means the intellect allows both possibilities when considering the concept alone, ignoring actual reality. This is because the proposition includes a representation of a real state of affairs, which can correspond or not. Performative statements (insha) lack this representational function. Finally, the passage explains what 'real matter' is in categorical propositions: it is the subject being in itself such that the predicate can truly be predicated of it, varying according to the type of predication (essential, existential, accidental).

التعريف التنبيهي
المطابقة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the third response (distinguishing two kinds of conformity) break the circularity?
A) Because truth is defined by report, and report is defined by truth.
B) Because truth is conformity of the relation to reality, while concept conformity is different and not called truth.
C) Because the definition is only a reminder.
D) Because the proposition is not the same as report.

Key Takeaway
Circularity in definitions can be resolved by appealing to self-evidence, different aspects, or distinguishing types of relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In Usul al-Fiqh, 'validity' (sihha) is often defined in terms of legal effect, and legal effect in terms of validity. Apply the 'different aspects' response to see if this is a vicious circle.
?
Find a definition in a classical text that might appear circular and evaluate using one of the three responses given in this passage.

Propositions

Defining Qawl and Qadiyya: Uttered vs. Mental
Commentators on the definitions of qawl and qadiyya and the literal/metaphorical distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'qawl' (statement) and 'qadiyya' (proposition) in Islamic logic, and identify whether a usage is literal or metaphorical according to the commentators.

Core Idea
Qawl and qadiyya are not synonyms: one prioritizes uttered, the other mental, and their literal status is debated.

This passage from the Sharh on the Tahdhib discusses the definitions of two foundational terms: qawl (statement) and qadiyya (proposition). The commentators clarify that qawl in logic is always composite, unlike in grammar. They also explore a dispute about whether these terms are used literally for uttered or mental entities. Understanding this helps you grasp the subject matter of logic itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main terms
Locate the definitions of 'qawl' and 'qadiyya' in the Arabic. Note that both are defined in relation to utterance and mental concept.
2
Extract the definition of qawl for logicians
According to the text, logicians define qawl as a composite utterance (al-lafz al-murakkab) or a composite intellectual concept (al-mafhum al-'aqli al-murakkab). It is always composite, never simple.
Compare with grammarians: they include single words (mufrad) as qawl.
3
Distinguish qadiyya from qawl
Qadiyya is a proposition that can be either uttered or mental. The text gives the example of running 'Zayd is standing' in one's heart. It is called qadiyya just as the uttered form is.
4
Analyze the literal/metaphorical dispute
Al-'Attar holds that qawl is literal for uttered and metaphorical for mental, while qadiyya is the reverse. Others say both are literal in both senses by homonymy. Understand the reasoning: transfer (naql) would require abandoning the first meaning, which did not occur.
This affects how we interpret definitions of mental propositions.
5
Apply to current definition
Determine whether the definition at hand targets the uttered or mental qadiyya. If mental, then 'qawl' in the definition must be taken as mental; if uttered, then as uttered. This shows the importance of the dispute.

أو المفهوم العقلي المركَّبُ - جنس يشمل القضيَّةً وغيرها من المركبات
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: أَوْ الْمَفْهُوْمُ الْعَقْلِيُّ) وهو القضيَّةُ العقليَّةُ التي أجريتها على قلبك من غير تلفظ بها، كما إذا أجريت على قلبكَ : زيدٌ قائم، فيُقالُ لذلك : قضيَّة، كما يُقالُ على اللفظ ، قيل : على سبيل الحقيقةِ فيهما(۱)، وقيل: في أحدهما حقيقة، وفي الآخَر مَجاز، وقولُهُ: وَهُوَ اللُّفظُ المركَّبُ ؛ هذا تفسير للقول عند المناطقة، وهو عندَهُم لا يكونُ إِلَّا مُركَّباً، وأما عندَ النُّحاة؛ فهو شامل للمفرد والمركب. (قَوْلُهُ : مِنَ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ) بيان للغير
العطار
عليهِ قُدَّسَ سِرُّهُ في أَوَّلِ مَبَاحِثِ المعاني المفردة، فالقولُ : حقيقة في الملفوظ ، مجاز في المعقول ؛ على عكس القضيَّة، ولا يمكن أن يكون لفظ القضيَّةِ منقولاً عن القضيَّةِ الملفوظةِ إلى المعقولة بناءً على أنَّ القدماء : جعلوا موضوعاتِ مسائل المنطقِ الألفاظ، والمتأخّرينَ أجروا الأحكام على المعقولات ؛ لأنَّ المنقول يُشترط فيهِ هجر المعنى الأَوَّلِ، وَلَا هجرَ هَهُنا ، على أنَّ جعل القدماء الألفاظ موضوعات المسائل ؛ لا يقتضي الوضعَ؛ أي: وضعَ لفظ القضيَّةِ بإزاء القضيَّةِ الملفوظة؛ لجواز أنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الجعلُ بإقامةِ الدَّليل مقامَ المدلول تسهيلاً لِلفَهم، كيفَ وقَدِ اتَّفَقُوا على أنَّ موضوع المنطقِ المعقولاتُ الثَّانيةُ ، أو المعلومات التصوريَّةُ والتصديقيَّةُ ا.هـ. فسقط قولُ المحشّي : القولُ في هذا الفنّ المركَّب، ويشبه أن يكون المركَّبُ المعقول؛ لأنَّ نظر الفنّ بالذَّاتِ في المعقول حتَّى يكون الملفوظ قولاً بالعرض على عكس المرتَّبِ .. هـ. ثمَّ إِنْ كانَ المقصود تعريف القضيَّةِ المعقولةِ كَمَا هو الظَّاهرُ ؛ يُحملُ القولُ على المعقول، وإن كان المقصود تعريف القضيَّةِ الملفوظة؛ يُحملُ القولُ على
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : الحقيقة فيهما ... إلخ) أي : بطريق الاشتراك اللفظي في كل من القول والقضية، فالقول الملفوظ للقضية الملفوظة، والمعقول للمعقولة، وصحَّ أخذ المشترك في التعريف لأن المعرف مثله، فهو في قوة تعريفين لحرفين، وعبد الحكيم يرى أن القضية حقيقة في المعقول مجاز في الملفوظ ، والقول بالعكس، وعليه فلا اشتراك، وصح تعريف المعقول بالملفوظ لأنه دال عليه. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
or the composite intellectual concept - a genus that includes the proposition and other composites. al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'or the intellectual concept') and it is the intellectual proposition that you conduct in your heart without uttering it, as when you conduct in your heart: Zayd is standing, so that is called a proposition, just as it is called for the utterance. It was said: on the basis of literalness in both, and it was said: in one of them literal and in the other metaphorical. And his saying: 'and it is the composite utterance'; this is an interpretation of 'statement' according to the logicians, and according to them it is only composite, but according to grammarians it includes both simple and composite. (His saying: 'from the composites') explanation of the other. al-'Attar: Upon him, may his secret be sanctified, at the beginning of the discussions of simple meanings, the statement is literal in the uttered, metaphorical in the mental; contrary to the proposition. And it is impossible for the word 'proposition' to be transferred from the uttered proposition to the mental, based on the fact that the ancients made the subject matters of logical issues the utterances, and the later scholars applied the rulings to the mental concepts, because transfer requires abandoning the first meaning, and there is no abandonment here. Moreover, making utterances the subject matters of issues does not require a lexical assignment, i.e., assigning the word 'proposition' to refer to the uttered proposition, because it is possible that such making is by placing the sign in place of the signified to facilitate understanding, especially since they agreed that the subject of logic is secondary intelligibles or conceptual and assertoric information. End quote. So the statement of the glossator falls: 'Statement in this discipline is composite, and it seems that the composite is mental, because the discipline's primary focus is on the mental so that the uttered is statement by accident, contrary to the order...' End. Then if what is intended is the definition of the mental proposition, as is apparent, the statement is interpreted as mental; and if what is intended is the definition of the uttered proposition, the statement is interpreted as... (1) (His saying: 'literalness in both...etc.') i.e., by way of homonymy in both 'statement' and 'proposition'. So the uttered statement is for the uttered proposition, and the mental for the mental. And the inclusion of a homonym in a definition is valid because the definiendum is like it, so it is like two definitions for two terms. And 'Abd al-Hakim holds that 'proposition' is literal in the mental and metaphorical in the uttered, and 'statement' is the opposite. According to this there is no homonymy, and defining the mental by the uttered is valid because it indicates it. End quote by al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the definitions of the terms 'qawl' (statement) and 'qadiyya' (proposition). It begins by presenting the definition of 'qawl' as either a composite utterance or a composite intellectual concept, noting that the latter is a genus that includes propositions and other composites. Al-Dasuqi then explains that an intellectual concept is a mental proposition you run in your mind, like 'Zayd is standing,' and it is called a proposition just as the uttered form is. There is a disagreement on whether the terms are used literally (haqiqa) for both the mental and uttered senses, or one is literal and the other metaphorical. Al-'Attar adds a detailed discussion: according to him, 'qawl' is literal for uttered and metaphorical for mental, while 'qadiyya' is the opposite. He argues that 'qadiyya' is not a transferred term (manqul) from uttered to mental because that would require abandoning the original meaning, which did not happen. The ancients discussed utterances as subject matter, but later logicians apply rulings to mental concepts; this does not imply lexical assignment. The subject of logic is secondary intelligibles. Therefore, if the definition aims at the mental proposition, 'qawl' must be taken as mental; if at the uttered proposition, 'qawl' is taken as uttered. The footnote mentions the view of 'Abd al-Hakim, who holds that 'qadiyya' is literal in mental, metaphorical in uttered, and 'qawl' is the reverse, so no homonymy. This passage guards against confusing the spoken and mental realms in definition.

Key Terms
القول (al-qawl)
القضية (al-qadiyya)
المفهوم العقلي (al-mafhum al-'aqli)

B) 'Qawl' is literal for uttered and metaphorical for mental; 'qadiyya' is the opposite.
C) Both are literal for mental and metaphorical for uttered.
D) Both are literal for uttered and metaphorical for mental.

Key Takeaway
The definitions of 'qawl' and 'qadiyya' are complex due to the interplay between spoken and mental realms; logicians must carefully distinguish literal from metaphorical usage.

The Differentia of Proposition: Truth-Bearing Judgment
Defining proposition and addressing doubtful declaratives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differentia of a proposition and explain why doubtful declaratives are still propositions.

Core Idea
A proposition's truth-bearing capacity lies in its judgment, not its modality.

In logic, a proposition is defined by its capacity to be true or false. But what about statements where the speaker is uncertain—do they still count? This passage from the commentary on the Ishārāt clarifies that the differentia 'admits truth and falsehood' applies to the judgment itself, not to the whole expression. Let's unpack this key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its differentia
The passage states that the phrase 'admits truth and falsehood' is the differentia (fasl) of the proposition. This means that any expression that can be true or false is a proposition; otherwise it is not.
The differentia distinguishes propositions from other expressions like commands or simple concepts.
2
Recognize the objection about doubtful declaratives
An objector points out that doubtful declaratives (khabariyya mashkūka) also admit truth and falsehood—since they are reports that could be true or false—so they should be covered by the definition. But the definition is meant to exclude them? Actually, the objection is that they would be included, which seems problematic if the definition is only for certain propositions.
Doubtful declaratives include statements like 'Zayd might be standing', where the truth is uncertain.
3
Examine the response: the truth-bearer is the judgment
The commentator answers: 'The one that admits truth and falsehood is the judgment (al-ḥukm).' The judgment is the propositional relation (nisba kalamiyya) that reports a real matter. Imperative and conceptual expressions lack such a judgment, so they do not admit truth and falsehood. Doubtful declaratives, however, contain a judgment—even if the speaker is uncertain about its correspondence to reality.
The judgment is the core of the proposition; it is what makes the proposition true or false.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
Consider 'Strike!' (imperative): it has no judgment, so no truth-value. 'Human' (concept): also no judgment. 'Zayd is standing' (declarative, even if doubtful): contains the judgment that Zayd is standing, which is either true or false. Hence it is a proposition.
This resolves the objection: doubtful declaratives are propositions because they contain a judgment.

(His saying: 'and the doubtful declarative' i.e., doubtful in its relation; as when you say: Zayd is standing, and you are doubtful about the establishment of standing for him or not.) And his saying: 'and the doubtful is devoid of it' i.e., devoid of the judgment, if he intended the judgment.) al-'Attar: the uttered, and in the first case what is meant by admission of truth and falsehood is the mind's deeming them possible in that very statement, and in the second, deeming them possible in its meaning. And this is more appropriate than what the glossator said: 'Here there are two definitions and two definienda, except that they were both performed by one expression due to homonymy.' End quote. (His saying: 'I say: the one that admits truth and falsehood is the judgment') i.e., the judgment which is one of the parts of the proposition, but the judgment that is one of its parts is the judgment in the sense of occurrence and non-occurrence.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of proposition (qadiyya) and its differentia of admitting truth and falsehood. It begins with a chapter heading 'On Definition and its Divisions', listing three types: traditional (like 'rational animal'), imperative (like 'strike'), and doubtful declarative (like 'Zayd is standing' when uncertain). The main text states that the phrase 'it admits truth and falsehood' is a differentia that excludes non-propositions, and the definition fits the proposition. An objection is raised: the doubtful declarative also admits truth and falsehood, so it would be included in the definition. The answer is that the one which admits truth and falsehood is the judgment (hukm), not the whole proposition. Al-Dasuqi explains that the admission of truth and falsehood arises from the relation that reports a real matter; imperative relations and concepts do not report, so they don't admit truth/falsehood. The doubtful declarative is doubtful in its relation, but it still contains a judgment (the relation) which admits truth/falsehood. Al-'Attar adds nuance: the admission of truth/falsehood can be considered in the statement itself or in its meaning. The correct answer is that it is the judgment (the relation of occurrence or non-occurrence) that admits truth/falsehood. This passage guards against the mistaken inclusion of doubtful declaratives as non-propositions; they are propositions because they contain a judgment.

الحكم
الصدق والكذب

The differentia of a proposition is 'admits truth and falsehood', and this applies to the judgment (the propositional relation) within the proposition, not to the entire utterance.

Two Meanings of Hukm and the Inclusion of Doubtful Declaratives
Commentators debate whether hukm is mental assent or linguistic relation, affecting the status of doubtful declarative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of hukm (mental assent vs. linguistic relation) and explain why the doubtful declarative is included in the definition of proposition.

Core Idea
The definition of proposition hinges on the linguistic relation, not the speaker's conviction.

In this passage, al-Dasūqī and al-ʿAṭṭār debate whether the hukm in the definition of proposition means mental assent (tasdiq) or the linguistic relation (nisba kalamiyya). This distinction determines whether doubtful statements—where the speaker is uncertain—are included. Al-ʿAṭṭār shows that the definition intends the linguistic relation, so doubtful statements are included. Al-Sharnūbī then criticizes the earlier commentator for missing the 'aspect qualification' (qayd al-haythiyya). This lesson helps you navigate similar definitional debates in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of hukm
In the passage, distinguish between hukm as mental conviction (tasdiq) and hukm as the linguistic relation (nisba kalamiyya). Which one is used in the definition of proposition?
Al-Dasūqī initially interprets hukm as mental apprehension.
2
Locate the doubtful declarative
Find where the passage discusses the 'mashkuka' (doubtful statement). What is the objection against including it?
The objection is that the doubter has no conviction, so perhaps the statement lacks hukm.
3
Understand al-ʿAṭṭār's resolution
How does al-ʿAṭṭār show that the doubtful statement does have the relevant hukm (linguistic relation) even lacking conviction?
He distinguishes two meanings: hukm as tasdiq (absent) and hukm as the relation (present).
4
Analyze al-Sharnūbī's critique
What is the 'qayd al-haythiyya' and why does al-Sharnūbī say the commentator was heedless? How does this affect the definition?
The qayd al-haythiyya means the definition considers the statement from the aspect of admitting truth, not the speaker's state.

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: هُوَ الْحُكْمُ مُرادُهُ بِهِ : إدراك الوقوع أو اللاوقوع، ويُقالُ لَهُ: إِنَّ المحتمل للصدق والكذب ؛ النِّسبةُ الكلاميَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ مَوردُ الإِيجَابِ والسَّلْبِ؛ كثبوتِ القيام لزيدٍ في : زيدٌ قائم، وحينئذ ؛ فالخبريَّةُ المشكوكةُ داخلةٌ في التَّعريف قطعاً لاشتمالها على نسبة محتملة للصدق والكذب.
(قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا عَرَفْتَ) يُقالُ لَهُ : تقدَّم أنَّ التصديقَ مُباين للقضيَّةِ، فالتصديق عبارة عن الحكم ؛ أي : إدراك أنَّ النِّسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة؛ سواء كان ذلك الإدراك
العطار
لا بمعنى العلم بذلكَ الَّذي هو التصديقُ أو أحد أجزائِهِ، فَلَا يصحُ أَنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّ المشكوكة عارية عنه، إذ المشكوكةُ إنَّما هِيَ عاريةٌ عن الحكم بالمعنى الثَّاني؛ لا عن الحُكْم بالمعنى الأوَّلِ، فَعُلِمَ أَنَّ الخبريَّةَ المشكوكة محتملة للصدق والكذب، فهي داخلة في تعريف القضيَّةِ كَمَا هو قضيَّةُ كلامِهِم، ولهذا ؛ لم يتعرَّضُوا لإخراجها، قاله المحشّي ، وأجيب : بأنَّ الحُكْمَ لا يحتمل ذلكَ إِلَّا بَعدَ فَهْمِهِ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ وحصولِهِ في الذهن، وهو وإنْ كانَ في حد ذاتِهِ الوقوع واللا وقوع؛ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ من حيثُ ذلك الحصولُ في الذهن؛ إيقاع وانتزاع؛ فَلْيتأمل.
عن قائله، فيدخل فيه
(1) (قَوْلُ الشَّارح : كما عرفت ... إلخ) أي : من أن الحكم إلخ) أي من أن الحكم هو إذعان النسبة والشاك لا إذعان عنده فلا حكم عنده . اعلم أنَّ المقضية مرادفة للخبر، وقد عرَّفُوها بأنَّها قول يحتمل إلخ، وقيد الحيثية ملاحظ في التّعريف؛ أي: من حيث هو بقطع النظر الخبر المقطوع بصدقه والمقطوع بكذبه وخبر الشَّالاً قطعاً، فمن الذي قال بخروجه من المعرف حتى يخرجه من التّعريف، ومن الذي قال : إنَّ الخبر يعتمد الحكم بمعنى إذعان النّسبة الذي هو وصف المدرك مع أنا نقطع بدخول خبر الكاذب فكيف بخبر الشَّا؟ الحقيقة أنَّ الشَّارح ذهل عمَّا مضى وعمَّا هنا من اعتبار قيد الحيثية، فقرر ما لم يعلم على أنَّه علم. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
وفي
Al-Dasūqī: (His statement: 'it is the judgment') he intends by it: the apprehension of occurrence or non-occurrence, and it is said to him: indeed the one that admits truth and falsehood is the linguistic relation which is the subject of affirmation and negation, like the establishment of standing for Zayd in 'Zayd is standing'. And thus, the doubtful declarative is definitely included in the definition because it contains a relation that admits truth and falsehood. (His statement: 'as you know') it is said to him: it has preceded that assent (tasdiq) is distinct from the proposition, for assent is an expression of judgment, i.e., the apprehension that the relation exists or does not exist, whether that apprehension is of knowledge or not. Al-ʿAṭṭār: Not in the sense of knowledge, which is assent or one of its parts. So it is not correct to say that the doubtful is devoid of it, because the doubtful is only devoid of judgment in the second sense, not judgment in the first sense. Thus it is known that the doubtful declarative admits truth and falsehood, so it enters the definition of proposition as is evident from their statements. For this reason, they did not attempt to exclude it. The commentator said this, and it was answered: The judgment does not admit that except after its understanding from the utterance and its occurrence in the mind, and although it is in itself occurrence or non-occurrence, yet from the perspective of that occurrence in the mind, it is an imposition and extraction; so reflect. From its speaker, it enters. Footnote: (His statement: 'as you know') i.e., that judgment is [the same] etc. i.e., that judgment is the conviction of the relation, and the doubter has no conviction, so he has no judgment. Know that the proposition is synonymous with the declarative statement, and they have defined it as a statement that admits [truth and falsehood] etc., and the condition of the aspect is considered in the definition; i.e., from the aspect of [the statement] without consideration of the statement whose truth is certain and that whose falsehood is certain, and the statement of the doubter is certainly [something else]. So who said it is excluded from the defined that he excludes it from the definition? And who said that the statement depends on judgment in the sense of conviction of the relation, which is a description of the perceiver, when we are certain of the inclusion of the statement of the liar, so how about the statement of the doubter? The truth is that the commentator was heedless of what has passed and of the consideration of the condition of the aspect here, so he asserted what is not known as if it were known. End. Al-Sharnūbī.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement where al-Dasūqī and al-ʿAṭṭār debate the interpretation of 'hukm' in the definition of proposition. Al-Dasūqī warns that if 'hukm' is taken as the mental apprehension (idrak al-wuqu' aw la-wuqu'), then the doubtful declarative would be included because it contains a linguistic relation that admits truth. Al-ʿAṭṭār responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'hukm': the first is the judgment as the mental act of conviction (tasdiq), the second is the judgment as the linguistic relation (nisba kalamiyya). The doubtful statement lacks the first but possesses the second, and since the definition intends the second (as the locus of truth), the doubtful statement is indeed included in the definition. The commentator al-Sharnūbī then offers a searing critique: the original commentator (al-Dasūqī?) was forgetful—the definition of proposition includes a qualification (qayd al-haythiyya) that looks at the statement 'insofar as it admits truth' without considering psychological conviction. Thus, the doubtful statement, like the liar's statement, certainly enters the definition. This exchange highlights the importance of distinguishing psychological from linguistic aspects in logical definitions. In uṣūl al-fiqh, a similar distinction is made between the ruling as a legal category and the utterance that conveys it.

Key Terms
حكم
تصديق
قيد الحيثية
نسبة كلامية

C) Because it is not a declarative statement.
D) Because the definition excludes all doubtful statements.

Key Takeaway
The doubtful declarative is included in the definition of proposition because the definition focuses on the linguistic relation (nisba kalamiyya) that admits truth, not the mental assent (tasdiq) of the speaker.

Literal vs Metaphorical Usage of 'Khabar'
Author clarifies that calling a doubtful statement 'khabar' is metaphorical, not literal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) application of the term 'khabar' in logic, and identify whether a given statement is a khabar in the literal sense.

Core Idea
Not every statement called 'khabar' is literally a report; some are only metaphorically so.

This lesson clarifies a subtle but important point in Islamic logic: the term 'khabar' (statement/proposition) is used both literally and metaphorically. When we call a doubtful statement (mashkuk) a 'khabar', we are not using the term in its original sense. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion about the nature of propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal definition of 'khabar'
From the passage, a khabar is defined as 'what admits truth and falsehood'. This is its essential property in literal usage.
This is the standard definition in mantiq.
2
Compare with doubtful statements
A doubtful statement (mashkuk) does not admit truth and falsehood in the same way; the speaker has no conviction about its truth value.
If the speaker doubts, the statement is not considered a khabar literally.
3
Apply the distinction
Recognize that calling a doubtful statement 'khabar' is metaphorical (majaz), not literal (haqiqah). The term is extended by analogy.
Metaphorical usage requires a connection; here the connection is that both are statements.
4
Test your understanding
Given a statement, decide if it is a literal khabar or a metaphorical one by checking if it admits truth and falsehood in the literal sense.
For example, 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' is literal; 'Perhaps it will rain' is metaphorical.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ إطلاق الخبر على المشكوكِ ليس بالحقيقة؛ لأنَّ الخبر ما

Chapter on the Definition and its Divisions. And know that applying the term 'khabar' to the doubtful statement is not in reality; because the khabar is what admits truth and falsehood, and the doubtful is not like that, but rather it is metaphorically.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage clarifies a terminological point. The author states that calling a doubtful statement (mashkuk) a 'khabar' is not literal (haqiqah) but metaphorical (majaz). The reason given is that the literal meaning of 'khabar' is a statement that admits the possibility of truth and falsehood. A doubtful statement, by definition, is one where the truth value is uncertain; it does not admit truth and falsehood in the sense of being potentially either, because the speaker has no conviction. Therefore, it is not a khabar in the literal sense. However, it is still called khabar metaphorically, by extension. This distinction is important because the definition of qadiyya (proposition) includes both certain and doubtful statements, as discussed previously. For example, saying 'Perhaps it will rain tomorrow' is a doubtful statement; it is called a khabar metaphorically, whereas 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' is a literal khabar because it admits truth and falsehood and is actually true.

Key Terms
الخبر
المشكوك
الحقيقة
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because doubtful statements are not known to be true or false.
B) Because the literal definition of khabar requires the possibility of truth and falsehood, which the doubtful lacks.
C) Because doubtful statements are always false.
D) Because the doubtful statement is not a proposition.

Key Takeaway
The term 'khabar' has a specific literal meaning (a statement admitting truth and falsehood), and applying it to doubtful statements is metaphorical.

Understanding the Primary Division of Propositions: Categorical vs. Conditional
Author presents the primary division of proposition into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the primary division of propositions is into categorical and conditional, and identify the role of the relation (nisba) as the formal part.

Core Idea
The relation (nisba) is the form that makes a proposition actual; the terms are merely potential.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of propositions in Islamic logic. Before considering quantity or quality, logicians first divide propositions based on the nature of the relation (nisba) between their parts. The commentary by al-Dasuqi highlights why this division is primary: the nisba is the formal aspect that actualizes the proposition, while the terms are material and potential. Understanding this priority sets the stage for all subsequent logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
The author states that every proposition is either categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya). A categorical proposition asserts or denies a predicate of a subject (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). A conditional proposition connects two statements (e.g., 'If the sun is out, it is day').
These types are distinguished by the nature of the relation between their parts.
2
Understand the basis of the division
Al-Dasuqi explains that this division is primary because it is based on the nisba (relation), which is the formal part of the proposition. The nisba is what makes the proposition actual (fi'l), whereas the terms are the material part (maddah) and are potential (bi al-quwwa).
Form vs. matter is a key Aristotelian distinction used here.
3
Recognize priority of nisba over terms
The terms are only designated as subject/predicate or antecedent/consequent after the nisba is realized. Therefore, the nisba is logically prior in consideration, even though it may be understood later.
This shows why the division based on nisba is primary.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider the categorical proposition 'Prayer is obligatory'. The nisba is the relation of obligation between prayer and the subject. In the conditional 'If one purifies, then prayer is valid', the nisba is the connection between purification and validity.
In both cases, the nisba defines the proposition type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القضية إما حملية أو شرطية كما قال.
الدسوقي
(قوله: إما حملية) تقسيم أولي للقضية، قدمه على ما عداه؛ لأنه باعتبار النسبة وبه تكون القضية بالفعل؛ لأنها جزء صوري، ولا كذلك الأطراف، فإنها جزء مادي بها الشيء بالقوة، وأيضاً إنما يعرض للطرفين التسمية بالموضوع والمحمول والمقدم والتالي بعد تحقق النسبة؛ فهي أسبق في الاعتبار وإن تأخرت في التعقل.
Then the proposition is either categorical or conditional, as he said. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'either categorical') is a primary division of the proposition, which he presented before others because it is based on the relation (nisba), and by it the proposition is actualized; because it is a formal part, unlike the terms, which are a material part by which the thing is potential. Also, the terms are only designated as subject, predicate, antecedent, and consequent after the relation is realized; thus the relation is prior in consideration even if it is later in understanding.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions. The author states that every proposition is either categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya). Al-Dasuqi's commentary explains why this division is primary: it is based on the relation (nisba) which is the formal part of the proposition—that which makes it actual. In contrast, the terms (subject, predicate, antecedent, consequent) are material parts that give the proposition potential content. The relation is what gives the proposition its form; without it, the terms are just concepts. Additionally, the designations 'subject', 'predicate', etc. only apply after the relation is established. Therefore, the division based on the relation (nisba) is logically prior in consideration, even though one might first think of the terms. This highlights the importance of the formal aspect in logic over the material content.

Key Terms
حملية (hamliyya): categorical proposition
شرطية (shartiyya): conditional proposition
نسبة (nisba): the relation, formal part of a proposition

Key Takeaway
The primary division of propositions is into categorical and conditional based on the relational form (nisba), which is the essential formal aspect that makes a proposition an actual logical statement.

The Primary Division of Propositions: Categorical and Conditional
Commentary explains metaphorical usage of 'khabar' and divides propositions into categorical and conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish categorical from conditional propositions and explain why the division based on the relation is primary in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The relation (nisba) is the formal part of a proposition, making the categorical/conditional division foundational.

In this passage, we learn how Islamic logicians classified all propositions into two fundamental types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-Attar also clarify why this division is primary—because it is based on the relation, the formal element that actualizes the proposition. Understanding this division is essential for analyzing any argument in Fiqh, Kalam, or Usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two explanations for 'khabar' in doubtful statements
Find the two reasons given for calling a doubtful proposition a 'khabar'. One is formal similarity (isti'ara), the other is part-whole relationship (majaz mursal). Note how al-Attar corrects a misunderstanding here.
This shows the careful linguistic analysis that underpins logical terminology.
2
Extract the primary division of propositions
Locate the sentence: 'ثمَّ القضيَّة إما حمليَّةٌ أو شرطيَّةٌ'. This is the main division. A categorical proposition predicates something of a subject directly; a conditional proposition relates two clauses with a condition.
Remember: 'Zayd is standing' is categorical; 'If the sun rises, then it is day' is conditional.
3
Analyze why this division is considered primary
Read al-Attar's commentary: he explains that the division is based on the relation (nisba), which is the formal part (juz' suwari). The extremes (subject/predicate or antecedent/consequent) are material parts and become named only after the relation is established.
This reflects the Aristotelian cause framework: formal vs material cause.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider a typical fiqh ruling: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (categorical). If you drink khamr, you are punishable (conditional).' Test your ability to classify each statement.
This skill is crucial for evaluating the structure of legal and theological arguments.

إمَّا باعتبار أنَّ صورته صورة الخبر أو باعتبار اشتماله على أكثر أجزاء الخبر . ثمَّ القضيَّة إما حمليَّةٌ أو شرطيَّةٌ كما قال. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا بِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّ صُوْرَتَهُ ... إلخ) فهو مُجاز بالاستعارة والعلاقة المشابهة في الصُّورة. وحاصله : أَنَّنا شبهنا الأخبار المشكوكة بالتي فيها الحكم بجامع المشابهة في الصورة، واستُعِير اسم المشبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشبَّه استعارةً تصريحيَّةٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا حَمْلِيَّةٌ) تقسيم أولي للقضيَّةِ، قدَّمَهُ على ما عداه؛ لأنَّه باعتبارِ النِّسبةِ وَبِهَا تكونُ القضيَّةُ بالفعل؛ لأنَّها جزء صوري، ولا كذلك الأطرافُ، فإنَّها جزء مادّيّ بها الشَّيءُ بالقوَّةِ، وأيضاً إنَّما يعرضُ للطَّرفَيْنِ التّسمية بالموضوع والمحمول والمقدم والتالي بعد تحققِ النِّسبة؛ فَهِيَ أسبقُ في الاعتبارِ وَإِنْ تَأخَرَتْ في التَّعقُلِ .
Either by considering that its form is the form of a report (khabar) or by considering that it contains most parts of a report. Then the proposition is either categorical or conditional, as he said. al-Dasuqi: (His saying: “Either by considering that its form... etc.”) It is a metaphor by way of simile, the relation being similarity in form. The summary: we likened doubtful reports to those that contain a judgment, due to resemblance in form, and the name of the likened-to was borrowed for the likener, a declared metaphor. (His saying: “Or by considering...” etc.) That is, it is a metonymy, the relation being whole-to-part; i.e., we used “report” which is a name for the whole, intending the part—which is the doubtful statement—as a metonymy. (His saying: “its containing”) i.e., the doubtful statement. (His saying: “most parts of the report”) which are the subject of judgment, the predicate of judgment, and the linguistic relation. This is based on the report containing the subject, the predicate, the judgment-relation, and the judgment. al-Attar: (His saying: “Either by considering that its form is the form of a report”) it is a declared metaphor of the type of applying a thing to what resembles it in form, so the relation is formal similarity, not as it was said: that it is a metonymy; that is an oversight. (His saying: “Or by considering that it contains most parts of a report”) these are, besides the judgment, the predicate, the subject, and the relation. (His saying: “Either categorical”) it is a primary division of the proposition, placed before the others because it is based on the relation, and by it the proposition is actual, because it is a formal part, unlike the extremes, which are material parts by which a thing is potential. Also, the extremes only become named subject and predicate, antecedent and consequent after the relation is realized; so the relation is prior in consideration, even if it is later in intellection.

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the definition and classification of propositions (qadiyya). It begins by discussing why doubtful statements (i.e., propositions whose truth is uncertain) are called 'akhbar' (reports). Two explanations are given: first, they resemble reports in form, so it is a metaphorical usage (isti'ara); second, they contain most parts of a report (subject, predicate, relation) as a metonymy (majaz mursal). The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-Attar clarify the linguistic devices. The passage then states the fundamental division of propositions into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). al-Attar explains that this division is primary because it is based on the relation (nisba), which is the formal cause of the proposition; the extremes (subject and predicate) are material and only receive their names after the relation is established. This prioritizes the relation in logical analysis.

Definition of Categorical Proposition and Its Conditions
Author defines categorical proposition as judgment affirming or denying something of something, with examples of actual and potential singular terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the elements of a categorical proposition (judgment, subject, predicate, affirmation/negation) and classify its terms as actually or potentially singular.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition is a mental judgment affirming or negating something of something, with terms that may be single words or compound units.

This lesson unpacks the core definition of the categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya), the building block of syllogistic logic. The author defines it as a judgment (hukm) of affirmation or negation between two things. We will dissect this definition through the commentators' expansions, focusing on the possible states of the subject and predicate terms and the precise meaning of 'hukm' intended here.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Definition
Locate the phrase: 'If the judgment in it is with affirmation of something for something, or negation of something from something.' This is the definition of a categorical proposition.
The 'it' refers to the proposition itself.
2
Extract the Key Elements
The definition has three components: the judgment (hukm), the affirmation (thubut) or negation (intifa'), and the two things (subject and predicate). Note that judgment here is an act of assent (tasdiq), not the proposition itself.
3
Distinguish Actually Singular vs Potentially Singular Terms
Examine the examples: 'Man is a writer' (both actually singular), 'The rational animal moves by moving its feet' (both potentially singular), and the mixed case 'Zayd, his father stood up' (subject actually singular, predicate potentially singular).
Actually singular terms are single words; potentially singular are compound phrases treated as units.
4
Understand the Concept of 'Potential Singularity'
A potentially singular term is a compound expression considered as one conceptual unit, like 'rational animal' or 'Zayd standing'. This allows complex descriptions to function as subjects or predicates without breaking the proposition's structure.
5
Clarify the Meaning of 'Hukm'
Read al-Attar's quote from Mir Zahid: hukm has four meanings; here it means the fourth: 'tasdiq' (assent), i.e., the mental act of affirming or negating, not the predicate or the propositional link.
This precision avoids confusing the logical definition with grammatical or metaphysical senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعْرَفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
١ - [ القضيَّة الحملية]
(فَإِنْ كَانَ الحُكْمُ) فيها
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: فِيْهَا) أي: القضيَّة، والباء في قوله : (بثبوت) للتصوير؛ أي: ما حكم فيها حكماً مُصوّراً بثبوتِ شيءٍ لشيء، أو بانتفاء شيء عن شيء. وقوله : بثبوتِ شيء لشيءٍ ؛ كانَ الشَّيئانِ مُفردَيْن بالفعل أو بالقوَّة، أو الأوَّلُ ؛ مفرد بالفعل، والثاني بالقوَّةِ، أو العكس، فقولُ الشَّارح : الإِنسانُ كاتب ؛ مثالٌ لِمَا إذا كانا مُفردَيْنِ بالفعل، فإنسان : مُفرد بالفعل، وَكَذَا كاتب، وقوله : والحيوان النَّاطِقُ ينتقل بنقل قدَمَيْهِ، وزيدٌ عالم ... إلخ، مِثَلانِ لِمَا إِذَا كَانَا مُفَردَيْنِ بِالقَوَّةِ، فالمثالُ الأَوَّلُ منهما في قوَّةِ الإنسان ماش، والمثالُ الثَّاني منهما في قوَّةِ هذا اللفظ يُناقضُهُ هذا اللفظ وإنَّما عدد مثال ما إذا كانَ الشَّيئانِ مُفردَيْن بالقوَّةِ ؛ إشارةً إلى أنَّه لَا فرقَ بينَ المركَّب الإسنادي والتقييدي ؛ في أنَّ كُلا منهما يكونُ في قوَّةِ المفرد، وبقي ما إذا كانَ الأوَّلُ مُفرداً بالفعل، والثاني بالقوة، والعكس؛ نحو: زيد قام أبوه، وزيد قائم ؛ قضيَّة.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ كَانَ الْحُكْمُ ... إِلَخ) قالَ مير زاهد : الحُكْمُ يُطلَقُ على أربعة معانٍ ؛ الأَوَّل: جزءُ القضيَّة؛ أي : وقوع النِّسبة أو لا وقوعها، والثَّاني : المحكوم به، والثَّالث: القضيَّةُ من حيث اشتمالها على ربط أحدِ المعنيينِ بِالآخَرِ أو سَلْبِ الرَّبطِ ، والرَّابع: التَّصديقُ والمرادُ هَهُنا هو المعنى الرابع، وعبارة المصنفِ تَحتملُ الأَوَّلَ ؛ بأن تكون الباء فيها للبيان.
(قَوْلُهُ : مثالان إلخ) بل الثاني فقط والأوّل الموضوع فيه مفرد بالفعل، والمحمول بالقوة لأن المفرد بالفعل ما ليس جملة ولو مركباً وبذلك يسلم الشارح من التكرار . ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Chapter: On the Definition and its Divisions
1. The Categorical Proposition
(If the judgment) in it
 al-Dasuqi: (His saying: “in it”) i.e., the proposition, and the ba in his saying “with affirmation” is for depiction; i.e., what is judged in it is a judgment depicted as affirmation of something for something, or negation of something from something. And his saying “with affirmation of something for something”: the two things may be actually singular or potentially singular, or the first actually singular and the second potentially, or vice versa. So the commentator’s saying “Man is a writer” is an example for when both are actually singular, for “man” is actually singular, and likewise “writer”. And his saying “The rational animal moves by moving its feet, and Zayd is knowledgeable... etc.” are two examples for when both are potentially singular: the first example is in the potential of “man walking”, and the second example is in the potential of “this word contradicts this word”. He multiplied examples for when both are potentially singular to indicate that there is no difference between the predicative compound and the restrictive compound in that each of them is in the potentiality of the singular. And there remains the case when the first is actually singular and the second potentially, and vice versa; like “Zayd, his father stood up” and “Zayd is standing” are propositions. 
 al-Attar: (His saying: “If the judgment... etc.”) Mir Zahid said: “Judgment (hukm) is applied to four meanings: first, the part of the proposition, i.e., the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation; second, the predicate (al-mahkum bihi); third, the proposition insofar as it includes the linking of one meaning to another or the negation of the link; and fourth, the assent (tasdiq). What is intended here is the fourth meaning, and the author’s wording can bear the first, if the ba is for clarification.
(Footnote: (His saying: “two examples etc.”) Rather, only the second is an example of both potentially singular, while in the first the subject is actually singular and the predicate potentially singular, because the actually singular is what is not a sentence even if compound. By this the commentator avoids repetition. End. al-Sharnubi.)
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). It begins with a chapter heading on definition and its divisions. The core definition is: a judgment (hukm) that affirms something of something or negates something from something. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that the terms (subject and predicate) can be in various states: both actually singular (e.g., 'Man is a writer'), both potentially singular (e.g., 'The rational animal moves by moving its feet' where the subject is a compound but considered as a unit), or mixed. Examples are given for each case. Al-Attar then clarifies the meaning of 'hukm' by citing Mir Zahid's four meanings: part of proposition, predicate, proposition as a link, and assent (tasdiq). The intended meaning here is the fourth (tasdiq), though the wording could also support the first. A footnote by al-Sharnubi corrects the example count, stating that only the second example truly represents both terms potentially singular.

Key Terms
حكم
ثبوت
انتفاء
مفرد بالفعل
مفرد بالقوة

Checkpoint

A) The part of the proposition (occurrence of the relation)
B) The predicate (al-mahkum bihi)
C) The proposition as a link between meanings
D) The assent (tasdiq)

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition is a judgment of assent that affirms or negates one thing of another, and its terms may be simple words or compound phrases treated as units.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical proposition about a fiqh ruling. For example, using the form 'Every [subject] is [predicate]', write a proposition about the obligation of prayer. Then identify whether the subject and predicate are actually or potentially singular.
?
Analyze the following definition from al-Ghazali's Mi'yar al-'Ilm: 'Knowledge is the cognition of the thing as it is.' Does it fit the definition of a categorical proposition? Why or why not?

Propositions

Definition of Categorical Proposition and Term Types
Author defines the categorical proposition and classifies its terms as actually or potentially simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the subject and predicate of any categorical proposition are actually simple, potentially simple, or mixed, and classify them accordingly.

Core Idea
The categorical proposition's terms can be simple in actuality or potentiality, not just actual words.

The categorical proposition is the basic building block of logical reasoning. Understanding its structure—especially whether its terms are actually simple or potentially simple—allows you to analyze any proposition from Islamic texts, such as fiqh rulings or theological statements. This lesson unpacks the classical definition and shows how to apply it to real examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-hamliyya). Recognize that the definition centers on a judgment (hukm) that affirms or negates something of something.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus: a proposition containing a judgment. The differentia: the judgment is formed by affirming (thubut) or negating (nafy) something of something, where the two things (subject and predicate) can be actually simple, potentially simple, or one of each.
3
Classify the four possible term combinations
List the four cases: both actually simple, both potentially simple, subject actually simple & predicate potentially simple, subject potentially simple & predicate actually simple. Use the examples from the passage: 'al-insanu katib' (both actual), 'al-hayawan al-natiq yantaqil' (both potential), 'Zaydun qama abuhu' (actual+potential), 'Zaydun qa'im' (potential+actual).
4
Understand 'potentially simple'
A term is potentially simple if it can be expressed as a single word without loss of meaning. For example, 'al-hayawan al-natiq yantaqil' can be reduced to 'al-insanu mashin'. This applies to both predicative and restrictive compounds.
The commentator notes that there is no difference between the two in being potentially simple.
5
Apply the classification to new examples
Take a proposition like 'al-khamru muḥarramun' (wine is forbidden). Both terms are actually simple. But 'al-khamru rajīsun' (wine is an abomination) is also actually simple. For a potentially simple case: 'mā yuskiru min al-sharābi ḥarāmun' (whatever intoxicates from drink is forbidden) has a subject that is potentially simple (can be reduced to 'al-muskir').

١ - [ القضيَّة الحملية]
(فَإِنْ كَانَ الحُكْمُ) فيها
(قَوْلُهُ: فِيْهَا) أي: القضيَّة، والباء في قوله : (بثبوت) للتصوير؛ أي: ما حكم فيها حكماً مُصوّراً بثبوتِ شيءٍ لشيء، أو بانتفاء شيء عن شيء. وقوله : بثبوتِ شيء لشيءٍ ؛ كانَ الشَّيئانِ مُفردَيْن بالفعل أو بالقوَّة، أو الأوَّلُ مفرد بالفعل، والثاني بالقوَّةِ، أو العكس، فقولُ الشَّارح : الإِنسانُ كاتب ؛ مثالٌ لِمَا إذا كانا مُفردَيْنِ بالفعل، فإنسان : مُفرد بالفعل، وَكَذَا كاتب، وقوله : والحيوان النَّاطِقُ ينتقل بنقل قدَمَيْهِ، وزيدٌ عالم ... إلخ، مِثَلانِ لِمَا إِذَا كَانَا مُفَردَيْنِ بِالقَوَّةِ، فالمثالُ الأَوَّلُ منهما في قوَّةِ الإنسان ماش، والمثالُ الثَّاني منهما في قوَّةِ هذا اللفظ يُناقضُهُ هذا اللفظ
وإنَّما عدد مثال ما إذا كانَ الشَّيئانِ مُفردَيْن بالقوَّةِ ؛ إشارةً إلى أنَّه لَا فرقَ بينَ المركَّب الإسنادي والتقييدي ؛ في أنَّ كُلا منهما يكونُ في قوَّةِ المفرد، وبقي ما إذا كانَ الأوَّلُ مُفرداً بالفعل، والثاني بالقوة، والعكس؛ نحو: زيد قام أبوه، وزيد قائم ؛ قضيَّة.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ كَانَ الْحُكْمُ ... إِلَخ) قالَ مير زاهد : الحُكْمُ يُطلَقُ على أربعة معانٍ ؛ الأَوَّل: جزءُ القضيَّة؛ أي : وقوع النِّسبة أو لا وقوعها، والثَّاني : المحكوم به، والثَّالث: القضيَّةُ من حيث اشتمالها على ربط أحدِ المعنيينِ بِالآخَرِ أو سَلْبِ الرَّبطِ ، والرَّابع: التَّصديقُ والمرادُ هَهُنا هو المعنى الرابع، وعبارة المصنفِ تَحتملُ الأَوَّلَ ؛ بأن تكون الباء فيها للبيان.
(قَوْلُهُ : بِثْبُوْتِ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ ظاهرُ في : زيدٌ قامَ، وأما في نحو: «قام زيد» ممَّا فيه المحمولُ مُقدَّم ؛ فَلَا يشملُهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يُقالَ : الثَّبوتُ: إما قياسي فقط؛ أي: ليس حمليًا كَمَا في : قام زيد، فإنَّهُ قياسي ليس حمليًا ؛ أي: ليسَ النُّبوتُ فيه بطريق هو هو، أو قياسي حملي؛ أي: بطريق هو هو كالثَّبوتِ في: «زيد قائم»، ومراد المصنفِ بالثَّبوتِ؛ ما يشملُ الثَّبوتين، فيشمل : قام زيدٌ.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ مُنْتَقِلُ) مثالٌ لِمَا كانَ طَرفاهُ مُفردين بالفعل؛ بناءً على أنَّ مُنتقِل بصيغة اسم الفاعل، ولو قال : ينتقل مضارعاً حتّى يكونَ مِثالاً للقضيَّةِ الَّتِي يكون الموضوع فيها مفرداً بالفعل، والمحمول مفرداً بِالقوَّةِ؛ لَسَلِمَ مِنَ التِّكرارِ، ولعلَّهُ سهو مِن قَلَمِ النَّاسِخِ .
(قَوْلُهُ: وَزَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ ... إِلَخ) مثالٌ لِمَا طرفاها مفردانِ بِالقوَّةِ، وأمَّا مثالُ القضيَّةِ الَّتي يكون فيها الموضوع مفرداً بالقوَّةِ والمحمول بالفعل ; فَكَقَوْلِنَا : زيد قائم قضيَّة، والمراد من المفردِ بالقوَّةِ ; مَا يمكنُ التَّعبير عنه بلفظ مُفردٍ حالَ كَوْنِهِ جُزءاً من تلك القضيَّةِ وعند إفادة مُحكمها، والأطرافُ في القضايا المذكورة وإن لم تكنْ مُفرداتٍ بِالفعل ; إِلَّا أَنَّه يمكن أن يُعبَّر عنها بألفاظ مُفردةٍ وأقلُّهَا أَنْ يُقالَ: إنَّ هذا ذاكَ، أَوْ هُوَ هوَ ، أو الموضوع محمولٌ ، إلى غيرِ ذلكَ بِخلافِ الشَّرطيَّاتِ ; فإنَّهُ لا يمكنُ أنْ يُعبَّرَ عَن أَطرافِهَا بِألفاظ مفردةٍ، فَلَا يُقالُ : هذهِ القضيَّةُ تلك القضيَّةُ ، بل إن تحقَّقَتْ هذه القضيَّةُ ; تَحقَّقَتْ تلك القضيَّةُ، وإمَّا أَنْ تَتحقَّقَ هذهِ القضيَّةُ، أو تتحقق تلك القضيَّةُ وَهِيَ لَيسَتْ بِأَلفاظ مُفردةٍ، وعدل المصنِّفُ عن قول : الأصلُ في التَّقسيم إمَّا أن ينحلَّ طَرفاها إلى مُفردَيْنِ ... إلخ لسلامة ما هنا عمَّا أورد على ما هناك، وعبّر بلفظ شيءٍ دونَ مُفردٍ ; لِشمولِهِ كلَّ الأمثلة، بخلافِ المفردِ، فإنَّما يشمل بحسب الظَّاهِرِ الأوَّل، وبقي أَنَّ عَلِمْتُ في نحو : علمْتُ زيداً قائماً ; قضيَّةٌ بالفعل، والنِّسبة الملحوظةُ بينَ عَلِمْتُ وبين زيداً نسبةٌ تامَّةٌ خبريَّةٌ ولَيسَتْ بِحَملِيَّةٍ لأنَّ أحد طرفيها ليسَ بِمُفردٍ لَا بِالفعل وَلَا بِالقوَّةِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا تفاوت بينَ
Chapter: On Definition and its Divisions. 1. The Categorical Proposition. (If the judgment is) in it. (His statement: 'fiha' meaning the proposition, and the ba in his statement 'bithubut' is for taswir; i.e., what is judged in it as a judgment formed by affirming something of something, or by negating something from something. And his statement: 'by affirming something of something'; the two things are either actually simple or potentially simple, or the first is actually simple and the second potentially, or vice versa. So the commentator's statement: 'al-insanu katib' is an example of when both are actually simple, for 'insan' is actually simple, and likewise 'katib'. And his statement: 'al-hayawan al-natiq yantaqil bi naqli qadamayhi, and Zaydun 'alim... etc.' are two examples of when both are potentially simple. The first of them is in the potentiality of 'al-insanu mashin', and the second of them is in the potentiality of this utterance contradicts that utterance. He only multiplied examples for when the two things are potentially simple as an indication that there is no difference between the predicative compound and the restrictive compound in that each of them is in the potentiality of the simple. And there remains what if the first is actually simple and the second potentially, and vice versa; like: 'Zaydun qama abuhu' and 'Zaydun qa'im' is a proposition. (His statement: 'fa in kana al-hukm... etc.) Mir Zahid said: 'al-hukm' is used for four meanings: first: the part of the proposition, i.e., the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation; second: the predicate; third: the proposition in terms of its containing the linking of one meaning to another or the negation of the linking; fourth: assent (tasdiq). What is intended here is the fourth meaning, and the author's expression can bear the first, by the ba being for explication. (His statement: 'bithubuti shay'in lishay'in' apparently includes 'Zaydun qama', but as for like 'qama Zaydun' where the predicate is fronted, it does not include it unless it is said: affirmation (thubut) is either purely relational (qiyasi), i.e., not categorical, as in 'qama Zaydun', for it is relational not categorical, i.e., the affirmation in it is not in the mode of identity; or relational categorical, i.e., in the mode of identity, like the affirmation in 'Zaydun qa'im'. And the author's intent with 'thubut' is what includes both affirmations, so it includes 'qama Zaydun'. (His statement: 'wal-hayawan al-natiq muntaqal') is an example of where both terms are actually simple, based on 'muntaqal' being in the form of an active participle. If he had said 'yantaqil' as a present tense verb so that it would be an example of a proposition where the subject is actually simple and the predicate potentially simple, he would have avoided repetition. Perhaps it is an oversight from the scribe's pen. (His statement: 'wa Zaydun 'alim... etc.') is an example of where both terms are potentially simple. As for the example of a proposition where the subject is potentially simple and the predicate actually simple, it is like our saying: 'Zaydun qa'im' is a proposition. The meaning of 'potentially simple' is what can be expressed with a simple word when it is part of that proposition and when communicating its judgment. The terms in the mentioned propositions, even if they are not actually simple, can be expressed with simple words, the least of which is to say: 'this is that', or 'it is it', or 'the subject is the predicate', etc., unlike conditionals, for their terms cannot be expressed with simple words. So one does not say: 'this proposition is that proposition', but rather 'if this proposition holds, that proposition holds', or 'either this proposition holds or that proposition holds', and they are not simple words. The author deviated from saying: 'the principle in the division is that its two terms are analyzable into two simples... etc.' for the safety of what is here from the objection raised there, and he expressed with the word 'shay'' instead of 'mufrad' to include all the examples, unlike 'mufrad', which apparently includes only the first. And there remains that 'alimtu' in like 'alimtu Zaydan qa'iman' is a proposition in actuality, and the relation observed between 'alimtu' and 'Zaydan' is a complete informative relation, and it is not categorical because one of its terms is not simple either actually or potentially. Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-hamliyya). The author first states the chapter heading and then presents the definition from the matn: 'If the judgment in it consists in affirming something of something or negating something from something.' The commentator, al-Dasuqi, explains that the preposition 'ba' in 'bithubut' indicates the form (taswir), meaning the judgment is formed by affirming or negating. He then clarifies that the two things (subject and predicate) can be actually simple (mufrad bi'l-fi'l), potentially simple (mufrad bi'l-quwwa), or one actually simple and the other potentially. He gives examples: 'al-insanu katib' for both actually simple; 'al-hayawan al-natiq yantaqil' and 'Zaydun 'alim' for both potentially simple. He notes that the first example of the latter is in the potentiality of 'al-insanu mashin' (the human walking), and the second is a similar case. The multiplication of examples for the potentially simple case shows that there is no difference between predicative compounds (like 'human is walking') and restrictive compounds (like 'rational animal') in being potentially simple. He then mentions the remaining cases: subject actually simple and predicate potentially, or vice versa, giving 'Zaydun qama abuhu' and 'Zaydun qa'im' as examples. Next, al-Attar comments on the meaning of 'hukm' (judgment), citing Mir Zahid's classification of four meanings: the part of the proposition (the affirmation or negation itself), the predicate, the proposition as involving linking, and assent (tasdiq). He clarifies that the intended meaning here is the fourth (assent), but the author's wording could also bear the first meaning. Then al-Dasuqi addresses the phrase 'bithubuti shay'in lishay'in', noting that it appears to include 'Zaydun qama' but not 'qama Zaydun' where the predicate is fronted. However, he resolves this by explaining that 'thubut' (affirmation) can be of two types: purely relational (qiyasi) and relational categorical (qiyasi hamli). The author's intended meaning includes both, so 'qama Zaydun' is included. Al-Attar then critiques the example 'al-hayawan al-natiq muntaqal', suggesting that if the author had used the present tense 'yantaqil', it would have been a proper example of a subject actually simple and predicate potentially simple, avoiding repetition. He suspects a scribal error. Al-Attar then explains that 'Zaydun 'alim' exemplifies both terms potentially simple, and he provides another example 'Zaydun qa'im' for subject potentially simple and predicate actually simple. He defines 'potentially simple' as something that can be expressed with a simple word when part of the proposition, and notes that all these examples can be reduced to simple expressions like 'this is that', unlike conditional propositions which cannot. He explains why the author used 'shay'' (thing) instead of 'mufrad' (simple): to include all examples, since 'mufrad' would only cover the first case. Finally, he discusses 'alimtu Zaydan qa'iman', noting that it is a proposition with a complete informative relation but not categorical because one of its terms ('alimtu' meaning 'I knew') is not simple either actually or potentially.

Key Terms
قضية حملية (qadiyya hamliyya): A proposition that affirms or negates something of something, where the judgment is formed by affirmation (thubut) or negation (nafy) between two terms that are simple actually or potentially. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
حكم (hukm): Intended here as the fourth meaning (tasdiq/assent), though the author's wording could bear the first meaning (the part of the proposition, i.e., occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation). [Note: In earlier texts, hukm often means the proposition itself or the judgment; here it is specifically defined as assent.]
مفرد بالفعل (mufrad bi'l-fi'l): A term that is actually simple, i.e., not a compound phrase, like 'insan' (human) or 'katib' (writer). [Note: No significant shift.]
مفرد بالقوة (mufrad bi'l-quwwa): A term that is potentially simple, i.e., it is expressed as a compound but can be reduced to a simple expression, like 'al-hayawan al-natiq yantaqil' which is in the potentiality of 'al-insanu mashin'. [Note: No significant shift.]
ثبوت (thubut): Affirmation; here includes both purely relational affirmation (like 'qama Zaydun') and relational categorical affirmation (like 'Zaydun qa'im'). [Note: In some later texts, thubut is restricted to categorical affirmation; here it is broader.]

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the phrase 'bithubuti shay'in lishay'in' in the definition of the categorical proposition include?
A) Only propositions with both terms actually simple.
B) Only propositions like 'Zaydun qa'im' where the predicate is after the subject.
C) Both 'Zaydun qama' and 'qama Zaydun' because thubut includes two types of affirmation.
D) Only conditional propositions.

Key Takeaway
The categorical proposition is defined by a judgment of affirmation or negation, and its terms can be actually or potentially simple, a classification essential for logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Propositions

The Three Components of a Categorical Proposition
Commentary defines three parts of categorical proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and relation in any categorical proposition from Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has three parts: subject, predicate, and the relation between them.

Before you can evaluate an argument, you must first understand its building blocks: the propositions. In Islamic logic, a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya) is not just a simple statement—it has a precise structure. This lesson will help you dissect any proposition into its essential components, a skill you will use in analysing theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject (mawḍūʿ)
Find the term that is being judged upon. It is called the subject because it is placed to be predicated upon. It can appear before or after the predicate in utterance. Example: in 'Zaid is standing', 'Zaid' is the subject.
In Arabic, the subject may be a noun or pronoun, even as a hidden agent in a verbal sentence.
2
Identify the predicate (maḥmūl)
Find the term that is judged of the subject. It is called the predicate because it is carried (predicated) upon the subject. Example: in 'Zaid is standing', 'standing' is the predicate.
The predicate can be a noun, adjective, or even a verb in some constructions.
3
Identify the relation (nisba)
Determine whether the judgment is affirmative or negative. The relation is the connection between subject and predicate—either affirmation (thubūt) or negation (nafy). It is the core of the judgment.
In early logic, the relation itself is the judgment; later scholars distinguished it as a separate mental entity.
4
Check for a copula (rābiṭa)
Look for a word that explicitly indicates the relation, such as 'huwa' (is) or 'laysa' (is not). In Arabic, the copula is often omitted in nominal sentences but implied.
Later scholars added the copula as a fourth part (see lesson on perceptions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ[؟] وَأَقْسَامِهِ
- (وَ) إِمَّا (سَالِبَةٌ) إِن حُكم فيها بالنفي المذكور .
الحملية لا بُدَّ لها من ثلاثة أمورٍ :
١. [الموضوع]:
الأوَّل: المحكومُ عليه وَيُسَمَّى الْمَحكُومُ عَليه مَوْضُوْعاً)؛
الدسوقي
وقد يُجابُ : بأنَّ تسمية ما حكم فيها بثبوتِ شيءٍ لشيء، أو نفيه عنه : حمليَّةٌ ؛ نظراً لوجودِ الحمل في بعضِ الصُّوَر ، وإِنَّما نُسبَتْ للحمل دون الوضع؛ مع اشتمالها عليه أيضاً ؛ نظراً إلى أنَّ الحمْلَ مِن حيثُ توقُفُ تمام الفائدة عليه أشرفُ مِنَ الوضْعِ . (قَوْلُهُ : مِنْ ثَلَاثَةِ أُمُوْرِ) أي: أجزاء.
(قَوْلُهُ : الْمَحْكُوْمُ عَلَيْهِ) أي : سواءٌ تقدَّمَ في اللفظ أو تأخر، فالأوَّلُ كَ: «زيد قائم» .
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: ثُمَّ الْحَمْلِيَّةُ لَا بُدَّ لَهَا مِنْ ثَلَاثَةِ أُمُوْرِ) هذا على مذهب القدماء، إذْ عندهم إدراكُ النِّسبةِ الثَّابتة بين الموضوع والمحمول هو الحكم، وليس مسبوقاً عندَهُم بِتَصَوُّرِ نسبةٍ هي موردُ الحكم، فإنَّ إثبات تلك النسبةِ مِن تَدقيقاتِ المتأخّرينَ، حيثُ رأوا أنَّ في صورةِ الشَّكِّ[؟] ينضم إلى الإدراكات الحاصلة إدراك آخَرُ كما يشهدُ بِهِ الوجدانُ لا أَنَّهُ يزولُ إدراك ويحصلُ إدراك آخَرُ بَدَلَهُ، وَلِلمناقشة فيه مجال؛ إذ لأحدٍ أن يلتزم أنَّ المدرك في صورةِ الشَّلِّ[؟] هو بعينه المدركُ في صورة الحكم؛ أعني : الوقوع واللاوقوع، والتّفاوت في الإدراكِ؛ فإنَّهُ في الأَوَّلِ : مُدرَك بإدراك غير إذعاني، وفي الثاني : بإدراك إذعاني؛ قالَهُ الجَلالُ، وكأَنَّ الشَّارِحَ اختار مذهب المتقدمين هنا مِن أنَّهُ في بحثِ التَّصديق ؛ مرَّ على أنَّ الأجزاء أربعة للاحتياج على رأي المتأخّرينَ ، إلى أنْ يُقالَ : الرَّابطةُ دلَّتْ على الجزء الثَّالث والرَّابع معاً ، إحداهُمَا : دلالةٌ مُطابقةٌ، والثانية : دلالة التزام.
(قَوْلُهُ : مَوْضُوْعَاً) قالَ السَّيِّدُ : يتناولُ المبتدأ والفاعل أيضاً، فإنَّ زيداً في : قالَ زيد؛ موضوع، وقال محمولٌ ؛ لأنَّ مُحصّل معناه : زيد قائل، أو ذو قول في الزَّمانِ الماضي ا.هـ.
لأنَّه وُضِعَ ليُحمَلَ عليه
۲. [المحمول]
الثَّاني : المحكوم به ، (وَ) يسمَّى (المحْكُومُ بِهِ مَحمُولاً)؛ لحمله على الأول.
الدسوقي
والثَّاني: كـ: «قام زيدا، وقولُهُ لأَنَّهُ وضِعَ؛ أي : ذكر، وقوله: (المحكوم به :أي: سواءٌ تأخَّرَ أو تقدَّم . ) ؛ ؛
.
(قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ وُضِعَ لِيُحْمَلَ عَلَيْهِ هذا آخرُ الكلام، وقوله: (الثَّاني...إلخ)؛ كلامٌ مُستأنفُ، ويتعلَّقُ بتلك الثَّلاثةِ أربع إدراكاتٍ ، فَإدراك الموضوع تصور، وَكَذا إدراك المحمول
و
