وأمَّا النّسبةُ ، فَالإدراك المتعلّق بها إمَّا ألا يكون على وجه الإذعان، وهو تصور أيضاً، وإما أن يكون على وجهِ الإذعان؛ بأن يدرك أنَّها مُطابقةٌ لِلواقع أو غيرُ مطابقةٍ له وهو التصديق، فَفِي النّسبة إدراكان، والرابطةُ : مدلولها النِّسبةُ مِن حيث كونُها مُدركةً للحكم، بل قِيلَ : إِنَّ الرَّابطة مدلولها الحكم، فَقولُه : النِّسبةُ الَّتي بينَهُما ؛ أي: عينُ الحكم ؛ لَا النّسبةُ التَّصوريَّةُ الخالية عن ذلك؛ إذْ ليس لها لفظ وَلا رابط يدلُّ عليها ، فَالمشكوكةُ (١) لا رابط فيها .
العطار
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : فالمشكوكة ... إلخ) هذا خلاف ما قرره من أن المشكوكة داخلة في تعريف القضية، فكيف لا يكون فيها رابط وهي جزء من القضية وبالضرورة إذا فقد الجزء فقد الكلّ ولعل السبب في اضطراب المحشّي والشارح فيها أنَّها مِن التَّصورِ لعدم الإذعان والتصور قسيم التصديق الذي الكلام فيه، فكيف تجعل منه ولردّ هذا نقول : القضية إما مذعنة يتركب منها القياس البرهاني والجدل الخطابي أو مسلمة ولو مقطوعاً بكذبها ويتركب منها القياس الشعري والسفسطي، والمشكوكة من الثَّاني، هذا ما عن لنا، والله أعلم بالصواب، وإليه المرجع والمآب .
Chapter: On the Knowledge[?] and its Divisions. - And it is either negative if judgment is made with the mentioned negation. The categorical proposition must have three things: 1. Subject: The first: the thing judged upon, and it is called the subject; [Dasuqi commentary] It may be answered: That naming what is judged in it with the affirmation of something to something, or its negation, as 'categorical' is due to the presence of predication in some forms, and it is attributed to predication rather than position, despite including it also, because predication, since the completion of benefit depends on it, is nobler than position. (His saying: of three things) i.e., parts. (His saying: the thing judged upon) i.e., whether it precedes in utterance or follows; the first is like: 'Zaid is standing'. [Attar commentary] (His saying: Then the categorical proposition must have three things) This is according to the doctrine of the early scholars, for with them the perception of the fixed relation between subject and predicate is the judgment, and it is not preceded by a conception of a relation that is the subject of judgment. Verily, affirming that relation is from the refinements of the later scholars, as they saw that in the form of doubt, another perception joins the acquired perceptions, as intuition bears witness, not that a perception disappears and another perception takes its place. And there is room for discussion in it, for one may maintain that the perceived in the form of doubt[?] is the same as the perceived in the form of judgment, meaning occurrence and non-occurrence, and the difference is in perception: in the first, it is perceived with non-assentive perception, and in the second with assentive perception; al-Jalal said it. And it is as if the commentator chose the doctrine of the early scholars here, that in the discussion of assent, it passed that the parts are four, according to the later scholars' need, until it is said: The copula indicates the third and fourth part together, one of them by literal indication, and the second by implicative indication. (His saying: subject) Al-Sayyid said: It includes the mubtada' and the fa'il as well; for Zaid in 'Zaid said' is subject, and 'said' is predicate, because the gist of its meaning is: Zaid is a speaker, or one of speech in the past time. End. Because it is placed to be predicated upon. 2. Predicate: The second: the thing judged with, and it is called the predicate, because it is predicated upon the first. [Dasuqi commentary] And the second is like: 'Zaid stood', and his saying: because it was placed; i.e., mentioned, and his saying: (the thing judged with) i.e., whether it follows or precedes. (His saying: because it was placed to be predicated upon) This is the end of the speech, and his saying: (the second...etc.) is an independent statement. And related to those three are four perceptions: so perception of the subject is a conception, likewise perception of the predicate, and as for the relation, the perception related to it is either not in the mode of assent, which is also a conception, or it is in the mode of assent, such that it perceives that it corresponds to reality or does not correspond, and that is assent. So in the relation there are two perceptions. And the copula: its signification is the relation in terms of its being perceived for judgment; rather it is said: the copula's signification is the judgment itself. So his saying: the relation which is between them; i.e., the same as the judgment; not the conception-based relation devoid of that, for it has no word nor copula that indicates it. So the doubtful proposition (1) has no copula in it. [Attar commentary] (1) (His saying: the doubtful proposition...etc.) This contradicts what he established that the doubtful proposition is included in the definition of proposition; so how can it have no copula while it is a part of the proposition, and necessarily if the part is absent, the whole is absent? Perhaps the reason for the confusion of the glossator and commentator about it is that it is from conception due to lack of assent, and conception is the counterpart of assent which is the subject of discourse; so how can it be made from it? To refute this, we say: The proposition is either assentive, from which the demonstrative and dialectical syllogisms are composed, or taken for granted, even if its falsehood is certain, from which the poetic and sophistical syllogisms are composed. The doubtful proposition is of the second type. This is what occurred to us, and Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and destination.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Tadhheeb Sharh expounding on the structure of categorical propositions. It begins with a chapter heading that is somewhat unclear, likely 'On Knowledge and its Divisions'. The first substantive point defines a negative proposition as one where judgment is made with negation. The core discussion then states that a categorical proposition (hamliyya) necessarily requires three things: the subject (mawdu'), the predicate (mahmul), and implicitly the relation (nisba) between them. The commentator explains that the term 'categorical' is used because of the presence of predication (haml) in some forms, and predication is considered nobler than mere positioning. The first part enumerated is the subject: that which is judged upon, called 'subject' because it is placed to be predicated upon. The commentator clarifies that the subject can precede or follow in utterance, giving the example 'Zaid qa'im' (Zaid is standing). 

A lengthy gloss by al-'Attar then compares the views of early (mutaqaddimun) and later (muta'akhkhirun) scholars. According to the early scholars, the perception of the relation between subject and predicate is itself the judgment (hukm), and there is no prior conception of a relation that is the object of judgment. Later scholars, however, refined this by asserting that in cases of doubt, an additional perception joins the existing ones, serving as the object of judgment. Al-'Attar notes that the commentator seems to side with the early school here, though there is room for debate. The discussion then turns to the copula (rabita), stating that for later scholars, the parts of a proposition are four (subject, predicate, relation, and copula), but the copula indicates both the relation and the judgment — one by literal indication, the other by implicative indication. 

The second part enumerated is the predicate: that which is judged with, called 'predicate' because it is predicated upon the subject. Examples include 'Zaid qa'im' or 'Qama Zaid' (Zaid stood). The commentator again notes that the predicate can precede or follow. 

Finally, the passage explains that related to the three parts (subject, predicate, relation) are four perceptions: perception of the subject is a tasawwur (conception), perception of the predicate is also a tasawwur, and perception of the relation can be either non-assentive (another tasawwur) or assentive (tasdiq), thus giving two perceptions for the relation. The copula signifies the relation insofar as it is perceived for judgment; some say it directly signifies the judgment itself. A tricky point arises about the doubtful proposition (mashkuka): if it has no copula, how can it be a proposition? The glossator resolves this by noting that doubtful propositions belong to the category of non-assentive propositions used in poetic and sophistical syllogisms, not in demonstrative or dialectical reasoning.

A categorical proposition consists of subject, predicate, and the relation between them—these are its essential parts.

Propositions
intermediate
12 min
The Four Perceptions and the Copula
Commentary explains four perceptions and role of copula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq in a categorical proposition and explain the function of the copula.

Core Idea
The relation in a proposition is perceived either as mere conception or as assent, and the copula signifies both.

Knowing the parts of a proposition is not enough—you must also understand how the mind grasps them. The passage reveals that four perceptions correspond to the three parts: two concepts (for subject and predicate) and two for the relation (one conceptual, one judgmental). This distinction between tasawwur and tasdiq is central to Islamic epistemology, and the copula plays a crucial role in linking them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate conceptions
Recognise that the mere understanding of 'Zaid' and 'standing' are both tasawwur (conception) — they involve no judgment about truth.
These are simple awareness of the terms.
2
Analyse the relation perception
The relation between subject and predicate can be perceived in two ways: without assent (just understanding the connection) or with assent (judging whether it matches reality). The first is still tasawwur; the second is tasdiq.
In doubtful propositions, the relation is conceived without assent.
3
Locate the copula (rābiṭa)
Find the word that indicates the relation, such as 'huwa' or 'laysa'. The passage explains that the copula signifies the relation in terms of being perceived for judgment, or even the judgment itself.
Some say the copula directly signifies the judgment (hukm).
4
Distinguish early vs later views
Early scholars held that the relation perception is itself the judgment, while later scholars distinguished an additional mental entity: the perceived relation as a subject of judgment. This debate affects whether we count three or four parts.
The copula adds a fourth element for later scholars.

Chapter: On the Knowledge[?] and its Divisions. - And it is either negative if judgment is made with the mentioned negation. The categorical proposition must have three things: 1. [Dasuqi commentary] And the second is like: 'Zaid stood', and his saying: because it was placed; i.e., mentioned, and his saying: (the thing judged with) i.e., whether it follows or precedes. (His saying: because it was placed to be predicated upon) This is the end of the speech, and his saying: (the second...etc.) is an independent statement. And related to those three are four perceptions: so perception of the subject is a conception, likewise perception of the predicate, and as for the relation, the perception related to it is either not in the mode of assent, which is also a conception, or it is in the mode of assent, such that it perceives that it corresponds to reality or does not correspond, and that is assent. So in the relation there are two perceptions. And the copula: its signification is the relation in terms of its being perceived for judgment; rather it is said: the copula's signification is the judgment itself. So his saying: the relation which is between them; i.e., the same as the judgment; not the conception-based relation devoid of that, for it has no word nor copula that indicates it. So the doubtful proposition (1) has no copula in it. [Attar commentary] (1) (His saying: the doubtful proposition...etc.) This contradicts what he established that the doubtful proposition is included in the definition of proposition; so how can it have no copula while it is a part of the proposition, and necessarily if the part is absent, the whole is absent? Perhaps the reason for the confusion of the glossator and commentator about it is that it is from conception due to lack of assent, and conception is the counterpart of assent which is the subject of discourse; so how can it be made from it? To refute this, we say: The proposition is either assentive, from which the demonstrative and dialectical syllogisms are composed, or taken for granted, even if its falsehood is certain, from which the poetic and sophistical syllogisms are composed. The doubtful proposition is of the second type. This is what occurred to us, and Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and destination.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Tadhheeb Sharh expounding on the structure of categorical propositions. It begins with a chapter heading that is somewhat unclear, likely 'On Knowledge and its Divisions'. The first substantive point defines a negative proposition as one where judgment is made with negation. The core discussion then states that a categorical proposition (hamliyya) necessarily requires three things: the subject (mawdu'), the predicate (mahmul), and implicitly the relation (nisba) between them. The commentator explains that the term 'categorical' is used because of the presence of predication (haml) in some forms, and predication is considered nobler than mere positioning. The first part enumerated is the subject: that which is judged upon, called 'subject' because it is placed to be predicated upon. The commentator clarifies that the subject can precede or follow in utterance, giving the example 'Zaid qa'im' (Zaid is standing). 

A lengthy gloss by al-'Attar then compares the views of early (mutaqaddimun) and later (muta'akhkhirun) scholars. According to the early scholars, the perception of the relation between subject and predicate is itself the judgment (hukm), and there is no prior conception of a relation that is the object of judgment. Later scholars, however, refined this by asserting that in cases of doubt, an additional perception joins the existing ones, serving as the object of judgment. Al-'Attar notes that the commentator seems to side with the early school here, though there is room for debate. The discussion then turns to the copula (rabita), stating that for later scholars, the parts of a proposition are four (subject, predicate, relation, and copula), but the copula indicates both the relation and the judgment — one by literal indication, the other by implicative indication. 

The second part enumerated is the predicate: that which is judged with, called 'predicate' because it is predicated upon the subject. Examples include 'Zaid qa'im' or 'Qama Zaid' (Zaid stood). The commentator again notes that the predicate can precede or follow. 

Finally, the passage explains that related to the three parts (subject, predicate, relation) are four perceptions: perception of the subject is a tasawwur (conception), perception of the predicate is also a tasawwur, and perception of the relation can be either non-assentive (another tasawwur) or assentive (tasdiq), thus giving two perceptions for the relation. The copula signifies the relation insofar as it is perceived for judgment; some say it directly signifies the judgment itself. A tricky point arises about the doubtful proposition (mashkuka): if it has no copula, how can it be a proposition? The glossator resolves this by noting that doubtful propositions belong to the category of non-assentive propositions used in poetic and sophistical syllogisms, not in demonstrative or dialectical reasoning.

Key Terms
تصوّر
تصديق
إذعان
نسبة
رابط

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Four perceptions
D) Five perceptions

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition involves four mental perceptions: conception of subject, conception of predicate, conception of relation (non-assentive), and assent to the relation (judgment), with the copula signifying the latter aspect.

Defining the Copula and Its Classification
Author defines the copula, explains its linguistic role, and proves it is a particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the copula in any categorical proposition, classify it as explicit or implicit, and reconstruct the syllogistic proof that the copula is a particle.

Core Idea
The copula is a particle signifying a non-independent relation between subject and predicate.

In this passage, the logician defines the copula (rabita) and explains its role in a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). He then proves that the copula belongs to the category of particles (adawat) by a simple categorical syllogism. Understanding this distinction is foundational for analyzing propositions in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term 'rabita' (copula) is being defined. Find where the author states what the copula is: a word that signifies the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate.
2
Extract the definition components
The copula is defined as a word signifying the relation. Notice that the author also names the relation as the copula (metonymy). Then he classifies it as a particle (adah) by giving a reason: because it signifies a non-independent relation.
The non-independence refers to the relation's dependence on subject and predicate.
3
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism
Major premise (implicit): Everything that signifies a non-independent relation is a particle. Minor premise: The copula signifies a non-independent relation. Conclusion: Therefore, the copula is a particle. This follows the first figure (Barbara).
Check validity: the middle term is 'signifies a non-independent relation'.
4
Apply the classification of propositions
Based on whether the copula is explicitly stated, a proposition is called binary (if copula is implicit), trinary (if explicit), or quadruple (if modality is also stated).
In Arabic, the copula is often implicit in nominal sentences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمية أن يعبر عنها بلفظ دال عليها. النسبة تسمى رابطة؛ لدلالتها على ذلك اللفظ الدال على النسبة الرابطة؛ تسمية للدال باسم المدلول. ثم الرابطة أداة؛ لأنها تدل على النسبة التي هي غير مستقلة؛ لتوقفها على المحكوم عليه وبه. واعلم أن الرابطة إذا لم يصرح بها تسمى الحملية حينئذ ثنائية، وإن صرح بها ثلاثية، وإن صرح بالجهة أيضا فرباعية.
The judgmental (copula) is that it is expressed by a word signifying it. The relation is called the copula, because of its indication of that word signifying the relational copula; a naming of the signifier by the name of the signified. Then the copula is a particle, because it signifies the relation which is non-independent, due to its dependence on the subject and predicate. And know that the copula, if not explicitly stated, the categorical proposition is then called binary; if explicitly stated, trinary; and if the modality is also stated, then quadruple.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage defines the copula (rabita) as the word signifying the relation in a proposition. It first states that the judgmental (al-hukmiyya) is expressed by a word signifying it, then clarifies that the relation is called the copula because the word signifies the relation; this is a naming of the signifier by the signified. Then it establishes that the copula is a particle (adah) by a categorical syllogism: the copula signifies a non-independent relation, and everything signifying a non-independent relation is a particle, so the copula is a particle. The non-independence is due to the relation's dependence on the subject and predicate. Finally, it classifies propositions based on the explicitness of the copula: if not stated, it is binary (subject and predicate only); if stated, trinary; if modality also stated, quadruple. This follows the standard division in logic.

Key Takeaway
The copula is a particle because it signifies a relation that depends on the subject and predicate.

The Connector as an Instrument: A Syllogistic Proof
Dasuqi proves the connector is an instrument using a first-figure categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first-figure categorical syllogism used to prove that the connector is an instrument and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The connector is an instrument because it indicates a dependent meaning — a truth proven by a Barbara syllogism.

In this passage, Dasuqi provides a rigorous logical proof that the connector (rabita) is an instrument (ada) — a particle — by employing a first-figure categorical syllogism. This exemplifies how logicians use syllogistic reasoning to establish definitions. Understanding this proof will sharpen your ability to analyze definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim (natija)
The conclusion that the connector is an instrument (أداة) is explicitly stated as 'ثُمَّ الرَّابطة أداةٌ'. This is what the syllogism aims to prove.
2
Find the minor premise (المقدمة الصغرى)
The minor premise is given by 'لأنَّها تدلُّ على النّسبة التي هي غير مستقلة' – the connector indicates a relation that is not independent.
The connector is the subject of this premise.
3
Find the major premise (المقدمة الكبرى)
The major premise is implicit: 'Whatever indicates a non-independent meaning is an instrument.' It follows from the definition of an instrument (أداة) as a particle indicating a dependent meaning.
This premise is not stated but logically required.
4
Identify the middle term (الحد الأوسط)
The middle term is 'indicating a non-independent meaning' (or 'non-independent in conceptuality'), which appears in both the minor and major premises.
It connects the subject and predicate of the conclusion.
5
Determine the figure and mood, and verify validity
The middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor, so it is first figure. The mood is AAA (Barbara), which is always valid. Therefore the conclusion necessarily follows.
This syllogism is a canonical example of a valid deductive argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ الرَّابطة أداةٌ ؛ لأنَّها تدلُّ على النّسبة التي هي غير مستقلة؛ لتوقفها على المحكوم عليه وبه . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : ثُمَّ الرَّابِطَةُ) أي : اللَّفظُ الدَّالُّ على النّسبةِ أداة؛ أي: حرف . (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهَا ... إلخ) قياس من الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، استدلَّ بِهِ عَلى مَا ادَّعَاهُ مِن أَنَّ الرابطة أداةٌ، وقوله : لأنَّها ؛ أي: الرابطة. واعلَمْ أَنَّ الرَّابطة إذا لم يصرح بها ؛ تُسمَّى الحملية حينئذٍ ثُنائيَّة، وإِنْ صُرِّح بها ثلاثية، وإنْ صُرِّح بالجهة أيضاً ؛ فَرُباعيَّة، وَلَا تُسمَّى عند التصريح بالشورِ خُماسيَّة؛ لأَنَّ مَ معنى الشورِ ليسَ لازماً للقضيَّة . (قَوْلُهُ : غَيْرُ مُسْتَقِلَّةٍ) أي: بالمفهوميَّة .
Then the connector is an instrument (ada); because it indicates the relation which is not independent; due to its dependence on the subject and predicate. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'Then the connector' means the word indicating the relation is an instrument, i.e., a particle. (His statement: 'because it' etc.) It is a syllogism of the first figure, by which he argues for what he claimed, that the connector is an instrument. And his statement: 'because it' refers to the connector. And know that if the connector is not explicitly stated, the categorical proposition is then called binary; if it is stated, ternary; and if the modality is also stated, then quaternary. And it is not called quinary when the doubt is stated, because the meaning of doubt is not necessary for the proposition. (His statement: 'non-independent' means in terms of conceptuality.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage defines the connector (rabita) as an instrument (ada), i.e., a particle. The proof is a categorical syllogism of the first figure: the connector indicates a relation that is not independent (non-independent meaning), and whatever indicates a non-independent meaning is an instrument; therefore, the connector is an instrument. Dasuqi then explains the classification of categorical propositions based on whether the connector or modality is expressed: if the connector is omitted, the proposition is called binary (thunaiyya); if the connector is expressed, ternary (thulathiyya); if the modality (jiha) is also expressed, quaternary (rubaiyya). He clarifies that adding the expression of doubt (shubha) does not make it quinary because doubt is not an essential part of the proposition. Finally, he specifies that 'non-independent' is meant in terms of conceptuality (bi al-mufhimiyya), i.e., the meaning is not independent as a concept.

Key Terms
الرابطة
أداة
قضية حملية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The connector is proven to be an instrument via a valid first-figure categorical syllogism, demonstrating the rigor of Islamic logic.

Classification of Categorical Propositions: Binary, Ternary, and Quaternary
Dasuqi classifies categorical propositions based on expression of connector and modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition as binary, ternary, or quaternary based on whether the connector and modality are expressed.

Core Idea
Whether a proposition is binary, ternary, or quaternary depends on explicit expression of the connector and modality.

After defining the connector, Dasuqi explains how the expression of the connector and modality changes the classification of a categorical proposition. This distinction is crucial for understanding how propositions are analyzed in logical syllogisms. It also clarifies why adding doubt does not create a quinary type.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the connector is expressed
Check whether the connector (e.g., 'huwa', 'ka'ina', or a particle) is explicitly mentioned in the proposition. If not, the proposition is binary.
In Arabic, the connector is often omitted, as in 'Zayd qa'im' (زيد قائم), which is binary.
2
Identify if the modality is expressed
If the connector is expressed, look for the modality (jiha) — words indicating necessity, possibility, impossibility, etc. If the modality is also expressed, the proposition becomes quaternary.
Modality examples: بالضرورة (necessarily), بالممكن (possibly), بالممتنع (impossibly).
3
Apply the classification rule
Rule summary: connector omitted → binary; connector expressed → ternary; both connector and modality expressed → quaternary.
This is a straightforward classification based solely on explicit expression.
4
Note the exclusion of quinary
Even if doubt (شور) is expressed, the proposition is not called quinary because doubt is not an essential component of the proposition's meaning.
Only connector and modality are essential; doubt is accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Copula as a Tool and Its Metaphorical Relatives
Author defines the copula as a tool and discusses its forms in noun and verb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the copula in an Arabic proposition and determine whether it is used literally or metaphorically.

Core Idea
The copula is a tool, not a word, and most Arabic copulae are borrowed forms.

The copula (rābiṭa) is the link between subject and predicate. This passage teaches that the copula is a tool (adāh) indicating a non-independent meaning. It can appear in the pattern of a noun (like 'huwa') or a verb (like 'kana'), but neither is a true copula in its original linguistic sense—they are borrowed metaphorically. Understanding this helps you avoid reading extra meanings into the copula.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula in the proposition
Locate the word that connects the subject and predicate. In 'Zayd huwa 'ālim', the word 'huwa' links 'Zayd' and 'ālim'.
In Arabic, the copula may be omitted (e.g., 'Zaydun 'ālimun').
2
Classify its form as noun or verb
Check the morphological pattern. If it looks like a pronoun or noun (e.g., 'huwa', 'hiya'), it is 'qālab al-ism'. If it looks like a verb (e.g., 'kāna', 'laysa'), it is 'qālab al-kalima'.
Ignore the dictionary meaning; focus on the form.
3
Determine if the copula is used literally or metaphorically
The true copula is the mere relation; any extra meaning (e.g., time, person) indicates it is borrowed. 'Huwa' originally means 'he', 'kāna' means 'was', so they add pronominal or temporal meaning.
The passage states that neither 'huwa' nor 'kāna' is a true copula; they are borrowed for that function.
4
Apply to other examples
Try the proposition 'Allāhu huwa al-khāliq' (Allah is the Creator). Identify 'huwa' as the copula, in noun form, and metaphorical because it originally means 'he'.
This skill is crucial for precise translation and tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدَّالُّ على المعنى الغير المستقل يكون أداةً، فالرابطة أداة، لكنها قد تكون في قالب الاسم، كـ «هو» في : زيد هو عالم، وقد تكون في قالب الكلمة. فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. كـ «كان» في: «زيد كان قائماً». ومن هنا يُعلَمُ أنَّ لفظة «هو» ، وكان ليست رابطة حقيقةً، بل استعيرت للرابطة . ولهذا قال: (وَقَدِ اسْتُعِيرَ لَهَا)؛
The indicator of the non-independent meaning is a tool, so the copula is a tool, but it may appear in the form of a noun, like 'huwa' in: Zaid is knowledgeable, and may appear in the form of a verb. Chapter: On the definition and its divisions. Like 'kana' in: 'Zaid was standing.' And from this it is known that the word 'huwa' and 'kana' are not a true copula, but were borrowed for the copula. And therefore he said: 'And it was borrowed for it'.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage defines the copula (al-rabita) as a type of tool (adāh) that indicates a non-independent meaning. The author then clarifies that the copula can appear in two forms: in the form of a noun (qalab al-ism) like 'huwa' (he) in the proposition 'Zaid huwa 'ālim', and in the form of a verb (qalab al-kalima) like 'kana' in 'Zaid kana qā'iman'. The passage then transitions to a new section on definition (ma'rif) and its divisions, but continues with the same example of 'kana'. A key point is that neither 'huwa' nor 'kana' are true copulae in their original linguistic sense; they are used metaphorically (isti'āra) to serve as the copula. The true copula is the mental relation (al-nisba al-īqā'iyya wa al-intizā'iyya). The commentary of al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar further explains that 'huwa' is originally a pronoun and 'kana' is originally a verb, but they are borrowed for the copulative function. This distinction guards against the misunderstanding that any word linking subject and predicate is a true copula in its literal sense.

Key Terms
رابطة
أداة
قالب الاسم
قالب الكلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a verb and verbs cannot be copulae.
B) Because its original meaning includes a temporal dimension beyond the simple relation.
C) Because the copula must be a noun.
D) Because 'kana' cannot be used in the present tense.

Key Takeaway
The copula is a tool that indicates the relation; words like 'huwa' and 'kana' are borrowed, not true copulae.

The Copula Defined: Forms and Historical Borrowing
Author defines the copula and explains its Arabic and foreign forms, including historical borrowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the various forms of the copula (raabitah) in Arabic propositions and explain why and how foreign words like 'ast' and 'kaana' were borrowed to serve as copulas in Arabic logic.

Core Idea
The copula is any relation indicator, not just a word.

This lesson explores the broad definition of the copula (raabitah) in Arabic logic. You will learn that it includes not only the familiar words 'huwa' and 'kaana', but also inflectional vowels like the kasra on the predicate, and even borrowed Persian and Greek terms. The passage also reveals a fascinating historical moment: when philosophers translated Greek logic, they found Arabic lacking a dedicated copula word and adapted pronouns and verbs to fill the gap. By the end, you'll be able to spot the copula in any Arabic proposition and understand its linguistic roots.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition
Find the sentence where the author states what qualifies as a copula. He says: 'كل ما يدل على الربط فهو رابطة' (everything that indicates connection is a copula). Write this down as the general rule.
2
List the specific examples given
The author gives three explicit examples: the kasra vowel in 'Zayd Dabir', the word 'Aast' in 'Zayd qaa'im Aast', and the words 'huwa' and 'kaana' as mentioned in the first sentence. From the commentary, also note 'hasht' (Greek) and 'bood' (Persian for 'kaana').
The examples show that the copula can be a vowel, a pronoun, a verb, or a foreign word.
3
Analyze the commentary expansions
Al-Dasuqi clarifies each example: the kasra is non-temporal, 'hasht' is Greek for 'huwa', and 'Aast' is Persian for 'huwa'. Al-'Attar adds that 'ast' with fatha on the hamza is Persian. Notice how the commentators explain the origin and usage of each term.
The commentary reveals that the copula is not limited to Arabic; it includes borrowings.
4
Understand the historical borrowing rationale
The passage explains that when philosophers translated Greek wisdom, they found Arabic had no dedicated word for the relation. They borrowed 'huwa' (from Persian 'hast' and Greek 'aastin') for non-temporal relation and 'kaana' (from Persian 'bood') for temporal relation. Why 'huwa'? Because it is vague and pronoun-like, matching the abstractness of the relation.
This shows that logical terminology can be adapted from other languages when needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
واعلَمْ أَنَّ الرَّابطة لا تنحصر في لفظة "هو" و"كان"، بل كلُّ ما يدلُّ على الربط فهو رابطة كحركة الكسر في نحو: "زيد دبير"، و"أست" في نحو: "زيد قائم أست"، وغير ذلك مما يدلّ على الربط.
قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : كَحَرَكَةِ الْكَسْرِ) من إضافة العام للخاص، فهي للبيان؛ أي: كسرةُ الرَّاء في دبير في المثال الآتي. (قَوْلُهُ : زَيْدٌ دِبير ) أي : كاتب - وهو بكسر الراء ـ والحركة غير زمانية . (قَوْلُهُ: وَهَشت) عطف على حركة الكشر - وهو بفتح الهاء وسكون السين آخره تاء مثناة - لفظ يوناني معناه : هو . (قَوْلُهُ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ أست) أي : هو، وكان الأنسب أن يجعلَها مُتوسّطةً بينهما . (قَوْلُهُ : وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِمَّا يَدُلُّ عَلَى الرَّبْطِ أي : مثلُ بَود - بفتح الباء الموحدة معناه باليونانية، كان ومثل أستين، ومعناه باليونانية : هو . واعلم أنَّ لفظ كان وهو وغيرهما ؛ لا دلالة لها على النسبة فى اللغة العربيَّةِ، وَلَا مُستعملة فيها ، فَلَمْ يُوضع للنّسبةِ لفظ يدلّ عليها في تلك اللغة، فإذا سمعْتَ: (زيد قائمٌ) فَهِمْتَ ثبوت القيام لزيد، فإن أتيتَ بِكانَ أو هو، كانَ ذلكَ غيرَ مُفيد شيئاً.
قال العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : لَا تَنْحَصِرُ ... إلخ) وَلِذلكَ عبَّرَ بالجزئيَّةِ بقوله : (قد تكون ... إلخ) المفيدة لعدم الحصر . (قَوْلُهُ : زَيْدٌ دِبِيرِ) بكسرِ الرَّاءِ بمعنى كاتب، فحركةُ الرَّاءِ رابطة . (قَوْلُهُ : وَأَسْت) بفتح الهمزة: بمعنى «هو» في لغةِ الفُرس، ومثله: أستين، في لغة اليونان.
ثم قال الدسوقي: ثُمَّ إِنَّ الحكماءَ لَمَّا نقلوا الحكمةَ مِنَ اللُّغةِ اليونانيَّةِ لِلُّغةِ العربيَّةِ؛ وجدوا بإزاء كلِّ جزء من أجزاء القضيَّةِ لفظاً مستقلاً دالا عليهِ دونَ النّسبة، فقد وجدوا الحركاتِ الإعرابيَّةَ دالَّةٌ عليها ؛ فاستعارُوا كلمةَ «هو» بإزاء النِّسبة بدلاً عن : هست وأستين، واستعارُوا كانَ ) بدلاً عن بود ، وإِنَّما اختارُوا «هو» ؛ لأنَّها من المبهمات، والكنايات، والنسبة تشاركهما في الإبهام والخفاء، وبعبارة «هست» بالفارسية، و«أستين» باليونانية، وهي التي تدلُّ على ربط المحمول الاسم بالموضوع ربطاً غير زماني . وَلَمَّا لم يجدوا في العربيَّة في أوَّلِ وضعها لفظاً يقومُ مَقامَ ذلك بخلافِ الرَّبط الزَّماني، فإنَّ الكلمة الوجوديَّةً مثل كان، ويكون، وسيكون»، تدلُّ على ذلكَ الرَّبطِ في لغة العرب؛ اختار بعضُهُم لفظ «هو».
Chapter: On the Definition and Its Divisions. Know that the copula is not confined to the word 'huwa' and 'kaana', but everything that indicates connection is a copula, like the kasra movement in 'Zayd Dabir', and 'Aast' in 'Zayd standing Aast', and other things that indicate connection. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement: 'like the kasra movement' is a general-to-specific addition, so it is for explanation; i.e., the kasra of the ra' in Dabir in the following example. His statement: 'Zayd Dabir' i.e., writer - with a kasra on the ra' - and the movement is non-temporal. His statement: 'wa hasht' is coordinate to 'movement of kasra' - it is with fatha on the ha' and sukun on the seen, ending with a dotted ta' - a Greek word meaning 'huwa'. His statement: 'Zayd standing Aast' i.e., 'huwa', and it would have been more appropriate to place it between them. His statement: 'and other things that indicate connection' i.e., like 'Bood' - with fatha on the single ba' - meaning in Greek 'kaana', and like 'Aastin', meaning in Greek 'huwa'. And know that the word 'kaana' and 'huwa' and others have no indication of relation in the Arabic language, nor are they used in it. So no word was put for relation in that language. So if you hear: 'Zayd standing', you understand the establishment of standing for Zayd. If you bring 'kaana' or 'huwa', that does not benefit anything. Al-'Attar said: His statement: 'is not confined... etc.' and thus he expressed with partiality by saying: 'may be... etc.' which indicates non-confinement. His statement: 'Zayd Dabir' with kasra on the ra' meaning writer, so the kasra of the ra' is a copula. His statement: 'wa ast' with fatha on the hamza: meaning 'huwa' in the Persian language, and similarly 'Aastin' in the Greek language. Then al-Dasuqi said: Then when the philosophers transmitted wisdom from the Greek language to the Arabic language, they found for each part of the proposition an independent word indicating it without the relation. They found that the inflectional movements indicate it. So they borrowed the word 'huwa' for the relation instead of 'hast' and 'aastin', and borrowed 'kaana' instead of 'bood'. They chose 'huwa' because it is from ambiguous words and pronouns, and the relation shares with them ambiguity and hiddenness. And with the expression 'hast' in Persian, and 'aastin' in Greek, which indicate the connection of the predicate noun to the subject as a non-temporal connection. And when they did not find in Arabic at its first coinage a word that stands in place of that, unlike the temporal connection, for the existential word like 'kaana', 'yakoon', 'sayakoon' indicates that connection in the language of the Arabs; some of them chose the word 'huwa'.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the copula (raabitah) in Arabic logic. The author begins by stating that the copula is not restricted to the familiar words 'huwa' (he) and 'kaana' (was); rather, any indicator that links the subject and predicate qualifies as a copula. Examples include the kasra vowel in 'Zayd Dabir' (Zayd is a writer) and the word 'Aast' in the phrase 'Zayd qaa'im Aast' (Zayd is standing). This broad definition shows that the copula can be expressed through inflectional endings or borrowed foreign words. Al-Dasuqi's commentary expands on each example: he clarifies that the kasra on the ra' of 'Dabir' serves as a non-temporal connector, and that 'hasht' and 'Aast' are Greek and Persian equivalents of 'huwa'. Importantly, Dasuqi notes that Arabic originally had no dedicated word for the copula; the relation is understood inflectionally. When philosophers later needed a copula, they borrowed 'huwa' and 'kaana' from Persian and Greek, choosing 'huwa' for non-temporal connection because it is vague and pronoun-like, resembling the abstract nature of the relation. Al-'Attar adds that the use of 'huwa' is partial because the copula is not limited to it—the examples show various forms. This passage teaches that the copula is a functional concept, not a fixed word, and that Arabic logic accommodated foreign borrowings to express logical relations clearly.

Key Terms
رابطة
هو
كان
حركة الكسر
أست

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT considered a copula (raabitah)?
A) The kasra vowel in 'Zayd Dabir'
B) The Persian word 'Aast'
C) The subject noun 'Zayd'
D) The verb 'kaana'

Key Takeaway
The copula is any indicator of relation, not limited to the words 'huwa' and 'kaana'; it includes inflectional vowels and borrowed foreign terms, reflecting the adaptability of Arabic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the copula form in the Arabic proposition 'Zaydun katibun' (Zayd is a writer). State what serves as the copula and why.
?
Given the Persian phrase 'Zard ast' (It is yellow), what is the copula? How would a logician translate this into Arabic using the concepts from this lesson?

Propositions

Defining the Conditional Proposition: Conjunctive and Disjunctive Types
Author defines conditional proposition and divides it into conjunctive and disjunctive types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between categorical and conditional propositions and identify conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals.

Core Idea
Conditionals judge relations, not simple subjects; their two types are conjunctive and disjunctive.

In logic, propositions are divided into categorical (simple affirmation/negation) and conditional (judging relations). This passage defines the conditional proposition and splits it into conjunctive (if...then) and disjunctive (either...or) types, with examples from al-Dasuqi and clarifications from al-'Attar. Understanding this division is foundational for analyzing compound arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Contrast categorical vs. conditional
The author defines categorical as judging affirmation/negation of something for something. Conditional judges 'otherwise'. Identify the key difference: categorical predicates simple subjects, while conditional involves relations between propositions.
2
Identify the two types of conditionals
Conjunctive (muttasil) involves affirming/negating one relation on condition of another, e.g., 'If sun rising, then day exists'. Disjunctive (munfasil) involves incompatibility or non-incompatibility of two relations, e.g., 'Number is either even or odd'.
Al-Dasuqi gives examples for each.
3
Apply the definitions to examples
Given a proposition, classify it: categorical if simple subject-predicate; conjunctive conditional if it contains 'if...then'; disjunctive conditional if it contains 'either...or' or a judgment of incompatibility/non-incompatibility.
Use al-'Attar's examples: 'I saw either Zayd or Amr' is conditional even without explicit logical connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا فشرطية، أي: فالقضية شرطية. فالحملية: هي التي حكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء، أو بنفي شيء عن شيء، والشرطية: هي التي حكم فيها بغير ذلك، كما سيجيء من أن الشرطية هي التي حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة أو بنفيها، على تقدير نسبة أخرى إن كانت متصلة، وبتنافي نسبتين أو لا تنافيهما إن كانت منفصلة.
قال الدسوقي: (قوله : فشرطية) لوجود الشرط فيها. (قوله : بثبوت نسبة نحو: إن كانت الشمس طالعة؛ فالنهار موجود. (قوله: أو بنفيها نحو ليس إن كانت الشمس طالعة؛ فالليل موجود. (قوله : وبتنافي نسبتين) نحو: العدد إما زوج أو فرد، وقوله: (أو لا تنافيهما)؛ نحو: ليس زيد إما أن يكون عالماً أو عابداً، فهذا حكم فيه بسلب التنافي.
قال العطار: (قوله : بغير ذلك) هذا صريح في أن الشرطية يكون الحكم فيها بغير الاتصال والانفصال؛ نحو: رأيت إما زيداً وإما عمراً، والعالم إما أن يعبد الله وإما أن ينفع الناس، فالبيان بقوله كما سيجيء من أن ... إلخ؛ أخص من المبين.
And if not, then conditional, i.e., the proposition is conditional. The categorical: it is that in which judgment is made by affirming something for something, or negating something from something. And the conditional: it is that in which judgment is made by other than that, as will come: the conditional is that in which judgment is made by affirmation of a relation or its negation, on the condition of another relation if it is conjunctive, and by incompatibility of two relations or their non-incompatibility if it is disjunctive. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement: 'then conditional' because of the presence of the condition in it. His statement: 'by affirmation of a relation' like: if the sun is rising, then the day exists. His statement: 'or its negation' like: it is not the case that if the sun is rising, then the night exists. His statement: 'and by incompatibility of two relations' like: the number is either even or odd, and his statement: 'or non-incompatibility of them' like: it is not the case that Zayd is either knowledgeable or worshipful, so this is a judgment with negation of incompatibility. Al-'Attar said: His statement: 'by other than that' is explicit in that the conditional has judgment other than connection and disconnection; like: I saw either Zayd or Amr, and the scholar either worships Allah or benefits people. So the clarification by his statement 'as will come from that... etc.' is more specific than the explained.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition (qadiyya shartiyya) by contrasting it with the categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). The author explains: if the judgment in a proposition is not a simple affirmation or negation of a predicate for a subject (as in categoricals), then it is conditional. A conditional proposition judges with the affirmation or negation of one relation on the condition of another (conjunctive conditional, muttasil), or with the incompatibility or non-incompatibility of two relations (disjunctive conditional, munfasil). Al-Dasuqi provides examples: for conjunctive, 'If the sun is rising, then day exists' (affirmation) and 'Not: if the sun is rising, then night exists' (negation). For disjunctive, 'The number is either even or odd' (incompatibility) and 'It is not the case that Zayd is either knowledgeable or worshipful' (non-incompatibility). Al-'Attar further clarifies that the definition 'by other than that' includes cases where the judgment is not about connection or disconnection per se, giving examples like 'I saw either Zayd or Amr', which is a conditional without explicit logical connection. This shows that the conditional category is broader than just logical conditionals. The passage thus delineates the essential division of propositions into categorical and conditional, and within conditional, into conjunctive and disjunctive, with examples from everyday language and logical discourse.

Key Terms
قضية شرطية
قضية حملية
متصل
منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Dasuqi in the passage, which of the following is an example of a disjunctive conditional (munfasil)?
A) If the sun is rising, then day exists.
B) The number is either even or odd.
C) Not: if the sun is rising, then night exists.
D) Zayd is standing.

Key Takeaway
The conditional proposition is defined by its judgment on relations under a condition (conjunctive) or on incompatibility/non-incompatibility (disjunctive).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example of a conjunctive conditional in the Qur'an (e.g., 4:35 'If you fear a breach...') and explain why it fits the definition from the passage.
?
A jurist argues: 'Either the sale is valid or it is void; it is not void, so it is valid.' Identify the logical type (disjunctive conditional) and state whether the judgment is based on incompatibility or non-incompatibility.
